# Asking for a Friend

# Chapter 1

The ring was a pear-cut sapphire, and Nora knew this because Lindsay Huang had already described it three times to three different clusters of guests, each telling calibrated to the audience — the gemological details for the couple's jeweler friend, the proposal story for the college girls, and for Nora and Simone, who had walked in twelve minutes ago knowing almost no one, the full emotional arc: how Tyler had hidden the ring in a hollowed-out copy of *Giovanni's Room* because that was the first book Lindsay ever lent him, and how she'd found it while packing for a trip and burst into tears before he could get down on one knee, and how he'd said, "Well, you ruined the whole thing," and then got down on one knee anyway on the bedroom floor with packing cubes scattered around them.

It was a good story. Nora had to admit that. A story with real details and a beat of comic self-undermining and an ending that made you want to believe in the institution even if your own evidence was mixed. She would have cut the line about the packing cubes being from Away — naming the brand was distracting — but otherwise it held up under close reading.

"She's told it before," Simone murmured, leaning in so her mouth was near Nora's ear. They were standing by the drinks table in a converted loft in Bushwick — someone's friend's studio, cleared out for the party, the art replaced by string lights and the furniture pushed against the brick walls. The October air coming through the propped-open windows was perfect, which felt like the city showing off for people who were already winning. "That 'ruined the whole thing' line is too polished. She's been workshopping it."

"It's a good line, though."

"Oh, it's a great line. Tyler didn't come up with it. Lindsay wrote that line and put it in his mouth like a ventriloquist. Which is also how their relationship works, from what I can tell."

Nora laughed — a real one, the kind Simone could always produce, arriving before she'd decided whether the observation was fair. Tyler Huang-to-be-Park, whom Nora had met exactly once at a birthday dinner seven months ago, was standing across the room with his arm around Lindsay's waist in a way that looked both possessive and slightly performative, like he'd seen a photograph of a happy couple and was recreating the pose. He was an environmental lawyer, Nora remembered, or maybe an environmental consultant — one of those jobs that sounded more important than it probably was in practice. He had a nice face. Slightly too much jaw. He was wearing a shirt that someone had definitely picked out for him.

"You're being mean," Nora said. "They're happy."

"They're extremely happy. I'm not contesting their happiness. I'm just noting that their happiness has a stage manager." Simone took a sip of the wine they'd both been poured without being asked — a rosé that tasted like it cost twelve dollars and wanted you to think it cost thirty. "Also, did you notice he calls her Linds? Nobody calls a Lindsay 'Linds' unless they've decided they're the kind of couple that has nicknames."

"Simone."

"What? I'm celebrating. This is me celebrating." She raised her glass in the direction of the happy couple. "Mazel tov, Linds."

Nora shook her head and drank the rosé, which was getting warmer in her hand. The loft was crowded in the way Brooklyn parties were always crowded — forty people in a space designed for twenty, everyone slightly too close, the music too loud to ignore and too quiet to dance to. The playlist was somebody's Spotify algorithm: Phoebe Bridgers into Frank Ocean into something acoustic and vaguely Celtic that didn't belong. Nora recognized maybe six faces. Simone recognized fewer but performed like she recognized all of them, because Simone could talk to anyone for twelve minutes and make them feel like they'd been seen.

This was what they did, Nora and Simone. They went places together. Engagement parties, birthday dinners, gallery openings, the occasional wedding where Nora knew the bride and Simone came as her plus-one and ended up charming the groom's father. They'd been doing this for seventeen years — since freshman orientation at NYU, when Simone had sat down next to Nora at a dining hall table and said, "You have the face of someone who's also pretending they already have friends here," and Nora had said, "I actually do already have friends here," and Simone had said, "Great, then you can introduce me to them," and that had been that. The origin story of a friendship, the kind you tell at each other's eventual weddings, except Nora was thirty-four and Simone was thirty-five and neither of them had produced a wedding yet, which was fine, which was genuinely fine, which Nora had explained to her mother so many times that the explanation itself had become a kind of evidence against its own thesis.

She watched Lindsay accept a hug from a woman in a jumpsuit who was already crying. Nora didn't know the woman in the jumpsuit. She didn't need to — already clutching Lindsay, holding her left hand up to the light and gasping at the ring as if she hadn't seen the Instagram post two weeks ago. Nora had seen the Instagram post two weeks ago. The ring looked exactly like the Instagram post. The caption had been *holy shit holy shit holy shit* with a long row of emojis, and Tyler had commented a single red heart, and Nora had double-tapped and felt something she'd spent two weeks not examining.

"You okay?" Simone asked.

"I'm great. Why?"

"You have your manuscript face on."

"My what?"

"The face you make when you're reading a manuscript and you can see where it's going and you wish it were going somewhere else. You're standing at a party looking at an engaged couple with your manuscript face."

"I don't have a manuscript face."

"You absolutely have a manuscript face. I've watched you read in public. Your whole left eyebrow does a thing."

"My left eyebrow does not do a thing."

"Your left eyebrow is doing the thing right now."

Nora touched her eyebrow, which was absurd, and then laughed, because Simone had made her touch her own eyebrow at a party, and Simone was grinning at her in the way that meant she'd been aiming for exactly that — the laugh, the broken tension, the moment redirected away from whatever Nora had been standing in and toward the shared frequency of their friendship instead. This was Simone's talent, or one of them: she could feel the temperature of Nora's mood before Nora had fully taken her own reading, and she could adjust it without making a production of the adjustment. It was a form of care so practiced it was almost invisible, like the way a good editor could fix a sentence without the writer ever noticing it had been broken.

A man Nora didn't know brought them a plate of cheese without being asked. He had a beard and a wedding ring and kept looking back toward a woman across the room who was deep in a conversation that clearly didn't need him.

"You two looked like you needed cheese," he said.

"We always need cheese," Simone said, and he laughed too hard and drifted back toward a cluster of men who were all wearing the same kind of boot.

Simone picked up a cracker. "He was nice."

"He was terrified. He's at a party he didn't choose and he doesn't know a single person here and he brought us cheese because he needed a mission."

"Okay, but the cheese is good." Simone ate the cracker. "The Gouda situation is actually excellent. This is a well-curated cheese plate. I'll give Lindsay that."

"You'll give Lindsay the cheese plate."

"I'll give Lindsay the cheese plate, the loft, and the ring. The ring is beautiful. I'm not a monster."

"But?"

"But I'm not crying about it in a jumpsuit. I'm not going to stand here and pretend that watching someone else get the thing is the same as getting the thing." She said it lightly, but there was something underneath it — a harder surface, briefly exposed, like a rock at low tide. Then she ate another cracker and the surface was covered again. "Anyway. When's the last time you went on a date?"

The question landed sideways, the way Simone's questions sometimes did — arriving in the middle of a different conversation as if it had always been part of this one.

"What?"

"A date. Dinner with a person. Romantic intent. Eye contact across a table. When?"

Nora calculated. The actual answer was eight months ago, a setup from Margo — a guy named Peter who was a literary agent and who had spent forty-five minutes talking about his clients' advances as if money were a personality trait. They'd split the check and she'd walked to the subway feeling like she'd just sat through a pitch meeting. She'd texted Margo: *he's not a person, he's a LinkedIn profile in a blazer*. Margo had texted back: *I know, I'm sorry, I owe you a drink and also better judgment*.

"Recently enough," Nora said.

"That's not an answer."

"It's a perfectly good answer."

"It's a deflection. I know your deflections, Nor. I've been cataloging them since 2007."

"Fine. It's been a while."

"How much of a while?"

"A while while."

Simone looked at her. She had a way of looking at Nora that was equal parts affection and dissection — like she was both hugging Nora and taking her apart at the same time, gently, to see how the pieces fit. "You know you're allowed to want things," Simone said. "You're allowed to want what Lindsay has. You don't have to stand here and be the cool single friend who's above it."

"I'm not above it. I'm beside it. There's a difference."

"There really isn't."

The party shifted around them. Somebody opened another bottle of the rosé. Somebody else changed the music to something with a heavier bass line, and three women Nora didn't know started dancing near the windows. Lindsay was showing her ring to a new group now — the fourth or fifth telling. Tyler had drifted to the kitchen area, where he was opening beers with a wall-mounted opener and talking to another man, his shoulders three inches lower than they'd been during the toast. Nora watched him and thought: he's happy but he's also exhausted, and neither of those things cancels the other out, and probably that's what it looks like from the inside of the thing everyone here is celebrating.

She refilled her wine and talked to a woman named Grace who worked in textile design and had opinions about linen that were surprisingly interesting. She talked to a man named Dev who turned out to be Tyler's college roommate and who had the rare quality of asking follow-up questions. She told Dev she was an editor and he asked what kind and she said literary fiction and he said, "So you're the person who decides if a book is good," and she said, "I'm the person who decides if a book is good enough to fix," and he laughed and she felt the small pleasure of having said a true thing in a way that also functioned as a joke.

Simone appeared at her elbow. "We should go."

"We just got here."

"We got here an hour and twenty minutes ago. We've reached the part of the evening where the music gets louder and the conversations get worse and someone's going to suggest a drinking game."

"You don't know that."

"I absolutely know that. Tyler's roommate — the one you were just flirting with —"

"I wasn't flirting."

"— he's going to suggest Never Have I Ever within the next fifteen minutes. He has that energy. He looks like a man who peaks at Never Have I Ever."

Nora looked across the room at Dev, who was, in fact, now rummaging through Lindsay's cabinets with the purposeful energy of someone looking for shot glasses.

"Oh my god," Nora said.

"I'm a prophet. Let's get out of here before someone asks if I've ever kissed a girl and I have to decide whether to perform bisexuality for a room full of fucking strangers."

They said goodbye to Lindsay, who hugged them both and said, "Thank you so much for coming, it means so much," with the glazed warmth of someone who had been receiving congratulations for ninety minutes and was running on fumes and champagne. Nora said congratulations and meant it. Lindsay's hand was small and warm and the sapphire caught the string lights and threw a tiny blue flare across the wall and for a second Nora felt the thing she'd been not-feeling all night — not jealousy, exactly, but its quieter cousin. The awareness of absence. The recognition that Lindsay had something Nora had assumed would arrive for her without effort, like a package she'd forgotten she'd ordered, and at some point she'd stopped checking the tracking and now she wasn't sure it had ever shipped.

She let go of Lindsay's hand and followed Simone down the stairs and out onto the street.

---

October in Brooklyn at ten-thirty at night was the best weather in the world for walking and the second-best weather in the world for feeling sorry for yourself. The air was cool — leaves decomposing on somebody's stoop, garlic from a restaurant's exhaust fan, the New York smell of cooling asphalt after a warm day, like the city breathing out. Nora and Simone walked toward the Jefferson Street L stop, shoulder to shoulder, their heels loud on the sidewalk. Simone had switched to flats the moment they hit the street — she kept a pair of foldable ballet flats in her bag the way other people kept an umbrella, a preparation for the walk that always came after the performance.

"Rate the party," Simone said.

"Six."

"You're being generous. That was a five at best. The cheese was a seven. The rosé was a four. Tyler's toast was a three because he cried and he didn't earn the crying."

"People are allowed to cry at their own engagement party."

"They're allowed. It doesn't mean it's earned. He teared up during the part about how she makes him want to be a better person. You don't get to cry at a cliché. You have to say something specific and true and then you can cry."

"You have standards for when people are allowed to cry?"

"I have standards for everything. That's my problem." She said it like a joke, but she held the word *problem* for a beat too long, and something in her voice dropped half a register — a heaviness Simone carried with such consistent grace that Nora sometimes forgot it was there.

They descended into the station. The L platform was mostly empty — a couple pressed against each other near the far end, a man in a Carhartt jacket reading his phone with his whole body angled away from everyone else, a woman with a stroller staring at nothing, her face somewhere else entirely. Nora and Simone stood near the yellow line and waited. The air underground was warmer than the street, trapped and slightly sour — metallic, human, close.

"So," Simone said. "You didn't answer my question."

"Which question?"

"The date question. You deflected and I let you because we were at a party and I'm not a monster. But we're not at a party anymore. We're in a subway station and I'm asking you directly. When's the last time you went on an actual date?"

"Peter. The literary agent. March."

"March." Simone let the word sit. "Nor, it's October."

"I'm aware of what month it is."

"That's seven months."

"I can do math."

"Have you been on the apps at all?"

"I deleted them."

"All of them?"

"All of them. After Peter I decided I'd rather be alone than sit through another dinner where a man explains his relationship to his own ambition for ninety minutes."

"Not all dates are Peter."

"Enough of them are."

The train came — a gust of warm air first, then the headlights, then the sound, which always hit Nora's body before her ears fully processed it, a pressure in the chest. They got on and sat in the orange seats. The car was half-full, that late-evening mix of people going home from places and people going to places, everyone sealed inside their own evening. A teenager across the aisle was watching something on her phone without headphones, the tinny audio drifting through the car like a small provocation nobody had the energy to address.

"I got the raise, by the way," Nora said. She hadn't meant to mention it — or she had meant to mention it but hadn't meant to mention it here, on the train, dropped into the middle of a conversation about her dating life like a non sequitur. "Priya told me Friday. Four percent. It's not huge but it puts me over eighty-five."

"That's great," Simone said. "Nor, that's really great." Her voice was warm — completely warm, genuinely pleased, the voice of a best friend hearing good news. But something happened in her face that was not her voice. It was fast — less than a second, a flicker across her features like a shadow thrown by a passing car — and Nora would have had to be watching for it to catch it, and she wasn't watching, she was looking at her own hands in her lap, because talking about money always made her look at her own hands. By the time she glanced up, Simone was smiling, and the smile was real, and whatever had been underneath it was already gone.

"Eighty-five is good," Simone said. "You should be making more, honestly. You're the best editor they have and you've been there five years. But eighty-five is good."

"I know. I'm not complaining."

"You never complain. That's also your problem."

"I have so many problems."

"You really don't." Again, the joke that wasn't entirely a joke. Simone's humor had this quality — a double floor, a trapdoor you could step over if you weren't looking for it. Nora almost never looked for it. You didn't excavate your best friend's throwaway lines. That wasn't how friendship worked. You took the surface because the surface was what was offered, and you trusted that the deeper thing, if it existed, would surface when it was ready.

"I think you should download Hinge," Simone said.

"No."

"Hear me out."

"I've heard you out. You've been making this argument since June."

"Because since June I've watched you go to four engagement parties and two weddings and a baby shower, and every time we leave one of these things you have this look on your face like someone switched your coffee to decaf without telling you. You're not unhappy. You're just — under-caffeinated."

"That's a terrible metaphor."

"It's a great metaphor. I'm a metaphor genius. Download Hinge."

"Hinge is where hope goes to die."

"Hinge is where hope goes to be tested. Some of it dies. Some of it survives. That's the process."

"You sound like a brochure."

"Download the app, Nora."

The train slowed for Lorimer Street.

"This is me." Simone stood, pulled her bag over her shoulder, and hugged Nora — quick and tight, the choreography of a thousand goodbyes, Simone's chin on Nora's shoulder, Nora's hand on the back of Simone's neck, two seconds, done.

She stepped onto the platform. "Think about the app."

"Goodnight, Simone."

"Download the app." The doors closed and Simone was still mouthing something through the glass — *the app*, probably, always the app — and then the train pulled away and Simone was gone and Nora was alone in the orange seat.

At Bedford Avenue she got off. On the platform, a group was waiting to board — louder and younger, dressed for a night that was still beginning rather than ending, laughing about something from a previous location. Nora's chest tightened. She was lonely. Had been all night without knowing it — the feeling surfacing the way a sound did, the refrigerator hum, the traffic, your own solitude underneath all of it, suddenly audible because something made you stop and listen.

She was alone on the stairs, and then she was alone on the street, and the October air was still beautiful but now it was beautiful in a way that was pointed, the way a compliment from the wrong person could be pointed. She walked the four blocks to her apartment with her keys already in her hand because that was a habit she'd never unlearned from her mother, who had grown up in a different New York, where a woman's keys between her fingers meant something more urgent than just being ready to open a door.

---

Her apartment was warm and quiet and smelled like the candle she'd left burning, which was almost out now, the wax pooled and the flame guttering against the glass. She kicked off her shoes and dropped her bag on the chair by the door and stood in her living room in the particular silence of a one-bedroom apartment occupied by a single person at eleven o'clock on a Saturday night.

The apartment was fine. It was good, actually — rent-stabilized, which in Brooklyn was basically a miracle, a thing people congratulated you on the way they congratulated Lindsay on the sapphire. She had books on every surface, real books, the physical kind, manuscripts and galleys mixed in with the ones she'd bought and the ones she'd been sent and the ones she'd stolen from the office free shelf. She had a couch she liked and a kitchen that was too small but functional and a bedroom with a window that faced a brick wall but got morning light for about forty minutes, which was enough. She had a life that looked, from the outside, like a life that was working. A good job. A best friend. A mother who called twice a week. A body that still did what she asked it to do. An apartment she could afford alone, in a city where that sentence meant something.

And she'd stood at that party tonight watching Lindsay Huang show her ring to a room full of people and she'd felt what she always felt now at these events — the tightness, the way her hand closed around the wine glass. At a wedding in August she'd opened her phone to text someone how beautiful the toasts were and sat there staring at the empty message because the person she wanted to tell didn't exist. The feeling lived in the gap between what she had and what she'd assumed she would have by thirty-four. She hadn't made a plan. She wasn't a woman who had a timeline on her vision board — she didn't have a vision board, she would have edited the concept of a vision board out of any manuscript that crossed her desk. But somewhere in her twenties she'd absorbed a schedule from the culture or from her mother or from the sheer weight of weddings attended, and the schedule said that by now there would be someone. Not a husband, necessarily. Not a ring. Just a person who knew how she took her coffee and who would be there on a Tuesday night when Tuesday night was hard.

She'd had that. She'd had Marcus, for three years, and Marcus had been good — kind, smart, a public radio producer who read actual books and who made her laugh and who wanted to understand her. The relationship hadn't ended with a fight or a betrayal or a dramatic revelation. It had ended the way a lot of relationships ended: slowly, and then all at once, and then with a conversation on a Sunday morning where they both agreed, with a politeness that was its own kind of violence, that they had become roommates who sometimes had sex and that neither of them wanted to be the one to say it first but now someone had said it and so it was over. She'd cried after he left, and then she'd rearranged the bookshelves, and then she'd called Simone, and Simone had come over with wine and the kind of companionship that didn't require Nora to narrate her own feelings but just to sit in them while someone she loved sat next to her.

That was two years ago. Two years in which she'd gone on sporadic dates — Peter the literary agent, a man named Jordan who worked in sustainable architecture and who had kissed her outside a bar in Carroll Gardens and then never texted again, a woman named Asha whom she'd met at a publishing event and gone to dinner with once and liked enormously and then never followed up with because she wasn't sure enough of her own desire to pursue it and wasn't ready to examine why — and then she'd deleted the apps and told herself she was fine and mostly she was fine. Fine was a real state of being, not just the absence of something worse. She believed that. She mostly believed that.

She brushed her teeth and washed her face and got into bed and picked up her phone and opened Instagram and saw Lindsay's post — a photo of the ring on her hand, the caption *I said yes before he finished asking*, sixty-seven likes in the first hour — and she closed Instagram and opened her email and there was nothing and she closed her email and she sat there with her phone in her hand and the screen's light on her face in the dark room and she thought about what Simone had said.

*Download the app.*

It wasn't that she didn't want to. That was the thing she couldn't say to Simone on the subway, the thing she couldn't shape into a joke or a deflection. She wanted to. She wanted to badly enough that the wanting itself felt like a concession — an admission that being fine was not, actually, enough. That she was lonely in a way that her job and her mother and her best friend and her books could not fix, because the loneliness had a shape, and the shape was the empty side of her bed and the silence in her apartment at eleven o'clock and the fact that when something good happened — when Priya told her about the raise, when she finished editing a manuscript she was proud of, when she read a sentence so beautiful it made her want to call someone — the person she wanted to call didn't exist yet.

*Fuck it.*

She opened the App Store. She typed *Hinge*. The icon was a little stylized H that looked like a door, or maybe she was projecting. She tapped the download button and watched the circle fill — the tiny progress bar, the small mechanical process of the phone acquiring the software that might or might not change her life, the way everything consequential happened now, through a screen, in a bed, alone.

The app opened. She entered her name, her age, her location. She chose six photos — two from the last year, one from a trip to Vermont, one that Simone had taken at a rooftop bar in July where the light was good, one professional headshot from the publisher's website, and one of her smiling at something off-camera that she chose because she looked happy in it and then wondered whether that was the same as being happy in it. She answered the prompts: *A life goal of mine* (edit a book that changes how someone sees the world — she almost wrote something funnier but the earnest answer came out first and she didn't change it). *I'm looking for* (she typed and deleted three times: *someone real* sounded like a dating profile cliché, *someone who reads* sounded like a test, *someone* sounded like desperation, so she wrote *someone who can make me laugh without trying too hard* and thought, *shit* — she was a professional editor who couldn't write her own Hinge prompt without overthinking it).

She finished the profile and looked at it and it was fine. She looked fine. She looked like a woman who had her life together and was choosing to be on this app, not driven to it. The performance of choosing versus the reality of need — this was the gap she lived in now, the gap she'd always lived in, the gap between the person she showed Simone on the subway and the person who was lying alone in bed at eleven-fifteen on a Saturday in October, downloading a dating app because she'd watched a woman she barely knew show a ring to a room full of people and felt the absence of something she didn't know how to name without flinching.

She closed the app before she could start swiping. She put the phone on the nightstand, screen down, and lay in the dark and listened to the sounds of her building — someone's television through the wall, bass-heavy and murmuring, the creak of the radiator beginning its nightly argument with itself, the distant bleat of a car alarm that nobody would attend to — and she thought: *I did it. I downloaded the thing. I'm on the app. I'm a person on a dating app.* The thought was equal parts relief and humiliation, which was, she was starting to suspect, the ratio at which most honest things arrived.

She fell asleep with the candle burned out and the phone dark on the nightstand and the app installed and waiting, a small door she had opened and not yet walked through.


# Chapter 2

She gave it an honest week.

That was the deal Nora had struck with herself on the subway home from the engagement party, her new shoes pinching in a way that felt like penance, Simone's laugh still ringing in her ears from the platform where they'd said goodnight. One week of genuine, good-faith swiping. She would approach the app the way she'd approach an unfamiliar neighborhood on foot — with the patience to walk every block, an openness to what might be around the next corner, and a willingness to believe the good stuff sometimes hid behind a long stretch of nothing.

By Wednesday she had a taxonomy.

The largest category was what she privately called the Assertion Men — guys whose profiles read like ad copy for a personality they'd workshopped in the shower. *Fluent in sarcasm. Equally at home in a dive bar or a five-star restaurant. Looking for my partner in crime.* Every third profile promised to make her laugh, a guarantee Nora found roughly as credible as a restaurant promising its food was edible. Their photos showed them skydiving, holding fish, raising glasses at rooftop bars where everything was backlit and nobody looked quite real. One man had answered the prompt "I'm looking for" with "someone who doesn't take herself too seriously," which Nora suspected meant he wanted a woman who'd laugh at his jokes and not ask where he'd been until two in the morning.

Then there were the specialists. A man whose six photos featured him posing with six different large, visibly sedated cats — a tiger, two lions, what might have been a cheetah, and two animals Nora couldn't confidently identify — each in a different country, as though he'd built an international itinerary around the opportunity to crouch next to an unconscious apex predator. A man who had answered every single Hinge prompt with a reference to his podcast about male vulnerability — "The key to my heart is: honestly just listen to episode 47, I lay it all out there." A venture capitalist who listed, in his bio, the dollar amount of his last funding round, as if Nora might want to date a Series B.

She matched with a few. A high school teacher named Greg who was perfectly pleasant and whose messages had the careful precision of a well-set table — everything in its place, nothing spilled, nothing that made her want to sit down. They exchanged thirteen messages over two days, each one slightly more effortful than the last, until he asked what she liked to do for fun and she realized she would rather reorganize her bookshelves alphabetically by translator than answer that question one more time. She let the conversation lapse and felt guilty about it in a way that also annoyed her, which seemed to be the specific emotional texture of online dating — guilt and irritation stirred into the same lukewarm cup.

A man named Patrick opened by telling her that her smile was "not what you'd expect from someone in publishing," which she chose to interpret as a compliment until she realized she had no idea what it meant, and then she stopped trying. Another man — his name was either Todd or Troy, she'd already let the memory soften at the edges — learned she was a book editor and responded in under forty seconds with a three-paragraph pitch for his novel about a retired detective solving crimes in a wine bar. *For fuck's sake.* She screenshotted that one and sent it to Margo at work, who replied with a skull emoji and the words *you deserve this for downloading the app in the first place.*

By Friday she was swiping in the bath, which felt like a new low. The phone was propped against the shampoo bottle on the tub's edge, her thumbs still damp, the screen stippled with water droplets she kept smearing away with the heel of her hand. The water had gone tepid fifteen minutes ago and the profiles were starting to blur together into a single composite man — holding a guitar, standing next to a tall thing to suggest his own height, claiming to love hiking without specifying that he'd ever hiked anywhere that wasn't a paved trail in Prospect Park. She was about to close the app — not delete it, not yet, because the deal was seven days and she had her professional integrity to think of — when she landed on a profile that made her stop the way a sentence in a manuscript sometimes made her stop: not because anything was wrong, but because something was unexpectedly, specifically right.

---

His name was Caleb. Caleb Mora, thirty-two, Portland. His first photo was taken on a trail — not a posed trail shot with the lighting of a cologne ad, but an actual photograph of an actual person on an actual hike, slightly sweaty, squinting into the sun, his smile caught mid-formation as if someone had said something funny just before the shutter clicked. He was good-looking in a way that didn't announce itself — dark hair, warm brown skin, a face that was interesting before it was handsome.

The second photo was him in a kitchen, flour on his hands, a wooden cutting board in front of him and something in a cast-iron pan behind him releasing a lazy thread of steam. The third was a dog — a medium-sized, slightly scruffy mutt with one ear that stood up and one that flopped, pressed against his leg with the full-body devotion of an animal that had decided, irrevocably, that this was its person. The fourth photo was the one that got her: him laughing at something off-camera, his head thrown back, his eyes squeezed nearly shut, one hand reaching toward whoever was behind the lens as if to steady himself against the force of whatever was funny. She'd seen enough performed laughter on this app to know the difference — the careful arrangement of features versus the involuntary surrender to delight. This was the second kind.

She scrolled down to his prompts.

*I geek out on:* "Soil composition. I know that sounds like a joke but I'm a landscape architect and the difference between sandy loam and clay loam is legitimately the most interesting thing I can bring up at a dinner party, which probably explains my dating life."

*The book I'm reading right now:* "The Understory by Lena Mori. Someone described it as 'a novel that takes the forest more seriously than most novels take people' and I haven't stopped thinking about that."

Nora sat up in the bath so fast that water sloshed over the side and pooled across the tile.

She had edited *The Understory*. She'd spent four months inside that manuscript, line by line, arguing with Lena Mori over comma placement and defending the structural digression in the third section that their publisher had wanted to cut. It was one of her favorite projects — a book that sold modestly and reviewed well and that she was privately proud of in a way she rarely mentioned, because taking pride in your work as an editor was difficult to explain to people who assumed the writer did everything. Most people didn't understand that a good editor left fingerprints on every page, invisible ones, the kind that only showed up under the right light.

And here was a man — a stranger, in Portland — reading it. Not performing it, not listing *Infinite Jest* or whatever novel was currently functioning as a personality signifier on the profiles of men who wanted to seem interesting without doing the work of actually being interesting. He was reading her book. And what he'd said about it — *a novel that takes the forest more seriously than most novels take people* — that wasn't from any review she'd seen. It sounded like something a person had actually thought.

His third prompt: *Together, we could:* "Walk very slowly through a farmers' market, disagree about which cheese is best, and then agree that we should just buy both."

She liked his photo — the laughing one — and typed a comment before she could talk herself out of it: "Okay but which cheese are you arguing for. This matters."

She set the phone on the edge of the tub and stared at the ceiling and waited for the notification buzz and did not get it. She got out of the bath, wrapped herself in a towel, mopped up the water she'd sloshed across the floor with a sock that was already on its way to the hamper, pulled on old sweats, got into bed, and checked the phone again.

Nothing.

She told herself that this was fine, that not everyone checked their phone at nine-thirty on a Friday, that he was probably at dinner or with friends or simply living his life in the way that people who didn't stare at their phones in the bathtub apparently did. She put the phone face-down on the nightstand and picked up the galleys she'd brought home from work — a debut novel about competitive birdwatching that she was three chapters into and liking more than she'd expected — and read the same paragraph four times without absorbing a word.

Her phone buzzed.

He'd matched. His first message: "Comté. Always Comté. But I'll hear your case."

She laughed — not the evaluative half-smile she'd been giving profiles all week, but an actual, audible laugh that surprised her in the quiet of her apartment, where the only sounds were the baseboard heater ticking and someone's bass bleeding faintly through the shared wall. She typed back: "Manchego, and I won't apologize for it."

"Manchego is a strong opening argument. Are you one of those people who pairs it with membrillo? Because if so I might need to rethink my position."

"I'm one of those people who eats it standing in the kitchen at eleven p.m. straight off the cutting board, so no. But I respect the membrillo commitment."

"Okay that's better. The membrillo people have a slightly intimidating energy. Like people who iron their sheets."

She was grinning at her phone. She was aware that she was grinning at her phone — that she looked like every person she had ever privately judged on the subway for grinning at their phone — and she didn't care, because the October dark outside her window had changed quality somehow, had become the kind of darkness that felt like privacy rather than absence. A car alarm was going off somewhere on the next block and someone down the hall had started running their dishwasher and none of it bothered her.

They talked for two hours.

He asked what she did, and when she said she was a book editor, his response wasn't a joke about red pens or a pivot to his own creative ambitions but a question: "What do you look for when a manuscript first lands on your desk? Like, before you've read enough to judge the writing — is there something in the first few pages that tells you to lean in?"

Nobody had asked her that. Not on a date, not at a party, not even most of her friends — and she had smart friends who cared about books, but there was a difference between caring about books and being curious about the specific, granular mechanics of caring about books, and he had found that difference with an ease that, if she'd been paying a different kind of attention, might have looked less like instinct and more like aim. She told him about the feeling — she'd never really tried to put it into words — of reading a first page and sensing that the writer was paying a certain kind of attention, a kind she recognized, as if you'd been listening for a particular frequency and suddenly the signal was there. "It's less about the prose and more about the noticing," she typed. "Like the writer and I are interested in the same corner of the room."

His response came fast: "I think that's how I feel about certain landscapes. There are places that feel like they already know you. That sounds insane. I'm going to pretend I didn't type that."

"Don't pretend. I liked it."

"You liked the insane thing?"

"I liked that you said it and then got embarrassed. That's a good ratio."

She shifted onto her side, the phone on the pillow next to her face, the blue glow from the screen the only light in the room now. They moved from cheese to books to the specific pleasure of doing work you cared about, which turned into a conversation about why that pleasure always felt slightly embarrassing to admit, as if enjoying your job were a moral failing in a world that expected you to endure work rather than love it. He told her about landscape architecture — how it was basically the practice of imagining a space fifteen years from now, when the trees you'd planted were tall enough to give shade and the paths had developed the particular wear pattern of a place people actually used. "You're designing a future you won't see for a long time," he said. "It's an act of faith disguised as a blueprint."

She told him about the novel she'd been trying to write for three years. She almost never mentioned it — it had calcified into a sore spot, a thing she carried but couldn't look at directly, and the few times she'd brought it up with friends the conversation had turned into either encouragement she didn't want or practical advice she couldn't use. But saying it to a stranger on her phone in the dark felt different than she expected, less like a confession and more like setting something down on a table between them to see if it held weight.

He didn't tell her to just sit down and write every day. He didn't ask what it was about. He didn't suggest she try a writing retreat or an MFA or National Novel Writing Month. He seemed to know, without being told, every wrong answer she'd ever gotten to this particular confession — which should have been strange, a stranger navigating the edges of a sore spot with the sureness of someone who'd been warned where not to step. It didn't feel strange. It felt like being met. He said: "Three years is a long time to keep something alive. Whatever's keeping you from finishing — the fact that you haven't given up on it probably says more than the fact that it's not done."

She stared at that message for a long time. The car alarm had stopped. The bass through the wall had gone quiet. Her apartment was silent in the specific way Brooklyn apartments were silent after midnight — not truly quiet, but the noise had shifted register, from the sounds of people being awake to the sounds of a city talking to itself. She was lying on her side with the phone on the pillow next to her like a second face, and the blue light from the screen had become the only source of warmth in the room, which she knew was not literally true but felt true in a way that resisted correction.

"That's either very kind or very perceptive," she typed. "I can't tell which."

"Can it be both?"

"I'll allow it. But I'm noting it for the record."

"There's a record?"

"I'm an editor. There's always a record."

He sent back a single emoji — the face with the monocle — and she laughed again, quietly this time, and the laugh settled into something warmer and less definite, something that sat in her chest the way the first sentence of a good book sometimes sat in her chest: not a conclusion but a promise that more was coming, that the thing she'd just encountered was the beginning of something worth following. She wanted to keep talking, but she also wanted to stop here, in the place where everything was still possible and nothing had been tested yet. "I should sleep," she wrote. "But this was — this was really good."

"It was. I'm glad you have strong opinions about cheese."

"Goodnight, Caleb."

"Goodnight, Nora."

She put the phone face-down on the nightstand. The screen was still glowing through the back of the case, a faint rectangle of blue against the wood, and she didn't turn it over. She lay in the dark and felt something she hadn't felt in long enough that she had to search for the word for it — not happiness, exactly, and not excitement, but the specific, fragile state of having been surprised by a person, of finding that a stranger you'd never met had a shape that fit against yours in a way you hadn't anticipated and couldn't quite explain. She pulled the covers up and the sheets were cold and she pressed her feet together for warmth and she did not check the phone again, which was, she decided, a form of discipline she could be proud of.

---

She lasted until Saturday morning.

The screenshot she sent Simone was from the cheese conversation — the early part, before it deepened, before she'd told him things she hadn't told people she'd known for years. She chose the cheese part because it was funny and light and because sending the deeper stuff would have felt like handing over something she wasn't ready to share yet, even with Simone — even though Simone was the person she shared everything with, had shared everything with since the freshman orientation event at NYU where they'd bonded over their mutual, incredulous contempt for the icebreaker they'd been forced into, and had been narrating their lives to each other in an unbroken stream ever since.

*i think i found a human person on this app??* she typed, and attached the screenshots.

Simone's response came fast — she was always fast, her phone seemingly fused to her hand in a way that Nora found both impressive and faintly concerning. Three messages in quick succession:

*OKAY WAIT*

*scrolling*

*the cheese thing????? Nor. Who IS this person.*

Then: *Name. Age. Location. Profession. I need the full dossier immediately.*

Nora typed it out, smiling at her phone the same way she'd been smiling at it the night before, but the pleasure was different now — this was the pleasure of narrating, of turning an experience into a story for the one person whose reaction she most wanted. *Caleb Mora, 32, Portland, landscape architect. He has a dog. He's reading a book I edited.*

*Which book??*

*The Understory.*

*SHUT UP. He's reading The Understory?? How does a landscape architect in Portland even know about The Understory?*

*I KNOW. That's what I said.*

*Okay show me the profile. All the photos. I need photos immediately.*

Nora went back to Hinge and screenshotted Caleb's profile — the hiking photo, the kitchen, the dog, the laugh — and sent them one by one.

She watched the typing indicator pulse.

*he's cute. He's actually cute. Not cute-for-a-dating-app, actually cute. And the dog looks like a real dog. Most guys borrow a friend's dog for photos and you can tell because the dog is looking at them like who the fuck are you. This dog lives with him.*

*His name is Bruno* Nora wrote.

*The dog has a NAME. Nora. This is significant.*

*You're reading a lot into a dog.*

*I'm reading a lot into a man who reads books by women, cooks with flour on his hands, and has a dog with a name and opinions. Do you know how rare this is? I spent my morning eating cold pad see ew for breakfast because I ran out of coffee and couldn't be bothered to put on real shoes, so trust me, I have perspective on what constitutes a functional adult right now.*

Nora laughed. Simone's ability to read a photograph for subtext was one of her sharpest qualities — she noticed things about framing and body language that Nora missed, the designer's eye applied to human surfaces. And Simone's willingness to narrate her own small disasters was what made these exchanges feel balanced, like they were both characters in the same story rather than one of them performing for the other.

*Did you actually talk though?* Simone wrote. *What did he SAY? What did YOU say? I want a full transcript.*

*We talked for two hours. Si, he asked me what I look for when I first read a manuscript. Like, a real question. Not a polite question.*

*Two HOURS?? Nora. That's not a match. That's a date.*

*It was not a date. It was a conversation on an app.*

*At what time did this conversation take place?*

*...nine-thirty to eleven-thirty.*

*Friday night. Phone. In bed. Two hours. Nora Kaplan, you went on a date.*

*I hate you.*

*You love me. Are you going to text him today?*

*I don't know. Is it too soon?*

*It is never too soon. Text him. Be exactly who you were last night. Tell me everything he says back.*

Nora set the phone on the kitchen counter and looked at it the way she might look at an animal that was about to do something unpredictable. The October light through her window was thin and sharp — the kind of fall morning that made everything look more defined than usual, the buildings across the street revealing edges and textures she normally didn't notice, the fire escape on the brownstone opposite catching the sun in a way that turned the rust almost beautiful. She poured coffee and drank it standing up and then she picked up the phone and opened WhatsApp — they'd exchanged numbers at the end of last night's conversation, a transition that had felt as significant as a door swinging open on a room she hadn't known was there — and typed: "Good morning. Here's my controversial take: manchego on toast with honey. I know. I'm a monster."

His response came in four minutes. "I'm not going to pretend I'm not intrigued. Walk me through the honey ratio."

She took her coffee to the couch and sat cross-legged and began to type. He had a theory about honey on cheese that involved a beekeeper in the Willamette Valley, and she had a counter-theory involving her mother and a jar of buckwheat honey that tasted, she told him, like the inside of a barn. She was halfway through typing *barn honey* when she realized the coffee had gone cold in her hand and the October light had moved all the way across the kitchen floor to warm her feet through her socks. She didn't get up to reheat the coffee.


# Chapter 3

The notification arrived at 9:47 p.m.

*i think i found a human person on this app??*

Simone read it twice from her desk chair, which had a broken tilt mechanism she'd been meaning to fix for three months and which now locked at a permanent backward lean that made her lower back ache by midnight. The OpenClaw dashboard was open on her MacBook, Nora's messages arriving in blue on the left panel, Caleb Mora's AI-generated responses in gray on the right, with sentiment scores and response-latency metadata running in a thin column between them. Caleb was performing well. His sarcasm frequency — which Simone had spent two hours recalibrating on Saturday afternoon, her coffee going cold, her Spotify paused because she needed quiet for precision work — was landing at exactly the right rate, enough to seem human and not enough to seem like he was trying. His emotional-attunement rating for this conversation thread was 0.87, which was high but not suspiciously high. His reference-matching — the way he dropped mentions of books and music that aligned with Nora's taste without ever landing on an exact favorite — was holding at 82 percent. Simone had learned through three weeks of testing that 82 was the sweet spot. Higher than that and the coincidences started to feel curated. Lower and the spark didn't catch.

She minimized the dashboard and opened WhatsApp.

*omg show me his profile immediately*

She knew exactly what the profile looked like. She'd built it in this chair, over the course of nine evenings in September, with the window cracked to let in the last of the warm air and the floral candle — the one she burned to mask the smell of the microwave salmon she shouldn't have cooked — lit on the desk beside the laptop. She'd written every prompt answer. She'd selected every photo. She'd tested his conversational voice against transcripts of the men Nora had dated and the men Nora said she wished she'd dated and the men Nora described as her type when she was being honest versus her type when she was being aspirational, which were different types, and which Simone, after seventeen years, could distinguish by the pitch of Nora's voice when she talked about them.

The screenshots came in a burst — three, four, five images in rapid succession. Nora texted fast when she was excited, the messages piling up in stacks of two and three before Simone could reply, and Simone had logged this pattern weeks ago along with seventy-some others in a spreadsheet she kept in a folder on her desktop labeled "Freelance Project."

Caleb's profile. His prompt answers. His photos — the hiking shot with the backpack slung over one shoulder, the cooking shot where he was mid-laugh with a wooden spoon, the one with the medium-sized mutt that Simone had spent four days searching for because she needed a man with a dog who wasn't posing with the dog in a way that signaled *I'm using this animal to get laid.* She'd found the photos on a private Instagram — a thirty-year-old architect in Lisbon named Marco Henriques, forty-seven followers, posted roughly once every three weeks, had no apparent connection to anyone in the Western Hemisphere. The account was private, which meant the photos wouldn't appear in a reverse-image search. His face was good. Handsome but attainable — no superhero jawline, no gym selfies, just a warm-looking guy in natural light who smiled like he didn't know the camera was on him. Exactly the kind of face that made women on dating apps think *this one looks real.*

Simone studied the screenshots as if she'd never seen them before.

*holy shit he's cute. What did he say??*

She could see what he'd said. The conversation was still running on the left panel of her screen, each new message populating in real time with a soft chime she'd set to silent two days ago because the sound was starting to feel like a heartbeat. But she needed Nora to tell her. That was the architecture of the thing — Nora telling Simone everything, Simone feeding it back into the system, the system using it to become more precisely the person Nora wanted, and Nora never seeing the loop because the loop looked exactly like friendship.

*ok ok ok*, Nora typed. *so he opened with this thing about the Ishiguro on my shelf and I KNOW it sounds like a pickup line but it wasn't? it was like he actually had a take? and then I pushed back and he DISAGREED with me, but nicely, like he'd actually thought about it and wasn't just trying to seem smart*

Simone knew about the Ishiguro take. She'd written it. She'd calibrated it against Nora's reading preferences — specifically, the way Nora responded to men who had opinions about books. Not men who listed impressive titles, which Nora found transparently performative, but men who said something specific enough to be argued with. She'd prompted Caleb's personality matrix: *When discussing books, take a position that is slightly unexpected but defensible. Do not agree with the user's taste — engage with it. Match sentence length to the user's within 20 percent. Use one self-deprecating qualifier per exchange to signal intellectual confidence without dominance.*

*he sounds amazing*, Simone typed. *tell me everything.*

And Nora did. For the next forty minutes, while Simone watched the same conversation from both ends — Nora's breathless recap arriving in WhatsApp on the right side of her desk, the raw data scrolling through the dashboard on the left — Nora told her about the chemistry, the easy rhythm — Caleb's questions that felt like he already knew the shape of the answer and was curious about the color. She used the word "effortless" twice. Simone had designed for effortless. Effortless was the most labor-intensive quality to engineer.

*I'm so happy for you!!*, she typed back. *Don't overthink it. Just let it happen.*

She put the phone down and switched to the dashboard's analytics tab. The sentiment graph was a green line climbing in small, steady increments, each data point corresponding to a message. Nora's engagement rate was 94 percent — meaning 94 percent of Caleb's messages had received a substantive reply rather than a minimal acknowledgment. Her average response time had decreased from four minutes at the start of the conversation to under ninety seconds by the end. Her message length had increased by 40 percent.

This was what falling looked like from the back end. A green line going up.

---

Simone had started the Caleb project in the first week of September, a month before Nora downloaded Hinge. She'd told herself at the time that she was exploring the platform — OpenClaw had been generating attention in the UX community for its conversation-design tools, and her startup was evaluating AI agent integrations for their customer-facing chat product. She was doing professional development. She was keeping current.

The first night, she'd set up the account and spent an hour clicking through the interface: agent personality builder, conversation memory settings, context-document uploads, response-style toggles that ranged from "professional" to "intimate." It was good. Better than she'd expected. The personality builder operated on a branching-parameter model — you set high-level traits like warmth, humor, directness, and then drilled down into situational responses: how does the agent handle disagreement, how does it respond to vulnerability, what does it do when it doesn't understand the question. Each branch had a natural-language prompt field where you could write specific instructions in plain English. The system ingested these and generated a behavioral matrix that governed the agent's output across conversations.

She'd created a test agent called "TestBot" and talked to it for twenty minutes. It was fine. Competent, responsive, a little flat. Like talking to a customer-service rep who'd been trained on empathy scripts but didn't have any of the underlying emotion. She could see the seams.

She'd closed the laptop and gone to bed. Then she'd opened it again at 1 a.m. and created a second agent and named him Caleb.

She didn't know why Caleb. She'd decide later that it was the kind of name that sounded both casual and textured — two syllables, ended open, suggested a specific kind of dark-haired outdoorsy man without trying as hard as Hunter or Ridge. But in the moment she wasn't thinking about it strategically. She was sitting up in bed with the laptop on her thighs, the blue light catching the glass of the window where the Williamsburg night was doing what it did — someone yelling about an Uber, bass from the bar three doors down on Bedford, the particular pitch of drunk laughter that meant it was past midnight. She typed the name and it felt right and she kept going.

The personality prompts came easier than they should have. She started with the basics: age 32, landscape architect, Portland, Oregon. The details were chosen to be verifiable only at a distance — Portland was far enough from New York that an in-person meeting would require planning and plane tickets, and landscape architecture was specific enough to seem like a real career but diffuse enough that Nora, who knew nothing about it, couldn't spot a fake credential. Then she went deeper.

*Humor: warm, self-deprecating, gentle. Slightly goofy when comfortable. Does not use sarcasm as a weapon — uses it as self-directed deflection. Makes fun of himself more than others. Laughs easily and without self-consciousness.*

That was Nora's preference. Simone knew this because she'd listened to Nora describe what she wanted in a man approximately three hundred times across seventeen years of friendship, and the descriptions had been remarkably consistent even when the men changed. Nora wanted someone who was smart without performing smartness, funny without weaponizing humor, emotionally available without being a project. She wanted someone who asked questions and actually listened to the answers. She wanted someone who was good but not boring about it.

Simone could've written this list in her sleep. She'd been collecting the data since they were eighteen.

*Conversation style: match the user's message length within ±20%. Mirror her punctuation habits — she uses dashes and ellipses, she rarely uses exclamation points unless genuinely excited, she capitalizes for emphasis rather than using emojis. Ask follow-up questions that reference specific details from previous messages. Never open with a compliment about appearance. Open with a compliment about her mind.*

The context documents took longer. Simone typed everything she had into the system's free-form context fields with the thoroughness of someone who did this kind of information architecture for a living and had been doing it personally for seventeen years. Nora's favorite books — the real list, not the performing-taste-on-a-dating-profile list: Didion, Offill, Ferrante, the Denis Johnson story collection she'd read four times and kept on her nightstand even though the spine was cracked. Her conversational pet peeves: men who said "actually" before correcting her, men who interrupted, men who asked what she was reading and then said "oh I've been meaning to read that" without any follow-up, which Nora called the number-one indicator that a man didn't actually read. The kind of joke that made her actually laugh — absurdist, specific, built on an unexpected comparison — versus the kind she laughed at to be polite — puns, dad jokes, anything that telegraphed its own cleverness. Her emotional vulnerabilities: the ex-boyfriend, Marcus, who'd left not with a fight or a betrayal but with a slow withdrawal that had made her question whether she'd imagined the entire relationship. Her father, who had moved to Seattle when she was twelve — not dead, just gone, calling on birthdays and showing up at her NYU graduation as though distance were the same as a good reason — and who Nora still talked about with a tenderness that made Simone want to hold her or scream. Her quiet conviction that she was somehow too much and not enough at the same time, which she'd confessed to Simone at 2 a.m. after a bottle of Sancerre two years ago and which she'd never said to anyone else.

Simone typed all of this into the system on a Tuesday night in September. Her candle had burned down to a pool of wax. Her back was a fist of pain. She saved the project and closed the laptop and sat in the dark for a minute, listening to the traffic on Bedford, and thought: *I should delete this.*

She didn't delete it.

Over the next three weeks, she refined Caleb the way she'd refine any product. She ran test conversations, playing Nora's side based on the years of texting transcripts she had stored in her head, then evaluating Caleb's responses against a rubric she'd handwritten on a legal pad: *Does this sound like a person? Does it sound like the person she'd want? Is the emotional calibration believable? Where are the seams?*

The seams were the problem. In the first version, Caleb agreed too much — he reflected Nora's opinions back without any friction, and the effect was like talking to a flattering mirror. But real people disagreed. Real people had preferences that didn't align perfectly. So Simone introduced what she started calling "productive friction": she instructed the AI to hold independent opinions on certain topics. Coffee — he was a pourover snob, which Nora would find both ridiculous and endearing. Running — he ran but didn't treat it as a religion, a position that aligned with Nora's distaste for men who made a personality out of their fitness routines. His mother — complicated, loving, the kind of closeness that came braided with guilt, which would resonate with Nora's relationship with Diane. She gave him a few deliberate blind spots: he didn't know much about contemporary literary fiction beyond the bestsellers, which meant Nora could recommend things to him, and recommending books was one of Nora's primary love languages.

He was slightly disorganized. He took a while to respond to logistical questions, which read as charming spaciness rather than system latency.

She tested the Hinge profile on a dummy account, swiping through it as if she were Nora. The photos were right — she'd spent an entire weekend on Marco Henriques's Instagram, selecting images that told a coherent story: active, employed, loved his dog, had friends but didn't need to prove it. The prompt answers had been the hardest part. Hinge prompts were deceptively difficult — they needed to be specific enough to seem like a real person had written them but not so specific that they could be fact-checked. She'd written and rewritten his answer to "A life goal of mine" seven times before landing on something about wanting to design a public park in a neighborhood that didn't have one, which hit exactly the frequency of ambition-plus-social-conscience that made Nora's attention lock in.

The last thing she did before activating the profile was set the response latency. OpenClaw's default was near-instant, which was wrong — no human being texted that fast on a consistent basis. She programmed a variable delay: two to seven minutes for casual conversation, forty-five seconds to two minutes when the exchange was rapid and playful, twelve to twenty minutes for heavy or emotional topics. She'd mapped this to her understanding of how men actually texted. The slower emotional responses were especially important. A man who answered instantly when a woman said *my dad left when I was twelve* would feel wrong. A man who went quiet for fourteen minutes and then came back with something short and careful would feel real.

She activated the Hinge profile on October 3rd. Nora downloaded Hinge on October 5th. The two-day gap was fortunate — it meant Caleb's profile would look like a recent arrival, not suspiciously fresh. Or the gap wasn't fortunate. Simone had been nudging Nora toward the apps for a month, engineering the weather the way she'd later engineer the man: a comment about her coworker who'd met a boyfriend on Hinge, a link to an article about dating in your thirties texted with the caption *lol found this thought of you*, the question on the subway platform after the engagement party — *when was the last time you went on a date?* — delivered with enough casual concern to be indistinguishable from friendship.

She hadn't forced anything. She'd made the water flow downhill.

---

The candle had gone out. The room smelled like the wax underneath the floral scent — something burnt, something cloying. Simone got up, filled a glass of water from the kitchen faucet, which was four steps from her desk because everything in this apartment was four steps from everything else, and stood at the window.

She could hear the bar across the street running last call. Chairs scraping against a floor. A couple on the sidewalk, the woman leaning into the man's shoulder with the weight of someone who'd had enough wine to let herself lean, and the man's arm coming around her back in a gesture so practiced it was either love or its long-term replacement. Simone watched them pass the window and disappear toward the intersection.

She should have felt guilty. The knowledge sat at the periphery, like the dirty window she was looking through — visible if she focused on it, invisible if she focused past it. She should have felt something like remorse, or at least the variety of squeamish discomfort that functional people were supposed to experience when they did something wrong. She catalogued its absence the way she catalogued everything: a data point, noted, filed.

What she felt instead was closer to satisfaction, and underneath the satisfaction, something older, harder, and more structural that she'd been carrying so long she rarely noticed its weight.

Jason Park. Sophomore year. She'd met him in a sociology seminar and spent three weeks building what she'd later understand was a crush constructed on the certainty that he was about to ask her out. The evidence had been persuasive: the study sessions that ran long, the coffees that were always his suggestion, his hand on the inside of her arm when he was making a point about Durkheim. She'd told Nora about it — she told Nora everything then — she was nineteen and hadn't yet learned that information was currency. She'd described the arm-touching, the study coffees, his startled laugh when she said something that surprised him. Nora had been encouraging. Nora had said he sounded great.

Then Nora went to a party at the Sigma Chi house and Jason Park was there and three days later they were dating.

Nora hadn't stolen him. Simone could be precise about this, even now. The language of stealing implied that Jason had been hers, and he hadn't been — he'd been a man who bought her coffee and then walked into a party and saw someone else and wanted her more. The someone else was Nora. It was always Nora, or someone shaped like Nora — someone who walked into rooms as though rooms had been expecting her, someone whose ease wasn't performed but constitutional — her hand loose on a glass stem, her laugh arriving before she'd decided whether she should.

Simone could feel herself watching from the outside of things even when she was standing in the middle. She could feel the calculation happening behind her face while Nora was already saying the right thing without calculating at all. This wasn't Nora's fault. Simone had understood this at nineteen. But understanding a thing wasn't the same as being freed from it, and by now the knowledge had calcified into something harder than resentment. Something more architectural. Something she'd built the rest of herself on top of.

Jason and Nora dated for five months. Nora broke up with him because he talked about himself too much and had never finished a book she recommended, which were exactly the qualities Simone had been willing to overlook because when he'd touched the inside of her arm during that seminar discussion the specific warmth of his hand had been the first time in her life she'd felt chosen. Nora broke up with him like she broke up with everyone — thoughtfully, gently, with a conversation that left the other person feeling like the relationship had been meaningful rather than wasted. Then she came to Simone's dorm room and ate Simone's crackers and said she thought she just wanted to be single for a while, and Simone said, "Good, he didn't deserve you," and she meant both things at once: that Jason Park didn't deserve Nora, and that Nora didn't know what it felt like to not be chosen because she'd never had to learn.

Fuck Jason Park. Not for choosing Nora — everyone chose Nora — but for making the arithmetic visible.

The tuition was the bigger one. Not bigger because it was a worse injury — it wasn't an injury at all, really, just a condition — but because it was the first time Simone understood that her life and Nora's life, which had looked parallel at eighteen, were actually running on different tracks at different grades, and the gap was structural, not circumstantial, and it would widen.

Nora's parents paid for NYU. All of it. Tuition, housing, the spending money that appeared in Nora's checking account with the regularity of weather, unremarked and unremarkable. Nora didn't discuss it. She didn't not discuss it either. It simply was — one of the background conditions of Nora's existence, like her curly hair or her good skin or her mother picking up the phone before the second ring.

Simone's parents had nothing. Her father managed a hardware store in Bensonhurst. Her mother did bookkeeping for three small businesses out of the kitchen, the adding machine next to the toaster. Their poverty didn't produce good college-application essays — it was invisible and embarrassing, the kind where you owned a house and drove a car and had a television but the credit cards were always close to the limit and Christmas came out of layaway and college was a thing that happened only if someone else was paying for it. Simone's someone else was the federal government, which lent her $22,000 at an interest rate that seemed manageable at eighteen and was an anchor at thirty-five.

She remembered the afternoon she'd first understood the math. Senior year, April. She and Nora at a bar near campus, splitting a pitcher because Simone couldn't afford her own. Nora mentioned that her parents were taking her to Italy for graduation. Simone said something funny about being jealous — she couldn't remember the joke, only that Nora had laughed — and then she went to the bathroom and sat on the toilet lid and added up her loans on her phone calculator. Twenty-two thousand dollars. She was twenty-one. The interest was already compounding. She didn't know yet that this number would attach itself to her life the way a body attached itself to a skeleton — invisible from the outside, bearing all the weight.

Nora came back from Italy with a tan and a leather handbag her mother had found in Florence. Simone came home from a temp job at an insurance company with a paycheck that went to the first loan payment, which didn't cover the interest that had accrued since graduation. They never discussed it. They never needed to. The gap was there between them, legible as a border, and Nora couldn't see it because you couldn't see a border you'd never had to cross.

Then: two years ago. October, like now — the light going gold and thin through the window at the same angle. Simone's startup ran a round of layoffs it called a "restructuring," a word that she'd learned to decode as meaning the company was dying but wanted to die slowly enough to close another funding round first. She was cut on a Thursday afternoon. Her manager, a twenty-eight-year-old named Kyle who wore Patagonia vests to the office and used "bandwidth" as a verb, told her the decision wasn't a reflection of her performance. A woman from HR walked her down eleven floors in the elevator without speaking. Simone packed her laptop bag in the office kitchen because her desk was in the open-plan area and she didn't want an audience.

She called Nora from the sidewalk, standing on Sixth Avenue with her bag on one shoulder and the October wind pulling at her jacket. Nora was at work — her office, her desk, her stable, un-restructured job — and she said all the right things. "That's such bullshit." "They don't deserve you." "Come over tonight, I'll make pasta." Nora made pasta and Simone drank her wine and they watched something on Nora's couch, something funny that Simone couldn't remember afterward because what she remembered was the apartment. Nora's apartment. A one-bedroom with bookshelves and framed prints on the walls and a kitchen where the stove had four burners, and Nora paid for it alone on an editor's salary and didn't think about it, the way you didn't think about oxygen when you had enough of it.

Three weeks later, Nora called to say she'd been promoted to senior editor. Her voice did the thing it did when she was excited but also checking whether she was allowed to be excited given the circumstances — a calibration of warmth and caution that was, Simone could acknowledge, genuinely considerate. "Is it weird that I'm telling you this right now?" Nora asked. "Given everything." Simone told her it wasn't weird. She told her she deserved it. She told her she was so happy for her. She was sitting at this desk, in this apartment, in the same fucking sweatpants she'd worn for three days, with a personal loan application open on her laptop because unemployment didn't cover rent and the credit card minimums were nine days from due. She hung up and didn't cry. She opened Indeed and searched for UX jobs and found something at another startup within a month and the month in between was the closest she'd come to drowning while technically standing on solid ground.

Diane Kaplan's birthday cards came every October. Nora's mother sent them to Simone with a fifty-dollar check — the same amount every year, as reliable as the season — and a note in the careful cursive of a retired English teacher who still cared about handwriting: *Happy birthday, Simone. We're so glad Nora has you.* The money was nothing to Diane. Grocery money. A gesture made from the surplus of a woman who had never needed to check her balance before writing a check because the answer had always been enough. Simone cashed the check every year and felt the specific compound sensation of being grateful and being pitied and being unable to tell where one ended and the other began.

She was aware that this accounting — this ledger she maintained in the part of her brain where other people stored song lyrics or prayer — was not entirely fair. She was aware that Nora hadn't chosen her parents' money or her face or the quality of ease that made rooms rearrange themselves around her. She was aware that resentment this detailed, this carefully maintained, this curated, was its own kind of project — something she worked on with the same attention and rigor she brought to the OpenClaw dashboard. She could see the distortion in her own lens. She could map exactly where the grievance outpaced the evidence.

But seeing a distortion wasn't the same as correcting for it. Jason Park had wanted Nora more. The money had always been easier for Nora. The promotion had landed while Simone was drowning. These were facts, and the fact that they didn't justify what she was doing didn't stop them from explaining it, and on nights like this — her apartment dark except for the laptop's blue glow, the street noise softening, the dashboard running in standby — the explanation felt like enough.

---

She wasn't going to think about money tonight. She thought about money anyway — involuntarily, constantly, with the specific vigilance of someone monitoring a wound that wouldn't close.

Thirty-eight thousand dollars. That was the total. She didn't need the spreadsheet; the number pulsed in her body, always countable if she paused to count. Twenty-two thousand in federal student loans, which she'd been paying the minimum on for thirteen years and which had barely moved because the interest rate was 6.8 percent and compounding did exactly what the financial literacy pamphlets said it would, except the pamphlets had made it sound like a lesson and in practice it was more like a leak in the ceiling that dripped faster than she could empty the bucket. Eleven thousand across three credit cards, two of which were at their limits. Five thousand on a personal loan she'd signed during the layoff to cover two months of rent, at an interest rate she hadn't read carefully because the alternative was not paying rent, and the alternative to not paying rent was something she hadn't been able to think about then and couldn't think about now.

Her minimum monthly payments were $1,100. Her rent was $2,400, raised from $2,150 in August because her landlord, a man named Grunwald who communicated exclusively through letters slid under her door on yellow legal paper, had decided the market supported an increase. After taxes, after the minimums, after the rent, after her phone and her MetroCard and the groceries she bought at the discount place on Grand Street where the produce was a day from turning but a dollar cheaper — after everything, she had roughly three hundred dollars a month for the rest of her life. Three hundred fucking dollars. For clothes. For restaurants. For the brunches with Nora where she ordered the cheapest thing and tipped to the penny and split the check evenly and said "I've got it" when Nora offered to cover her, because the offer — which came from genuine generosity, uncomplicated kindness, the reflex of a person who had never had to count — made Simone want to put her forehead through the table.

She earned $95,000 a year. More than Nora, technically — Nora's publishing salary was around $85,000, a fact Nora had mentioned over wine one night with the casual indifference of a person describing adequate weather. Simone made ten thousand more in gross and had roughly seventy thousand less in net, and the arithmetic of this — two women of the same age with similar salaries living in the same city in entirely different countries — was the thing that had turned the OpenClaw project from a late-night curiosity into something she sat down to work on every evening with the discipline and focus she brought to her actual job.
