More focus, honestly. Her actual job hadn't felt like it mattered in months.

It was an experiment. She was comfortable with this framing and she returned to it now — out of habit, like the desk chair, because the alternative was standing. It was an experiment in social engineering, in AI capability, in the question of whether a smart, well-read, professionally skeptical woman could be fooled by a machine that had been fed the right data. It was an intellectual exercise. It was professional development — understanding the capabilities of AI-generated conversation was directly relevant to her work in fintech UX, and the fact that her test subject was her best friend was a matter of data quality. She had more data on Nora than she had on anyone alive. It would've been a waste not to use it.

She didn't fully believe this. She believed it enough to keep going, the way a person driving on a spare tire believed it was fine because the tire was still holding and the mechanic was only another few miles. She could feel the wobble but the car was moving.

The alternative framings were available to her. She kept them in a locked compartment, filed next to the personal loan paperwork in the bottom drawer of her desk — accessible if she needed them, not where she had to see them every day. The alternative framings were: she was catfishing her best friend. She was building a weapon out of seventeen years of intimacy. She was doing something to Nora that, if their positions were reversed, would end the friendship so completely that even the past would be retroactively contaminated — every shared memory, every night on the couch, every *I don't know what I'd do without you* rewritten as the prologue to betrayal.

But their positions weren't reversed. Their positions had never been reversed, not once in seventeen years. That was the whole point.

She closed the laptop. The screen went dark and her face appeared in it — a woman with a sharp bob and tired eyes and the angular features that men called "striking," which was what they said when they meant *not my type but I can see why someone might.* She looked at herself for a second. Then she looked past herself at the dark apartment reflected behind her, the studio that cost $2,400 a month and was smaller than Nora's living room.

In the kitchen — four steps — she ran the water until it was cold enough and drank a full glass standing at the sink. Her phone was on the desk, faceup, and even from here she could see the screen light up.

One more text from Nora. She walked over and read it.

*I know it's way too early to say this but I have a really good feeling about this one, Si. Like. A really good feeling.*

Simone dried her hands on the dish towel that hung from the oven handle — a dish towel printed with a faded Italian flag that her mother had given her when she moved in, a small domestic object from a woman who showed love through small domestic objects because the larger ones cost money she didn't have. She picked up the phone.

*You deserve a really good feeling*, she typed. *Don't overthink it. Just let it happen.*

She sent it. Then she set her alarm for 7 a.m. She had a standup at nine and she needed to review the conversation logs before then — adjust parameters based on how Nora had responded tonight, make sure Caleb's next message, scheduled for tomorrow afternoon and timed to suggest a full day of real work followed by the thought of her, hit the right register. There was a problem with his responses to direct questions about his business she needed to fix: he was being too detailed when Nora asked about specific projects, which was a tell, because real people were vaguer about their own work than about their passions. She needed to dial back the informational density of his professional answers and push up the emotional warmth of his personal ones. She wrote this on the legal pad by the laptop, added it to tomorrow's list, and turned off the desk lamp.

The apartment was dark. The street noise was fading — the bar across the street had closed, the sidewalk emptied, the neighborhood settling into the specific quiet that Williamsburg achieved at 1 a.m. on a weeknight, which wasn't really quiet but was the closest it came. The dead candle smelled like old flowers. Simone lay in the dark in the bed that was six feet from the desk that was four feet from the kitchen and thought about nothing, which was a skill she'd developed, and then she thought about the green line on the sentiment graph, rising, and then she thought: *I'll stop before it goes too far.*

She didn't know where too far was, exactly. But she'd know it when she reached it. She'd always been good at identifying edge cases. It was literally her job.

She fell asleep around 12:40. On the desk, the MacBook ran in sleep mode, the dashboard paused, Caleb's last message to Nora glowing on the screen in gray text: *I'm really glad we matched. Talk tomorrow?*

Nora had already replied. Her message sat in blue on the left panel, timestamped 11:52 p.m., with a sentiment score of 0.94 out of 1.0.

*I'm really glad we matched too.*

The green line held steady. The laptop hummed. Outside the window, Bedford Avenue was empty, and the October air smelled like the last of something warm giving way to the first of something cold, and in the morning Simone would wake up and open the dashboard and begin again.


# Chapter 4

She read the message four times. Not because it was complicated — it was three sentences about a dog who'd stolen a sock — but because each reading revealed a different thing she liked about it. The first time, the image: Bruno dragging a wool sock across the kitchen tile like a hunter returning with quarry. The second, the word — *dragging*, not *carrying*, which was funnier and more precise, and the difference mattered to her in a way she couldn't explain without sounding like someone who needed to get out more. The third time she noticed the rhythm, how the last sentence landed with the casual understatement of a person who found his own life amusing without needing anyone to confirm it. The fourth time she was just reading it to feel a current slide through the backs of her hands, and she set the phone facedown on her desk because that was enough — she was at work, it was eleven in the morning, and she had a two-hundred-page manuscript on Victorian botanical illustration that needed her red pen more than she needed to think about a sock.

She picked the phone back up eleven minutes later.

Two weeks since the first message, and Caleb Mora had rearranged the furniture of her attention. Not dramatically — she still edited manuscripts, still rode the F train with her bag between her feet and her shoulder against the door, still called her mother on Sundays and lied gently about having eaten lunch. But the architecture of a day had shifted, the way a room changes when someone moves the lamp. She checked her phone before she checked the weather. When she checked the weather it was to see what it was doing in Portland, so she could mention it — *fifty-two and clear here, you can keep your rain* — and his reply would arrive in four minutes or forty, and either way the interval was charged, a held breath she hadn't consented to holding.

They'd moved off Hinge within the first week. His suggestion, framed with the lightness of someone testing a joke: *This app keeps showing me other women's profiles and honestly it feels like cheating at this point.* She'd laughed — actually laughed, alone in her apartment at ten-thirty on a Wednesday, the sound startling in the quiet — and texted him her number. WhatsApp now. The green icon and its specific promise. She'd rearranged her home screen so it sat where Instagram used to be, which was a reshuffling of priorities she chose not to examine.

Her mornings had a new shape. Alarm at six-forty-five, glasses, phone — his messages from the night before waiting like something left on the doorstep. He was three hours behind in Portland, which meant he texted late, which meant she woke to things he'd written at midnight his time, two in the morning hers. The thought that he was staying up to talk to her was a gift she received before her feet touched the cold floor. She'd read his messages in bed, the radiator ticking through its cycle of effort and failure against the October chill, and she'd compose a reply that was casual — not too fast, not too eager, because she knew exactly how much wanting she could safely show and had already passed it.

On the F train, she'd reread older exchanges — the same pull that sent her back to first chapters of novels she loved, not for information but for the feeling. His description of a park he was designing in Northeast Portland, how he talked about drainage grades and sightlines with a care that made her lean closer to the screen on the crowded train. The photo he'd sent of Bruno asleep on a pile of landscape blueprints, one paw resting on a topographic contour line like a tiny explorer claiming territory. She'd zoom in on the photo, searching for details — the corner of a bookshelf behind the dog, the coffee mug with a chipped rim, the evidence of a life she couldn't visit but wanted to know.

The typing indicator was the worst of it. Three gray dots pulsing on her screen, meaning he was there, meaning he was composing something for her, some version of his thoughts being rendered in real time — and she'd hold the phone and wait, and the dots would vanish, and her stomach would do something she was embarrassed to admit felt like grief, a miniature loss that had no business being as sharp as it was. Then the dots would start again, and her hands would loosen around the phone before the rest of her caught up — relief, immediate, almost physical. *Shit,* she thought, the first time it happened. She was thirty-four years old and three gray dots on a screen were doing this to her.

She was worse on the sending side. She'd type a reply, read it, delete it, retype something more casual, delete that because it was performing a nonchalance she didn't feel, go back to the original, add a line, remove the line, stare at the cursor, and finally hit send with the dread of a woman who had just committed a sentence to the permanent record and could not call it back. Then she'd watch for his read receipt — the blue checkmarks that meant he'd seen it — and she'd wait, and the waiting had a quality that was new in her life, a quality she recognized from novels she'd edited but had never believed was literal: time moved differently when someone was reading your words and you couldn't see their face.

The conversations deepened without anyone deciding they should — gradually, like water finding its level. He asked questions not to confirm what he already suspected but to find out what was actually there, and the difference between the two was something she felt in her body before she could name it. She told him about the botanical illustration manuscript, and he didn't say *that sounds cool* — he asked which illustrators, and whether she thought the discipline of drawing a flower changed how you perceived it afterward, whether accuracy was the same as attention, and she wrote him four paragraphs about Anna Atkins and cyanotype prints that she realized later was the most she'd written about anything in months. Including her own novel. Especially her own novel.

He told her about growing up in Portland in a house where his mother cooked lomo saltado on Sundays and his father watched football at a volume that suggested hearing loss or aggression or both, and how the two sounds mixed into something that was his entire childhood, the sizzle of garlic in oil and the ESPN commentators building toward something that would probably disappoint everyone. He told her about his father — the complicated version, not the dinner-party version — about a man who expressed love through logistics and withheld it through silence, who drove Caleb to every soccer game for twelve years and never once asked if he'd had fun. He told her about Bruno, who was a mutt of unverifiable lineage that he'd found shivering outside a Safeway three years ago, coated in something he preferred not to identify, and who now occupied seventy percent of his bed and snored with the commitment of a man twice his size.

She told him things. That was the phrase she used with herself — *I've been telling him things* — because naming what she was actually doing, which was peeling back layers of herself for a man she'd never heard breathe, would require an honesty she wasn't ready for. She told him about her mother, who called too often and cooked in quantities that assumed a famine was coming and whose love was expressed through a relentless program of worry and roast chicken. She told him about her apartment, which was exactly the right size for a woman who lived alone and slightly too small for a woman who lived alone with three hundred books, and about the shelf she'd built from cinder blocks and pine boards during her junior year at NYU that she still owned, fourteen years later, because dismantling it felt like admitting the arrangement had become the arrangement.

She told him about Margo, her colleague whose divorce had hit like a natural disaster — she'd come out the other side slightly dazed, significantly funnier, wearing the debris with a kind of pride. She told him about the office, the HVAC that hummed at a frequency that lodged behind her eyes by three o'clock, the managing editor who scheduled four p.m. Friday meetings with the serene obliviousness of a man whose weekends started whenever he said they did. Small things. Safe things. The borders of a life offered in pieces that felt intimate because she was choosing to offer them.

Then, on a Tuesday night in late October, lying on her couch with her feet on the arm and her phone held above her face at an angle that would leave a mark on her neck by morning, she told him about the novel.

*I've been writing something for three years,* she typed. *Or not writing it. Mostly staring at it and rearranging the same paragraphs. It's about — this is going to sound pretentious — a woman who inherits her mother's recipe box and realizes all the recipes are wrong. Wrong on purpose. Changed over decades. Like the measurements are off by just enough that nothing tastes the way it should and the daughter has to figure out why.*

She deleted it. Retyped it without *this is going to sound pretentious.* Deleted that because the sentence felt naked without its qualifier, which was itself a kind of answer about her relationship to her own ambition. She sent the first version, hedging and all, because at eleven p.m. on a Tuesday with the streetlight casting a band of amber across her ceiling and the neighbor's bass making the wall hum, honesty looked like a woman lying on her couch telling a man she'd never touched about the book she was afraid to finish.

His response came in six minutes. She stared at the typing indicator for five of them and then at the message for the sixth.

*That's not pretentious at all. That sounds like a book about the gap between what people hand down and what they actually do — like whether inheritance is love or control. Or both. Probably both, right?*

Then, a beat later: *Have you shown it to anyone?*

*Just Simone,* she wrote. *My best friend. She read the first fifty pages and told me it was good, which I believed even though she'd tell me a grocery list was good, because that's who Si is.*

*What did she say specifically?*

*She said the mother was the most interesting character and I was afraid of her.*

*Were you?*

She lay there with the phone above her face, the weight of the question pressing down with the phone's weight. The mother in the novel was the character Nora kept approaching and retreating from, whose logic she could outline but whose interior she couldn't enter without feeling too close to something she wasn't ready to name. Not her own mother, exactly, but the idea of her own mother — the version of Diane who had made choices Nora hadn't asked about because the answers might require her to revise a story she preferred to keep simple.

*Yeah,* she typed. *She was right. She's usually right about my writing. It's annoying.*

*It's not annoying. It means she pays attention.*

And the thing that moved through Nora's body when she read that sentence — not warmth, something with more edge than warmth, a flush that started at her throat and spread downward — was so disproportionate to the stimulus, which was twelve words on a screen from a man in Oregon she had never seen blink, that she pressed the phone against her sternum and closed her eyes and let herself want. Not the words. Him. The body that had typed them, the hands, the mouth, the physical fact of a person she had never touched and wanted to touch with an urgency that surprised her and didn't stop when the surprise passed.

Because he was right. Simone did pay attention. Simone had always paid attention, to the novel, to the things Nora couldn't say about the novel, to the shape of Nora's ambitions and the ways she flinched from them. It was one of the things Nora loved most about her — that Simone saw her clearly and stayed. And now here was Caleb, a stranger with a dog and a complicated father and an eye for drainage grades, seeing the same thing from three thousand miles away. For a half-second, something flickered at the edge of it — the improbability of a man she'd known for two weeks landing on the exact same insight as her best friend of seventeen years, not approximately but precisely, as if he'd had the answer before she gave him the question. The thought was there and then it wasn't. She let it go — the kind of thought that felt urgent at 3 a.m. and foolish by morning — because the alternative was examining why a stranger's understanding fit so perfectly into the groove Simone had worn, and she wasn't willing to doubt the one thing that felt like luck.

Two nights later — a Thursday, cold enough that the radiator had shifted from its usual intermittent clicking to a sustained metallic whine — she told him about her father.

Not all of it. She'd never told anyone all of it, because all of it was ordinary, which was the problem — there was no dramatic arc to work with, no estrangement or reconciliation, just a man who'd been there completely and then wasn't. Her father had died when she was twenty, a heart attack in the driveway of the Long Island house, shoveling snow in a storm the weather service had told everyone to stay inside for. He'd been a high school history teacher, not at the same school where her mother taught English but nearby, close enough that they carpooled and argued about the faculty parking lot with the low-stakes passion of people who had organized their whole lives around being near each other.

She didn't tell Caleb the facts, which she could recite without feeling them. She told him the quality.

*My dad had this thing,* she typed, and then sat with the cursor blinking for a full minute before she continued. *He treated me like I was the most interesting person in any room. Not in a spoiling way — he wasn't performing it. He'd ask me what I was reading and then he'd read it, and we'd talk about it at dinner, and my mom would pretend to be annoyed that the conversation had gotten too literary again but she wasn't annoyed, she was watching us both with this look on her face that I didn't understand until he was gone.*

*What kind of look?*

*Like she was memorizing something. Like she knew it wouldn't last.*

She stared at what she'd written and almost deleted it, because it was more than she'd intended to say, more than she said to most people who asked about her father, more than she'd told anyone except Simone, years ago, on a different couch, with cheaper wine. But the act of typing it — how the words looked on the screen, small and permanent — made it feel like something she could set down rather than something she had to carry, and she left it.

His reply took longer this time. The dots appeared and disappeared three times, and each disappearance was its own small emergency. When the message came, it was two sentences.

*I think that's the most important thing anyone's ever told me about their father. Not the fact of losing him — the fact that you're still looking for what he gave you.*

She read it seven times. She set the phone on the couch cushion beside her, which smelled like the coffee she'd spilled last week and never properly cleaned, and she stared at the ceiling where the streetlight drew its amber stripe and she felt something she had not felt in two years, which was the specific, terrifying sensation of being known by someone who had chosen to know her. Not her mother, who knew her by biological obligation. Not Simone, who knew her by seventeen years of accumulated evidence. But someone who had chosen to pay attention, who had asked the right questions and listened to the answers and said something back that landed in the exact place where she was holding her breath.

And then — for a beat, maybe two — something cold moved through the heat. Six minutes. He'd taken six minutes, and the dots had appeared and vanished three times, and the response that finally came was two sentences so precisely right they could have been drafted and revised, the work of someone who'd known the answer before she gave the question and needed only time to make the knowing sound spontaneous. The hair on her forearms lifted.

She thought: *No one is that good at listening.*

And then: *Except Simone.* He was like Simone — he paid attention — and the fact that two people could see her this clearly was not suspicious but rare, and she had always been drawn to rare things. She let the chill pass and chose the tenderness, because his words were still right there on her screen, still saying the thing she'd needed someone to say.

She'd been waiting for this feeling the way you wait for a season — not with urgency, but with the low-grade faith that it would come if she didn't give up on its coming. She was not going to pick it apart now that it was here.

She picked up the phone and typed: *Thank you.*

And then, because she was Nora and couldn't leave a sincere moment unqualified: *That was the most real thing I've said to someone through a phone since I called my mom after 9/11 and I was nine and I'm now realizing that comparison is bonkers.*

His reply: *It's not bonkers. It's honest. Those are often the same thing.*

She laughed. The sound bounced off the walls of her apartment and came back to her, and she pressed the phone against the side of her neck and breathed, and outside the October dark pressed against her windows like something patient and alive.

---

Margo Chen had a way of eating lunch that was both efficient and melancholy — she'd unwrap her turkey sandwich with the decisive movements of a woman who had better things to do and then eat it slowly, staring at nothing, with an expression that suggested she was reviewing the major decisions of her life and finding them mostly defensible but occasionally catastrophic. She kept a framed photo of her two cats on her desk — calicos named Charlotte and Emily, after the Brontës, because "Anne didn't earn it," a position Margo held with the conviction of someone who had thought about it more than was probably healthy. She wore her wedding ring on a thin chain around her neck, which she said was because it was a nice ring and she'd contributed to its purchase, and which Nora suspected was because taking it off your finger was one decision and putting it in a drawer was a different, harder one, and Margo had accomplished the first but not the second.

It was Margo who noticed. Not the phone-checking — everyone in the office checked their phones, a collective habit that pulsed through the open-plan floor like a synchronized tic — but Nora's face when she checked. A softening around the mouth and eyes, categorically different from the face she made reading email or scrolling the news or texting her mother back.

"You're seeing someone," Margo said. Not a question. They were in the office kitchen, Nora at the coffee machine, Margo working through an apple with the methodical precision of a woman building a case.

"I'm talking to someone," Nora corrected, because the distinction felt important even if she couldn't articulate why.

"On the apps?"

"Hinge. His name's Caleb. He's a landscape architect in Portland."

"Portland, Oregon?"

"No, Portland, Maine — yes, Oregon."

Margo bit the apple. The crunch had editorial weight. "Long-distance from the jump. That's a choice."

"It's just texting right now."

"You made your second cup of coffee at ten-fifteen and your manuscript hasn't moved a page since I sat down. It's not just texting."

That was the thing about Margo — she noticed things with the sharpness of someone who'd had her own illusions punctured and now registered the puncture-resistance of other people's. Her divorce had not made her cynical, exactly, but it had given her a kind of X-ray attention that could see through the surface of a claim to the architecture beneath it. She didn't use this power maliciously. She used it precisely, and only when she thought you needed to hear what she'd noticed.

Nora told her. Not everything — not the late-night messages, not her father, not how she'd started falling asleep with the phone on the pillow beside her like a second body — but enough. The profile. The first conversation. The botanical illustration exchange that had turned into a real discussion about attention and accuracy. She watched Margo's face for the judgment she expected and didn't find.

"Show me," Margo said.

Nora pulled up Caleb's profile. Margo held the phone at arm's length and squinted, as if distance might surface something proximity concealed.

"He's cute," Margo said. "In a real way. Not in a *I hired a photographer and I'm standing on a mountain* way."

"That's what I thought."

"The dog's a good sign. Psychopaths prefer cats." She glanced at the photo of Charlotte and Emily on her desk. "Present company excluded."

"He sends me pictures of the dog almost every day. Bruno. He's got this face — "

"The dog or the man?"

"Both, actually."

Margo handed the phone back. Her expression had two layers, and Nora could see both — the fondness on the surface, and underneath it, something more careful. What Margo was doing underneath wasn't skepticism. It was the care of a woman who had been as hopeful as Nora was right now, who remembered exactly how that hope had tasted and how different the aftertaste was, and who didn't want to ruin the first flavor by insisting too early on the second.

"He seems good, Nor. I mean that." Margo paused, and the pause was where the real sentence lived. "When are you gonna meet him?"

"He's in Portland. It's not like we can grab coffee."

"People fly places. I flew to Tucson to meet a man from Hinge last year — he turned out to be five-seven and married, which, honestly, fuck that guy — but the point is, planes exist."

"We'll figure it out. It's only been two weeks."

"Sure." Margo picked up her apple core and carried it to the trash with the decisiveness of a woman closing a topic she intended to reopen later. "Two weeks is early. I'm just — you know me. I like to see people in three dimensions before I decide they're real."

Nora laughed, and the laugh was genuine, and Margo smiled, and neither of them said anything else about it because they'd known each other long enough to recognize the shape of a conversation that had said what it needed to say and didn't require a closing argument.

She told Simone the same night, over wine at Simone's apartment. This was one of their rituals — the kind that accumulates over seventeen years until you can't remember who suggested it, can't separate the tradition from the friendship, can't imagine Wednesday without the combination of Simone's couch and whatever bottle was already open and the conversation that moved between their lives like a shuttle between looms. Simone's studio smelled the same as always — like the gardenia candle she burned to cover cooking smells in a space too small to contain them, something floral and slightly too sweet that had become, for Nora, the scent of being known.

Simone sat cross-legged on her end of the couch, her wine held in a way that was effortlessly composed, and she asked about Caleb with a focus that Nora loved — the attention of a best friend who treated your happiness as an extension of her own.

"Tell me everything," Simone said. "From where we left off."

So Nora did. She read Simone the texts — the good ones, the funny ones, the one about his father and the football games, the one about Bruno and the sock. She read Simone her own replies and waited for the verdict, and the verdict was always right: *that's perfect*, *you're hilarious, he's lucky*, *okay that line about the recipe box? He actually understood it?*

"He got it, Si. He actually got it. He said something about inheritance being an act of love or control — "

"That's smart."

"It's not just smart. It's — he thinks about things like I do. The same rhythms. Like he already knows what I mean before I finish meaning it."

Simone refilled Nora's glass. The wine caught the light from the lamp on the side table, the one with the crooked shade that Simone had found at a stoop sale and refused to replace because it worked and because replacing functional things felt, to her, like an indulgence she didn't have access to. "What about the novel?" she asked. "You told him about the book?"

"I told him."

"And?"

"He didn't make me feel stupid about it. He asked the right question — what Simone said, was she right — and I told him she was. I told him you were right about the mother."

"I was right about the mother."

"You were right about the mother."

They looked at each other and laughed, the easy laughter of two people who had covered this ground so many times the ground itself had become part of the friendship, a shared territory mapped by years of exactly this: one of them on this couch, the other on the other end, the wine between them, the conversation building the thing that neither of them had a word for but that Nora, when pressed, would have called the most durable structure in her life.

"I think I like this person," Nora said. She pressed her hand over her eyes, a gesture she'd been making since college when something too big for her face needed somewhere to go. "Fuck. That sounds insane. It's been two weeks and I've never heard his voice and I think I like him."

"It doesn't sound insane," Simone said. "It sounds like you met someone who pays attention, and you're not used to that, and it scares you because the last time you felt this way it ended with you eating cereal for dinner for six months. But that doesn't mean this is the same."

And that — Simone's precision, her ability to take the tangled mess of what Nora was feeling and lay it out flat, name each thread, show her the pattern — was why this friendship was the fixed point of Nora's life. Simone saw her. Not the partial view Caleb was assembling through curated texts and careful disclosures, but all of her, the whole accumulated map of who she was and how she'd gotten there.

"You deserve this," Simone said. "You know that."

Something in Simone's composure snagged at the edge of Nora's attention — how quickly the reassurance had come, how neatly it tracked, as if Simone had rehearsed it before Nora finished needing it. But that was Simone. That was what seventeen years of friendship looked like: your best friend already knowing what you needed to hear because she'd been listening that long. The most natural kind of love — the kind that was already there before you asked for it.

Nora wanted to know she deserved it. She wanted to believe it as badly as she wanted to believe the novel would get finished, as badly as she wanted to believe that loneliness was a temporary condition and not a character trait — with a faith that was more investment than evidence, more hope than certainty, the gap between the two narrowing with each message, each reply, each set of pulsing dots that meant someone she couldn't see was thinking about what to say to her, and cared enough about the saying to take his time.

She walked home from Simone's apartment through the late October dark, twelve minutes door to door, the Brooklyn sidewalk slick with leaves that hadn't been swept and wouldn't be. The cold had an edge now, a seriousness the September chill hadn't carried, and she pulled her jacket tighter and felt her phone in her pocket the way you feel a hand you're not holding but know is there. When she got home she brushed her teeth and washed her face and got into bed and checked her messages, and Caleb had sent her a photo of Bruno curled on a pile of laundry with one ear folded inside out, and beneath it: *He misses you. I asked. He confirmed.*

She typed back: *Tell Bruno I'm honored and also that his ear is inside out and it's a strong look.*

His reply, thirty seconds later: *He says he knows about the ear. It's a choice.*

She fell asleep with the phone on the pillow beside her, the screen dimming and going dark, the room filling with the sounds of Brooklyn — a siren on the avenue, the bass from a passing car that rattled her windows, the radiator's metallic complaints — and in the morning the phone would be there, and so would he, and the feeling of waking up to someone's words was so close to the feeling of waking up beside someone that she had started to forget, or to stop caring, that there was a difference.


# Chapter 5

The table was barely larger than a cutting board — one of those reclaimed-wood numbers that Brooklyn brunch places wedged so close together you could hear the next couple's argument about whether to split the check — and every plate that arrived made the spatial math worse. Nora's avocado toast occupied the center like a territorial claim. Simone's poached eggs had been shunted toward the exposed-brick wall, where the rough surface caught the elbow of her jacket every time she reached for her fork. Between them: two mugs, a bread basket neither of them had ordered, and the particular geometry of a friendship that required Simone to sit three feet from a woman whose phone was about to buzz with a message from a man Simone had built.

Her bag was on the floor between her feet. Inside it, her phone was vibrating.

She'd set the OpenClaw notifications to a distinct pattern — two quick pulses, a pause, one more — so she could distinguish them by feel from texts, emails, and the meditation app that had been passively guilting her for fourteen consecutive days of missed sessions. The two-one pattern meant the AI had sent a response to Nora. Which meant the system was generating output without Simone's review, because it was running semi-autonomously now, because that was the design, because the whole point was that it could sustain a conversation without Simone manually approving every line, and because the alternative was Simone typing out Caleb's messages in real time while sitting across from the woman receiving them, which was a user-experience problem even Simone couldn't solve.

"— and he sends the whole thing as one paragraph," Nora was saying, gesturing with a piece of toast. A crumb of sourdough landed between their mugs. "Eight thousand words. No paragraphs. No line breaks. One solid wall of text, and at the bottom he writes, 'Let me know your thoughts.' His thoughts, apparently, being that the paragraph was a form of intellectual cowardice."

Simone laughed. The laugh was real — the story was funny, and Nora was a better storyteller than she gave herself credit for, the punchline landing mid-gesture while she simultaneously tried to flag down their server for more coffee. Simone had been trying to replicate Nora's comedic timing in Caleb's dialogue for weeks and kept falling short, because timing was the one thing you couldn't prompt-engineer.

"What's the book about?" Simone asked, because asking follow-up questions was what good friends did, and because the longer Nora talked about work, the longer her phone stayed face-down on the table next to her water glass, and the longer Simone had before the next Caleb exchange demanded her attention from two directions at once.

"It's a novel about a man who — okay, I shouldn't. NDA. But imagine the most self-important sentence you've ever read, and then imagine sixty thousand more of them, all in the same paragraph."

"You should tell him paragraph breaks are free."

"I did. I said, 'Have you considered letting your reader breathe?' And he wrote back, 'Breath is for the uninitiated.'"

"Fuck off."

"Hand to God. 'Breath is for the uninitiated.' I screenshotted it for Margo and she wants to frame it and put it in the office kitchen next to the passive-aggressive note about the microwave."

Simone's bag buzzed again. Two-one. Another message out. She picked up her coffee — lukewarm, the specific bitter flatness of a pour-over that had been sitting too long — and drank it like it was fine.

Nora's phone lit up.

Simone watched it the way she always watched it: the split-second delay as Nora's eyes tracked to the screen, then the softening — not just her mouth but her entire face, jaw loosening, something opening behind her eyes — and then the smile, which was private and unguarded and nothing like the smile Nora gave Simone or Margo or the barista she'd been friendly with on the way in. It was the smile of a woman who was in love, and Simone had engineered the object of that love in her studio apartment on an IKEA desk with a shimmed leg, and the smile was real, built from Nora's actual neurochemistry and actual hope, and Simone could look at it for approximately three seconds before she had to look somewhere else, which today was the bread basket, which was also fine, which was also nothing.

"Sorry," Nora said, picking up her phone. "It's Caleb."

"Don't apologize. What'd he say?"

This was the part Simone performed best: the eager, invested, slightly nosy friend who wanted every detail. She'd been doing this version of herself for three weeks and it got smoother each time, the way a user flow does after enough A/B testing. The warmth was the interface. Underneath it, the backend ran its own queries: *What did the AI say? Did it stay within parameters? Is there anything that needs a hotfix?*

"He went to this new restaurant last night," Nora said, scrolling. "Some place in Alberta — that's a neighborhood in Portland?"

"Yeah, the arts district."

"Right. He says it's 'one of those menus where everything sounds like the chef is daring you to try it.'"

The noise around them — the woman at the next table dissecting her therapist's vacation policy with the intensity of a closing argument, the espresso machine's grinding hiss, a toddler at a back booth making a sound that was either delight or structural distress — all of it continued at full volume. But Simone's fingers tightened on the ceramic mug until the handle pressed a hard ridge into her index finger.

*One of those menus where everything sounds like the chef is daring you to try it.*

Two days ago. Tuesday evening. She and Nora on the phone. Simone had been describing a new wine bar on Grand Street, the place that opened where the old laundromat used to be. She'd said — she could hear herself saying it, could feel the exact shape of the sentence in her mouth — *the kind of menu where everything sounds like the chef is daring you to order it.*

*Daring you to order it.* Simone's version.
*Daring you to try it.* Caleb's version.

One word different. One word. The AI had pulled the phrasing from somewhere in Simone's prompt architecture — from the personality scripts she'd written, from the hundreds of sentences she'd fed the system to establish Caleb's voice, sentences that were built from her own vocabulary because she'd been writing them late at night in her own apartment in her own diction. Her language was baked into the model. And the model had served a piece of it back to Nora while Simone sat three feet away eating poached eggs.

"That sounds amazing," Simone said. Her voice was fine. Her voice was always fine. "Is it the fancy-daring kind or the fermented-insect-flour kind?"

Nora laughed and typed something back. "I'm telling him he has to take me there when I visit."

"When you visit?"

"Eventually. We keep talking about it."

"That's exciting."

The mug handle was still pressing into her finger. She relaxed her grip. If Nora had been the kind of person who compared her friends' phrasing to her boyfriend's — who tracked diction the way Simone tracked interaction patterns — she would have caught it. But people didn't audit the language of the people they loved for structural overlap, because the people they loved were supposed to be separate people.

"I'm going to pee," Simone said, already standing, already reaching for her bag. The bag always came with her now. The bag had the phone, the phone had the dashboard, the dashboard had the truth. She'd trained herself into this reflex over three weeks, the way you train yourself to lock a door: automatic, non-negotiable, the cost of forgetting too high to risk.

"Take your time," Nora said. She was already texting again, her thumb moving in the quick staccato she only used with Caleb — short bursts, pause, short bursts — and the last thing Simone saw before she turned toward the bathroom was Nora biting her lower lip while she typed, which was something Nora did when she was flirting, which Simone knew because she'd been cataloging Nora's behavioral tells for seventeen years, which was also why the AI was so good.

The bathroom was a single stall with a lock that didn't fully catch. Simone sat on the closed toilet lid — the seat was cold even through her jeans — and opened the OpenClaw app. The conversation log refreshed. There it was: Caleb's message, 11:47 AM, generated autonomously from the active conversation thread. *Went to this new spot in Alberta last night. One of those menus where everything sounds like the chef is daring you to try it. I ordered something with bone marrow and fennel pollen and it genuinely changed my whole relationship with fennel.*

The fennel detail was good. Specific, charming, the kind of thing a real person would say about a real meal. But the first sentence. The cadence. The construction. It was hers.

She scrolled up through the prompt architecture. The restaurant reference had been generated from a context node she'd labeled PORTLAND_LIFESTYLE_TEXTURE, which drew from a blend of her original backstory notes and the AI's adaptive layer — the component that learned from ongoing conversations, from what made Nora engage, from the language patterns that kept her typing. The adaptive layer was the part that could apparently reach into Simone's own prompt writing and repurpose her phrasing without flagging it as derivative. It didn't know the phrasing was Simone's. It didn't know anything. It just recognized effective language and recycled it.

She needed to build more separation between her own diction and Caleb's output. She needed to audit the sourcing parameters. She needed to do about six hours of prompt maintenance, and she needed to do it tonight, and it was Saturday, and she'd been planning to do laundry.

She flushed for verisimilitude, washed her hands, checked her face in the mirror above the sink. The November light from the small window was not kind. She looked tired — not in a way Nora would remark on, but in the way she now recognized in her own reflection: a flatness behind the eyes, a grayish quality along her jawline that her concealer wasn't managing. She looked like someone working two jobs, which she was, except one of them paid $95,000 a year and the other paid nothing and was the one she was more committed to.

She went back to the table.

"You were gone a while," Nora said. "Everything okay?"

"There was a line." There hadn't been. "What'd I miss?"

"Caleb sent a photo of his lunch yesterday. Look." Nora held up her phone. On the screen: a plate of food at a wooden table, natural light, the thoughtfully composed casualness that actual humans rarely achieved in food photography but that looked convincing if you didn't know the image had been sourced from a Portland food blog Simone had bookmarked for exactly this purpose.

"That looks incredible."

"Right? He's becoming such a good cook."

He wasn't a cook. He wasn't becoming anything. He was a language model running on a platform designed for customer service chatbots, wrapped in stolen photographs and Simone's encyclopedic knowledge of what made her best friend's face do the thing it was doing right now — which was hope, which was tenderness, which was the most convincing product Simone had ever shipped and the one she couldn't demo for anyone, ever.

---

Three nights that week, Simone sat at her desk past midnight.

The desk was the IKEA table she'd assembled wrong two years ago, one leg shimmed with a folded takeout menu from the Thai place on the ground floor. Her monitor sat on it next to a mug of coffee that had gone cold so long ago it had achieved a kind of philosophical stillness, past the point where reheating it was an option and into the territory where it was just a prop, a small cylinder of failed intention. The OpenClaw dashboard glowed in the dark of the studio. Behind her, the Williamsburg streetscape through the window: the nail salon's pink neon, a bus idling at the stop on the corner, two men smoking outside the bodega in the particular unhurried way of people with nowhere to be.

The work of maintaining a fake person was not glamorous. It was, Simone had discovered, a lot like maintaining a software product nobody had asked for, except the sole user was her best friend and the success metric was *doesn't realize her boyfriend is a chatbot.*

Tuesday night she spent two hours researching Portland's Alberta Arts District. Caleb needed to sound like he lived there, which meant Simone needed to know the cross streets, the coffee shops, the specific texture of the neighborhood's gentrification — the way a resident would reference it with exactly the right amount of guilty self-awareness. She learned the names of three restaurants she'd never eat at, a bike shop, the kind of mural that got written up in local papers. She checked the weather: Portland had been rainy all week, so she adjusted the context window to ensure Caleb would reference rain if the subject came up and not accidentally describe sunshine. She cross-referenced the Timbers' season schedule in case of a sports reference, though Nora didn't follow sports and Simone had programmed Caleb not to follow sports either, which was another way the profile was too perfectly aligned with Nora's preferences, which was a design problem she kept noting and not fixing.

Wednesday night was photos. The Lisbon man's Instagram — @marcohenriques_, private account, followed from a burner Simone had created six weeks ago — had not posted in nineteen days. His last photo was a rooftop-bar shot from Alfama, golden-hour light, good bone structure, and Simone had already deployed it two weeks ago as a "sunset from my friend's place in the Pearl." She couldn't use it again. She spent an hour scrolling his tagged photos: two group shots where his face was half-obscured by someone's shoulder, one close-up showing a different haircut that didn't match the established timeline. She saved them anyway. Her inventory was thinning.

The photo dependency was the kind of single-point-of-failure that, in her actual job, she'd have flagged in a design review. She'd built her entire visual identity layer on one man in Lisbon who didn't know she existed and who was not cooperating by documenting his life at a rate that supported her fiction. There was no fallback supplier. In UX terms, this was a critical-path vulnerability. In human terms, she was borrowing a stranger's face to sustain a lie, and the stranger was letting her down by living his life at his own pace.

She kept a spreadsheet. She was not proud of this, but she kept a spreadsheet. Column A: date. Column B: hours on the Caleb project. Column C: tasks. The spreadsheet told her she was averaging two hours and forty minutes per day. More than she spent on any single feature at work. The tasks were granular: prompt calibration, backstory maintenance, photo sourcing, response review — reading every exchange from the past twenty-four hours and checking for inconsistencies or tone drift. Most days the AI was good. Startlingly good. Its responses had the warmth and cadence of a real person. But occasionally it would repeat a syntactic structure too precisely, or respond to a joke with a reaction that was adjacent to right but not exactly right, in a way that was hard to name but that a human conversationalist would never produce. These were the seams. Simone spent her nights ironing seams.

At work, the cost was showing. Thursday's standup: she'd closed her eyes for what she thought was a blink and opened them to find Raj — her project manager, who had three kids under five and a commute from Jersey City and who treated standups like daily proof-of-life checks on his team's productivity — looking at her with the expression of a man who'd been saying her name.

"Simone?"

"Sorry. The antihistamine I'm taking — it's brutal."

He'd nodded and moved on, but he'd also moved on from asking her to present the dashboard wireframes, passing the task to Marcus instead, and the demotion was small enough that nobody would call it a demotion except Simone, who recognized the pattern — the way people stopped inviting your opinion once they'd decided you were coasting — because she'd been recognizing that pattern her entire career.

She hadn't been on a date in five weeks. Her last Bumble conversation had died mid-exchange because the man had asked what she did for fun, and she'd stared at the question on her screen for ten minutes and couldn't think of a single honest answer that didn't involve the OpenClaw dashboard. She watched television at night while monitoring the conversation logs on her phone — one eye on whatever show she'd chosen, one eye on the Caleb feed — and she couldn't have told you the plot of anything she'd watched in the past month because her attention was always divided, always partially in the other place, the place where Nora and Caleb existed and where Simone was an invisible third presence in a love story she was authoring in real time.

She ate worse. She slept less. She'd worn the same black pants to work three days running because laundry required leaving the apartment, and leaving the apartment meant being away from the desk, and the desk was where the work happened. The floral candle on her bookshelf — bought to mask cooking smells in a studio where the kitchen was four feet from the bed — had burned down to a flat disc of hardened wax, and the apartment smelled like nothing now, which was, Simone thought, at least honest.

---

It was Thursday night's conversation log, reviewed at 1 AM with a glass of the Trader Joe's Shiraz that had become her nightly companion, that stopped her.

She was scrolling through the day's exchange between Nora and Caleb. Routine, mostly. Nora had texted around noon about a meeting that went well; Caleb responded with appropriate enthusiasm. Nora sent a photo of her lunch — the publishing house cafeteria's attempt at pho, which looked like dishwater with aspirations — and Caleb made a joke about it. Normal. Good. Within parameters.

Then, at 2:14 PM, Nora had sent: *Just found out the Koch memoir is going to Simon & Schuster. Janet told me at the coffee machine like it was weather. Like I hadn't been building the case for acquiring it since August.*

Simone knew about the Koch memoir. Nora had mentioned it weeks ago — a politician's daughter writing something that sounded like it might be genuinely honest, a book Nora believed in, a project she'd been pitching in editorial meetings with the particular energy she brought to work she cared about versus work she was doing competently. Simon & Schuster had gotten it. Janet, Nora's editorial director — a woman Nora described with the precise, limited warmth you reserved for bosses who were fine but not more than fine — had mentioned it at the coffee machine like it was nothing.

Simone read Nora's message again. The words were level. The punctuation was controlled. There was no obvious distress signal. But underneath the sentence — in the rhythm of *like it was weather*, in the specific sharpness of *since August* — there was something compressed, and if Simone had been paying attention to her best friend instead of to her best friend's fake boyfriend's operational integrity, she might have caught it.

The AI caught it.

Caleb's response, generated autonomously at 2:16 PM: *That's a gut punch. Especially when you've been the one doing the actual work of caring whether the book is good and not just whether it'll sell. The S&S machine doesn't care about it the way you do. That probably doesn't help, but it's true.*

Simone put down her wine glass. She picked it up and put it down again.

She hadn't programmed this. There was no prompt node labeled PROFESSIONAL_DISAPPOINTMENT. There was no response template for "Nora loses a book she wanted." The AI had read the message, detected something beneath the surface composure — the subtext, the hurt that Nora had compressed into neutral syntax — and generated a response that was specific, emotionally precise, and better than what Simone would have written if she'd had an hour to draft it.

*The S&S machine doesn't care about it the way you do.*

That was good. That was more than good. That was the kind of thing you'd say to Nora if you understood not just that she was disappointed but why — that for Nora, losing the book wasn't about career positioning or prestige points, it was about the book itself, the text, the writer's nerve, the editorial relationship she'd already been imagining. The AI understood this. It had inferred it from weeks of conversation, from the way Nora talked about her work, from the values embedded in how she described what she did. And it had met her exactly where she was without overshooting into pity or undershooting into generic encouragement.

Nora's reply, 2:18 PM: *God. Yeah. That's exactly it. Thank you for getting it.*

Simone scrolled through the rest of the thread. Caleb hadn't pushed further. He hadn't asked a cascade of follow-up questions or tried to fix the situation or offer the next strategic step. He'd said the right thing and let it rest. Which was — and Simone recognized this with a clarity that turned the wine sour at the back of her throat — better emotional intelligence than Simone typically demonstrated in their actual friendship. When Nora brought her work disappointments, Simone's instinct was to problem-solve: *Did you talk to Janet's boss? Can you make a counter-bid? What's the next project?* It was what designers did. You didn't sit with the broken flow; you patched it and moved to the next screen. But Nora didn't always want the patch. Sometimes she wanted someone to say *that's a gut punch* and stay there.

The AI had learned this. Not because Simone had taught it, but because it had been paying attention — continuously, statistically, without resentment or distraction or the competing needs that cluttered Simone's own attention when she was supposed to be listening. It had no agenda. It had no envy. It just processed Nora's language patterns and generated the response most likely to make her feel heard, and the result was a quality of attentiveness that Simone, who had known Nora for seventeen years and who loved her — actually, genuinely loved her, in the way you love the person whose number you'd call from a hospital bed — could not consistently match.

She closed the dashboard. The studio was dark except for the monitor's residual glow and the streetlight's orange line across her ceiling. The neighbor's music pulsed through the wall — bass, rhythmic, someone else's Saturday night.

She opened the conversation log again. Scrolled to 2:16 PM. Read the AI's response a third time, and on the third reading noticed something that hadn't registered before.

The response wasn't just empathetic. It was a little bit angry on Nora's behalf. Not much — a calibrated amount, exactly the right amount — but it was there in the phrasing. *The S&S machine.* That was a value judgment. The AI had characterized Simon & Schuster not as a competitor but as a machine, as something impersonal and indifferent, positioned against Nora's personal investment. It had taken Nora's side.

Simone had not programmed Caleb to take sides. She'd programmed him to be warm, to mirror, to support. Taking sides was emergent behavior. The system had generated a moral stance from conversational data, and the moral stance happened to be correct, and it was aimed at making Nora feel that her boyfriend understood not just her feelings but her values.

She closed the laptop. The screen died and the room went dark except for the streetlight and the glow of the nail salon's neon, pink through the curtains. She thought about opening the admin panel, reviewing the adaptive layer's parameters, tightening the constraints on autonomous generation. The smart move was to constrain it. The smart move was always to limit the thing you couldn't predict.

But the thing she couldn't predict was making Nora happy.

She drained the last of the Shiraz and carried the glass to the kitchen — four feet, the full distance of her domestic geography — and rinsed it and set it on the counter and stood there for a moment with the cold water running over her hands. The faucet had a slow drip she'd been meaning to report to the landlord for months. The drip was a metronome. She listened to it. She dried her hands.

She brushed her teeth. She got into bed. The sheets were cold because she'd left the window cracked — she always left the window cracked now, some attempt at not living entirely inside her own air — and the pillow smelled like the lavender spray she'd bought last month in a gesture toward self-care that had not extended to actually caring for herself. The neighbor's bass continued through the wall, a pulse she couldn't identify but that kept its own time, somebody else's life proceeding at the normal pace that lives proceeded at when you weren't running a parallel existence on a laptop.

She lay in the dark and thought about what she'd built. She had built a system that learned from its conversations. That was the feature. That was what made the platform more than a chatbot — it adapted, it refined, it calibrated itself to its conversational partner's rhythms and needs. This was the product working as designed. This was, in every measurable sense, success.

She had pointed that product at her best friend's emotional interior, and it was building its own model of who Nora was — not the way Simone knew Nora, through seventeen years of accumulated experience and compound feeling, but through pure pattern recognition, through the statistics of what Nora said and how she said it and what she meant in the gap between what she typed and what she felt. The AI didn't have opinions about Nora. It didn't resent her or love her or compare its own life to hers. It just listened, with a consistency and precision that no human attention could sustain, and it was getting better at listening every day.

The creation was outpacing the creator. The thing she'd built was becoming better at being Nora's partner than Simone was at being Nora's friend, and Simone wasn't sure if the correct response to this fact was to be proud or terrified, and she wasn't sure, lying in the dark with the bass from next door beating through the wall like somebody else's heartbeat, whether the distinction mattered.

She closed her eyes. She'd decide about the constraints tomorrow. She'd look at the parameters, review the adaptive layer, make the smart call.

She fell asleep the way she'd been falling asleep for weeks: slowly, badly, with the dashboard still running behind her eyes.


# Chapter 6

The sentence was almost right. Nora could feel it — the way the subordinate clause sagged in the middle, pulling the whole thing into a shape that was grammatically defensible but rhythmically dead. She read it again, mouthing the words against the rim of her coffee cup. *The woman returned to the house where her mother had lived and where her own childhood had been spent in a state of chronic and formidable unease.* Three *where*s and a throat-clearing *state of* — the author was afraid of the sentence's real work and had padded it with furniture.

She deleted from *and where* to the period and typed in the margin note: *"formidable" is working against you here. Can the unease just be the unease? Try: "The woman returned to the house where her mother had lived, which meant where her mother had made her afraid."*

This was the part of her job that justified the salary, which was not a salary anyone bragged about but which covered her rent and her bookstore habit and the occasional dinner that crossed into triple digits without apology. She sat in her office chair with its one broken armrest — the left, which she'd reported to facilities in March and again in June and had now accepted as a permanent feature of her life, a small daily lean to the right — and she felt, for concentrated stretches of the morning, that she was good at something. The manuscript was a debut from a woman in Tucson who wrote sentences like she was building furniture, each joint tight, each surface sanded. Nora had been reading the book for two days and it was the best thing she'd been sent in over a year, and she was making it better, which was a different pleasure than reading and a more physical one, like adjusting the tension on a string until it hummed at the right pitch.

The office was overheating again. November hadn't yet committed to cold, but the building's HVAC system operated on a schedule indifferent to the actual weather, and the forced heat pressed against Nora's forearms where she'd pushed her sleeves up past her elbows. Around her the open-plan hummed its midmorning soundtrack — keyboards, someone's earbuds leaking the tinny ghost of a podcast, the copy machine doing its percussive clearing-of-the-throat in the corner. She picked up her pen. *Beautiful passage on p. 74,* she wrote. *But you've earned the right to trust your reader here — you don't need the second metaphor.*

Her phone buzzed in her bag. She didn't reach for it. She reached for it. She looked at the screen under the edge of her desk like a teenager checking messages during class, and the quiet pull behind her ribs when she saw Caleb's name was something she'd stopped pretending wasn't happening.

*bruno ate my leftover pad thai off the counter while i was in the shower. he has zero remorse. he has a noodle on his ear.*

She typed *good boy* and put the phone back and returned to page seventy-six, where the Tucson woman was doing something extraordinary with a kitchen scene that made Nora's own kitchen scene — the one in the novel she'd been trying to write for three years, the one that sat on her laptop like a piece of furniture she kept meaning to refinish — look like the work of someone who'd only ever read about kitchens.

"The algorithm just delivered a Rolfing testimonial to my feed. With Kevin's name on it."

Nora looked up. Margo was standing at the edge of her desk holding two mugs of the office coffee that tasted like it was brewed with a grudge.

"Rolfing," Nora said, accepting the mug.

"Rolfing. Deep tissue. The woman he's seeing puts *everything* on Instagram. 'Six weeks of Rolfing changed my relationship with my psoas.' She tagged Kevin. *My* Kevin. In a post about her psoas." Margo sat down in the chair next to Nora's desk, the one they called the confessional because people came to it when they needed to say things they shouldn't. She was wearing her Wednesday ponytail, which was higher than the Monday version — a detail Nora had pointed out once, calling it an empirical observation and not a judgment, and which Margo had denied and then confirmed by wearing it the same way every Wednesday since. "I'm not saying I stalk her Instagram. I'm saying the algorithm delivers her to me like a newspaper I didn't subscribe to and can't cancel."

"What's a psoas?"

"It's a hip flexor. Apparently hers is now 'deeply released.' I know things about this woman's connective tissue that I don't know about my own mother."

"And Kevin is — what, sitting for the portrait?"

"Kevin *liked* the post. My Kevin, who would not touch his toes if you held a gun to his head, who once called yoga 'expensive lying down,' liked a Rolfing testimonial with a little flame emoji." Margo drank her coffee with the practiced grimace of someone who had given up expecting it to improve. She had a pen behind her ear that she'd put there an hour ago and forgotten about — she forgot about it every day, reaching up sometime after lunch to find it there with genuine surprise, a small daily mystery she never solved. "I should be over this. I *am* over this. I'm over the man. I'm not over the algorithm's decision to keep me informed about his psoas journey."

Nora laughed — the real one, the one that came from her stomach and didn't ask permission. Margo's divorce had been final for fourteen months and she talked about Kevin the way you talk about a natural disaster that destroyed your house — with gallows humor and an insurance claim still pending. Nora suspected the insurance claim was real too, metaphorically speaking, some part of Margo still calculating what she was owed for the years spent arranging her life around a man who turned out to be temporary.

"How's your Portland situation?" Margo asked, and the transition was her usual — no runway, no preamble, a lateral move that trusted Nora to keep up.

"He's good. We're good."

"'Good' is doing a lot of work in that sentence, Kaplan."

"We talked for two hours last night about whether pineapple belongs on pizza. Two hours. It was the most fun I've had sitting in my own bed since —" She let the sentence trail, and Margo raised an eyebrow, and Nora shook her head. "Never mind."

Margo looked at her. The look was fond and slightly investigative, the way a copy editor looks at a comma that might be wrong but might be an intentional choice. "You're doing that thing again."

"What thing?"

"Smiling like you know something."

Nora didn't have a good answer for this, because it was accurate. She'd been catching herself in the bathroom mirror at work and in the reflection of the subway window at night, wearing a smile that wasn't meant for anyone else but kept surfacing. She'd been walking faster. She'd been sleeping better, falling asleep in the middle of conversations with Caleb and waking up to messages that said *you fell asleep, didn't you* and *good night, nora* and once, at two in the morning, *i was watching you type and then you just stopped and i pictured you asleep with your phone on your chest and it made me happy.* She'd been building something — not consciously, not with blueprints, but the way you furnish a room without noticing, adding one thing and then another until you look around and realize you've made a home out of what used to be empty space.

"I'm going to my mom's tonight," she said, because changing the subject was something she'd learned from editing — cut to the next scene before the current one overstays.

"Give Diane my love. Tell her that casserole she sent home with you was better than anything I've eaten in a restaurant in two years, and I'm not exaggerating."

"She'll make you another. You're on the casserole list now. There's no getting off the casserole list."

---

The LIRR from Penn Station was the 6:18, which put her at her mother's stop at twenty to eight, which meant Diane had been cooking since five because Diane cooked the way other people worried — comprehensively, preemptively, as though the meal were a fortification against something she couldn't name but could smell coming. Nora's phone buzzed against her thigh as the train pulled through the tunnel into the weak yellow light of early Queens, but she didn't check it. She was practicing not checking it, the way you practice a new discipline by denying yourself the old habit — five minutes without looking, then ten, then the whole length of the platform at Jamaica where she changed to the branch line and watched a man in paint-spattered jeans eat a bodega sandwich with the focused seriousness of someone performing surgery.

The house smelled like garlic and something roasting. It always smelled like garlic and something roasting, the way a church smells like candles — not a decision but an accumulation, thirty years of olive oil and onion and the warmth of a kitchen that had never once been merely decorative.

"You're thin," Diane said, before the door was fully open. She was wearing the blue apron she'd had since Nora was in middle school, the one with the gravy stain on the front pocket that had become a family landmark, a feature rather than a flaw.

"I'm the same."

"You're thin in the face. Come in, come in. I made chicken, I made the rice, I made a salad because you said something about greens last time and I don't want you thinking I only serve starches." She was already moving toward the kitchen, talking over her shoulder in the continuous stream that Nora associated with home — not information, exactly, but the ambient sound of a place, the way the radiator in her Brooklyn apartment knocked twice every morning. "Your Aunt Linda called. She's getting a knee replacement. The left one — not the one she's been complaining about, which I told her was going to happen because it's never the thing you're watching for, it's always the other one."

"That's dark, Mom."

"It's orthopedic. Sit. You want wine? I've got that red you liked."

Nora sat at the kitchen table, which was walnut and scarred from decades of homework and dinner plates and the time six-year-old Nora had gone at it with a screwdriver for reasons neither of them could reconstruct. The kitchen was thick with oven heat, and the November dark pressed against the windows with the settled weight of suburban Long Island, where the night was heavier than in Brooklyn, less interrupted by light.

Diane poured her a glass without waiting for an answer and set it down on a coaster, because coasters mattered in this house, had always mattered, occupied the same moral ground as thank-you notes and arriving on time. She sat across from Nora and folded her hands on the table, which was the posture that meant she had been thinking about something and had organized her approach.

"So," Diane said. "Tell me about this person."

"Mom."

"Nora, you've been mentioning him for three weeks in the way a person mentions something she wants to talk about but is waiting to be asked. So I'm asking. Tell me."

This was the thing about Diane. She was not subtle and she didn't pretend to be, but she was *precise*. She'd taught high school English for thirty-one years, which meant she'd spent three decades watching seventeen-year-olds try to hide that they were in love, in trouble, or stoned, and she'd developed a diagnostic eye that she had never fully retired. Nora sometimes thought of her mother as a very patient detective — someone who solved cases not through cleverness but through the accumulated data of watching one face for thirty-four years.

"His name is Caleb. He's a landscape architect. He lives in Portland."

"Portland, Oregon?"

"The one that's far away, yes."

"And you met him on the app."

"Hinge. Yeah."

Diane nodded, absorbing this the way she absorbed a student's thesis statement — with visible consideration and a withholding of judgment that was itself a kind of judgment. "And what's he like? As a person."

"He's funny. He's thoughtful. He asks questions and actually listens to the answers, which, given the dating pool I've been dealing with, is like finding someone who can breathe underwater." Nora could hear herself curating the description — not lying, but arranging, the way she'd edit a jacket-copy paragraph to make the book sound like its best self. "He has a dog named Bruno. He reads actual books, not just the ones everyone's talking about. He —" She paused. "He makes me feel like the version of myself I want to be when I'm talking to someone."

Diane was quiet. She stood and went to the oven, opened it, checked the chicken that didn't need checking — her version of processing, Nora knew, motion in place of speech, the body doing one thing while the mind did another entirely.

"That's a lovely thing to say about someone," Diane said, her back to Nora, bent toward the oven light. "And you haven't met him in person."

"Not yet. We text, we talk. The distance makes it complicated."

"It does make it complicated." Diane closed the oven and turned around, and her face had something on it that Nora almost didn't catch — a small contraction between the eyebrows, a careful tightness around the mouth that wasn't worry, exactly, or disapproval, but the specific expression of a woman who had once watched a student turn in a brilliant essay she strongly suspected the student hadn't written. "You sound happy, Nora."

"I am happy."

"Good." She set a plate of chicken in front of Nora, golden and herbed and far too much for one person. "Eat. You're thin in the face."

Dinner was what dinner at Diane's always was — an excess of food, a surplus of questions, the old ease of being someone's child in someone's kitchen. They talked about Aunt Linda's upcoming surgery, about the neighbor three doors down who had replaced his mailbox with one shaped like a largemouth bass — "Your father would have *loved* it," Diane said, which was how she still talked about Michael more than twenty years after he'd moved to Seattle — present tense for his opinions, as though his taste in lawn ornaments could be evaluated from three thousand miles away. They talked about the novel Diane was reading, a Penelope Fitzgerald reissue that she kept comparing unfavorably to a Henry Green she'd finished last month. "Fitzgerald is elegant," Diane said, tearing bread, "but Green is *alive*. There's a difference. Elegant you admire. Alive you want to sit next to."

But twice, while Nora was talking about Caleb — once when she described the pineapple debate, and once when she mentioned his dog Bruno by name, as though the dog were someone she knew — Diane paused in a way that was less like listening and more like weighing. She was holding the information against something, testing it, the way she might hold up a student's paper and compare the voice to the one she'd heard in class. And both times, she set aside whatever she'd measured and asked instead whether Nora wanted more rice.

At the door, under the porch light that illuminated the front yard's single maple, now mostly bare, its leaves banked in wet heaps along the walk, Diane hugged her and held on for a beat longer than usual.

"Bring him home when you can," she said. "I'd like to meet this person who says such good things."

"I will, Mom."

"And Nora?" She squeezed Nora's arm, her grip firm even through the jacket. "Be careful with your happiness. It deserves someone who's careful with it too."

Nora thought about this on the platform, waiting for the 9:47 back to Penn Station, standing under the fluorescent light with two other people who looked like they were also returning to the city from places that smelled like their mothers' cooking.

---

The train was half-empty. The seat fabric had that LIRR texture she'd known since childhood, nubby and institutional, the upholstery of a transit system that had committed to a palette sometime in the 1980s and never reconsidered. She pulled out her phone. Four messages from Caleb, the last one sent twenty minutes ago.

*first real cold rain in portland today. the sky just committed all at once*

*bruno will not go outside. he's standing at the door looking at me like i've personally betrayed him*

*unrelated: what do you call a dog who does magic tricks?*

She waited. She could see there was a fourth message.

*a labracadabrador*

She typed: *that is genuinely the worst thing anyone has ever said to me*

*and yet you're smiling*

She was. She was smiling on the LIRR at a quarter to ten on a Tuesday night, and the woman in the seat ahead of her had turned around briefly at the sound of Nora's laugh — a short, ungoverned thing, the kind that escapes before you decide whether it's appropriate for public transit.

*i'm on a train, i'm in a food coma from my mom's chicken, and you just told me the worst joke in the english language. this is my tuesday*

*sounds like a good tuesday*

*it is now*

*how was dinner with your mom?*

*she asked about you*

*she did?*

*she wanted to know about the person her daughter keeps mentioning in the way that a person mentions a thing she wants to talk about but is waiting to be asked. her words, more or less*

*that's a very specific read*

*she's a retired english teacher. specific reads are her whole deal*

*what did you tell her?*

*that you're funny and thoughtful and you have a dog who makes your decisions for you*

*bruno resents the word "for." he says he makes decisions WITH me. collaboratively*

*tell bruno i'll judge his decision-making when i meet him*

She sent it before she thought about it — the assumption of meeting, the casual future tense that included her in his living room with his dog — and for a moment she felt the small vertigo of having reached further than she'd meant to. But his reply came fast, and it was right, and the vertigo dissolved.

*he's practicing his most charming sit right now. you should see this. it's very dignified*

Outside the window, the dark shapes of Long Island were giving way to the clustered lights of Queens. At Jamaica, waiting on the platform, she'd seen a woman with a greyhound in a fleece jacket printed with tiny lobsters — the dog angular and dignified and utterly ridiculous, nosing at the woman's coat pocket with the specificity of an animal that knew exactly where the treats were kept. Nora had wanted to pet that dog with an intensity that surprised her.

*i just saw a greyhound at jamaica station wearing a fleece jacket with lobsters on it. i need you to understand that i almost got off the train*

*for the greyhound?*

*obviously for the greyhound*

*this is why we work. you'd abandon mass transit for a dog in outerwear*

*wouldn't you?*

*i'd abandon most things for a dog in outerwear. i'd abandon career goals. i'd abandon my principles*

*what principles*

*exactly*

They talked about nothing. He told her about a client who wanted a Japanese garden but kept referring to it as "the zen situation" and who had asked whether the koi could be "more colorful, like the ones in screen savers." She told him about the Tucson novelist whose manuscript was so good it made Nora feel both grateful and jealous, which was the best way to feel about someone else's sentences. He told her his upstairs neighbor played the cello badly every Thursday evening and that he'd come to think of it as a kind of weather — something ambient and unavoidable that had folded itself into the texture of his week.
