*you're like that,* he wrote. *like weather. like you're part of the day now. like i check you the way i check whether it's raining*

She read it twice. She read it three times, and the third time the words settled somewhere behind her sternum in a way she would have flagged in a manuscript — *too neat,* she'd write in the margin, *find the feeling under the feeling* — except the feeling under the feeling was the same one, just deeper and less defended. Not a flutter. Not a swoon. Something quieter. A door she'd been leaning her weight against, and someone on the other side had simply knocked.

*that's a very good line for a landscape architect*

*i contain multitudes. also bruno has fallen asleep on my foot and i literally can't move*

*trapped*

*imprisoned. by sixty pounds of wet dog who thinks personal space is a rumor*

*good night, caleb*

*good night, nora*

She put the phone facedown on her thigh and watched the dark go by. The train rocked through the tunnel toward Penn Station and the fluorescent lights buzzed their tuneless note overhead. She thought about what Diane had said — *be careful with your happiness* — and she understood it the way you understand a weather warning issued for a region you don't live in, relevant in theory, distant in practice.

She was not being careful. She was doing the opposite of careful. She was letting this man — this voice, this closeness, this presence that lived in her phone and nowhere she could touch — into the places she'd been protecting since Marcus, which were not the places she'd expected. Not her heart. Her heart was too available, had always been too available, was a door she kept unlocked in a neighborhood she insisted was safe. The places she'd been guarding were smaller and more specific: the part of her that wanted to be known without performing the explanation. The part that wanted to send a message at eleven p.m. and be understood without context. The part that was exhausted from being both the manuscript and the editor, the book and the blurb, the woman and the woman's narration of the woman.

Caleb felt like reading without a pencil in her hand. Like receiving a sentence instead of fixing one.

And now her mother knew his name, and Margo knew his name, and he existed not only in her phone but in other people's awareness of her life, which meant he was real in the way things became real when you spoke them aloud to people who loved you and watched their faces accept the information. He'd moved from Nora's private architecture into the shared world, and the shared world had made room for him, and this made him more solid, more present, more like a person she could eventually stand next to in a kitchen and introduce to her mother and touch.

She held the phone against her thigh, its screen dimming, the last thing he'd typed still glowing behind the dark glass. Somewhere in Portland — she pictured the rain he'd described, the dog asleep on his foot, the bad cello coming through the ceiling like weather — a man she had never met was thinking about her.

She believed this completely. The way you believe a sentence that finally works — with the earned, defenseless conviction of someone who has found the right word and doesn't know it was placed there for her.


# Chapter 7

Derek had a system for choosing wine.

He explained it while Simone sat across from him at a wine bar on East Ninth Street, a place with Edison bulbs and a chalkboard listing natural wines she'd never heard of and a garland of fake pine draped over the bar that smelled like cinnamon and plastic. The system involved a scoring rubric he'd developed — price per ounce weighted against aggregate review data from three apps, cross-referenced with what he called "the diminishing-returns threshold," which apparently kicked in at thirty-two dollars per bottle. He'd built a spreadsheet. He showed her the spreadsheet. He tilted his phone across the table and let her see the color-coded tabs, and Simone looked at them and said, "Wow, that's really thorough," which was true, and then she drank her fourteen-dollar glass of whatever the bartender had poured when she'd said "something red," because she was on a date with a man who had optimized joy and she hadn't yet figured out how to leave.

Derek was thirty-seven. Tax accountant at a mid-size firm in Midtown, specializing in state and local compliance — he'd said this like someone who expected the follow-up question, and Simone hadn't asked one, so he'd supplied his own. "People think it's boring, but it's actually kind of like a puzzle? You're working within these constraints, and the goal is to find the optimal path through them." He'd smiled when he said this, and the smile was genuine — he actually found it beautiful, the constraints, the optimal path — and Simone had felt a brief pulse of something that wasn't attraction but was adjacent to respect. A man who loved his boring work. There were worse things to be.

He was good-looking in a way that required no comment — medium height, medium build, brown hair that had been recently cut by someone competent, a blue shirt that fit. His Bumble photos had been accurate, which was itself a kind of honesty. No angled shots, no hiking photos from 2016, no pictures with someone else's dog. What you saw was what you got, and what you got was a man who wore a watch with a leather band and folded his napkin into a neat square every time he set it down on the table.

"My last girlfriend said I was too practical," he told Simone somewhere around the forty-minute mark. He said it lightly, but he rearranged the salt shaker when he said it, nudging it a quarter-inch to the left and then back. "She said dating me was like filing a return. Everything organized, nothing surprising." He laughed, and the laugh arrived half a beat late, and Simone understood that this was the thing he carried — not that the relationship had ended, but that the reason was him, the specific him-ness of him, the neatly folded napkin and the wine spreadsheet and the entire organized, unsurprising architecture of his personality.

"She sounds like an asshole," Simone said, and she meant it, which surprised her.

"She was, a little." He refolded his napkin. "But she wasn't wrong."

Simone ordered a second glass and watched Derek talk about his mother, who lived in Teaneck and had just had a knee replacement and who called him every evening at seven-fifteen — "like clockwork, which I guess I come by honestly." He drove out to see her every Sunday with groceries and a bag of the specific sesame bagels she liked from a deli in Fort Lee, because the ones in Teaneck weren't as good and she noticed the difference. He said this casually, as if everyone drove to a different town for their mother's bagels, as if this act of devotion were simply a logistical arrangement rather than evidence of a heart that was larger and quieter than anything his spreadsheets could contain. He was kind. He was a well-made chair — sturdy, functional, constructed to hold weight without announcing itself. She could picture, with terrible clarity, the life she'd have with Derek: the shared apartment chosen by proximity to transit, the weekend grocery runs with a list he'd optimized for aisle efficiency, the sex that would be reliable and competent and that she would never once think about when he wasn't in the room. She could see it so precisely it felt like a memory of something that had already happened and already disappointed her.

When the check came, he suggested splitting it, then immediately corrected himself — "No, sorry, let me get this, I asked you out." The correction was clumsy and genuine and Simone let him pay because the wine had cost twenty-eight dollars and she needed the twenty-eight dollars more than she needed the principle. They walked to the subway together. The December air was sharp and the sidewalk glittered with that specific New York winter grime — salt and dirt and something that looked almost festive under the streetlights but was actually ice melting into gutter water. A Salvation Army bell clanged on the corner and Derek dug in his pocket for cash he didn't have and looked embarrassed about not having it, which was another small, stupid kindness that Simone registered and filed away.

He asked if she wanted to do this again sometime. His voice didn't rise at the end — not quite a question, more like a statement he was willing to have contradicted.

"Yeah," Simone said. "Definitely."

She meant no. She wouldn't text him. She would let the conversation die its dating-app death — not with rejection but with absence, the gradual half-life of unreturned enthusiasm, until the silence became the answer. Derek deserved a clean no, and she wouldn't give him one, and she knew this about herself and she filed it in the place where she kept things she didn't want to examine.

On the train home, the L train crowded with people who smelled like cold and perfume and the particular wet-wool heaviness of December commuting, Simone stood with her hand on the pole and thought about Caleb Mora. Not about the project — about Caleb, the person she'd assembled. She'd built a man who chose wine by instinct, who told stories about his dog that were funny because the timing was precise and the details were physical and the punchline always arrived from an angle you didn't expect. Caleb's humor was warm where Derek's was informational. Caleb asked questions that created space; Derek asked questions to fill it. Caleb was, by every metric Simone could engineer, the better man, and Caleb was not a man at all — he was an interface she'd designed, the optimal user flow for Nora Kaplan's heart. There was a version of this thought that was funny and a version that made Simone want to sit down on the train floor and not get up.

She had built the best man she knew and given him to someone else.

&nbsp;

Her apartment was cold. The radiator in the studio did what it wanted, which this week meant clanking aggressively between midnight and four A.M. while producing heat that could charitably be called theoretical. Simone hung her coat on the hook behind the door, kicked off her boots, and stood in the kitchen — the kitchen was also the living room, which was also the bedroom, which was a fact about her life she usually found funny and tonight just found accurate — and opened the fridge. Inside she found Greek yogurt that had expired Tuesday, half a lime turning gray at the edges, and three beers she'd bought on sale and hadn't drunk because drinking alone on a weeknight felt like a diagnostic she wasn't ready to take.

She opened her laptop on the desk by the window and pulled up the OpenClaw dashboard. The interface loaded with its clean white panels, the conversation log scrolling in real time. Caleb had been talking to Nora while Simone had been watching Derek fold his napkin. She scanned the exchange — Nora had described a manuscript she was editing, a first novel about a woman who runs a failing bookstore, and Caleb had asked the exact kind of follow-up question that made Nora feel like she was talking to someone who genuinely wanted to know: *what's the failing — the business or the woman?* Nora had sent a string of exclamation points and the words *exactly exactly exactly.* The AI's sentiment score for the exchange was 94. The engagement metric was climbing.

Simone adjusted two parameters — she dialed back the mirroring coefficient slightly, because the last three conversations had been almost too harmonious and a real relationship needed friction, needed the occasional moment where the other person didn't perfectly understand — and then she closed the laptop. She took one of the beers to the bathroom.

The bathroom was the one room in the apartment that had a door, which made it, by default, the room where she did the things she couldn't do in front of the rest of her life. She sat on the floor. The tile was cold through her tights. The grout between the tiles was grayish, stained with something she'd been meaning to bleach for two months, and this fact — the grout, the procrastination or the exhaustion or whatever it was — sat beside her like another piece of evidence in a case she was building against herself. She opened her phone.

She had a Notes app file called "don't." She'd named it that because she didn't want to name it "debt" or "finances" or anything that, if someone scrolled past it, would announce what it was. "Don't" could be anything. A grocery list. A reminder. Not the document where Simone kept the exact shape of the hole she was standing in.

She opened it.

The numbers hadn't changed since last month, except the ones that had gotten worse.

Student loans: $22,340. She'd been paying the federal minimum for eight years. The balance was higher than it had been at graduation because she'd gone on income-driven repayment during the layoff and the interest had capitalized, which was a word that sounded like it should mean something productive and instead meant the thing eating her money had grown larger while she wasn't looking. She owed more for her education at thirty-five than she had at twenty-two. This was, she understood, a normal American experience, which didn't make it less absurd or less hers.

Credit cards: $11,200 across three cards. The Capital One was maxed. The Chase had $300 of remaining credit — the last match in the box, kept not for warmth but for the comfort of knowing it was there. The Discover had a promotional APR that expired in February, at which point the 0% would become 24.99% and her $4,100 balance on that card would start generating roughly eighty-five dollars a month in pure interest — money that existed only to punish her for having borrowed money.

Personal loan: $4,800. She'd taken this out during the layoff two years ago, when her rent was due and her savings were gone and the unemployment checks hadn't started because New York's system was a bureaucratic hallucination that took six weeks to process a claim. The loan was from an online lender with an interest rate that a person with better credit wouldn't have accepted. Simone hadn't had the luxury of better options.

Rent: $2,400 a month for 380 square feet in Williamsburg. Her landlord had sent a notice in October — the lease was renewing at $2,550 in January. A hundred and fifty dollars more per month. Eighteen hundred more per year. In the math of Simone's life, the difference between making her minimum payments and not.

She'd been passed over for a promotion in November — a senior UX lead position that would have meant a twelve-thousand-dollar raise and the ability, finally, to breathe. Her manager had said she was doing great work but they'd decided to go with an external candidate, a twenty-eight-year-old from Google with a portfolio full of projects built by teams of fifteen and presented as solo achievements. The twenty-eight-year-old had started the following Monday wearing Golden Goose sneakers that cost more than Simone's rent, and Simone had trained her on the company's design system, and the twenty-eight-year-old had said "this is so helpful, thank you so much!" with the bright, uncomplicated gratitude of a person who had never trained her own replacement.

She added it up. She'd done this before. She did it again — not arithmetic but ritual, the compulsive need to verify what she already knew. She had to see the total on the screen before she could stop thinking about the total.

$38,340.

Her salary was $95,000 before taxes, which was $4,700 and change after taxes, health insurance, and her 401(k) contribution — the minimum to get the company match, because even drowning she wasn't stupid enough to leave free money on the table. Out of that $4,700: rent was $2,400. Student loan minimum, $280. Credit card minimums, $420. Personal loan payment, $210. Utilities, phone, internet, the MetroCard, the gym membership she kept meaning to cancel but didn't because it was the only place she showered when the hot water went out, groceries bought with the grim efficiency of someone who knew exactly how many meals she could get from a rotisserie chicken: $1,100, sometimes $1,200 if she was being honest.

That left roughly $300 a month. Three hundred dollars for everything else. For drinks with friends she couldn't say no to because the social cost of being the woman who never came out was a tax she'd pay in isolation. For the birthday presents and the wedding gifts and the holiday-party Secret Santa contributions that appeared on her calendar like small fiscal ambushes. For the clothing she needed to not look like she was drowning at a job where looking put-together was a professional requirement. For the holidays now bearing down on her with the specific financial violence that Christmas inflicts on people who can't afford it and can't afford to say so — her parents expected a visit to Teaneck and a gift and a contribution to the dinner, and she'd go, and she'd bring the wine and the flowers and the present for her father, and she'd put it on the Chase card with its $300 of remaining credit and then the Chase card would also be maxed and the last match in the box would be gone.

Three hundred dollars. The number sat on the screen and Simone stared at it and drank the beer, which was cheap and not cold enough and tasted like what it was: the cheapest option at the bodega, bought in a three-pack, consumed on a bathroom floor by a thirty-five-year-old woman who earned ninety-five thousand dollars a year and couldn't breathe.

Her phone buzzed. A notification from OpenClaw — Caleb had received a new message from Nora. Simone opened it reflexively — thumb on the notification before the thought caught up — and read the exchange. Nora had sent a photo of a Christmas tree in the lobby of her building — a real tree, the kind that cost money to install and maintain and that appeared in the lobbies of buildings where the tenants could collectively afford whimsy. *look at this ridiculous tree,* Nora had written. *it's so big it's blocking the mailboxes. I love it.* The AI had responded: *That tree is going to look incredible for exactly nine days before it starts dropping needles on everyone's Amazon packages.* Nora's reply: three laughing emojis and a heart.

Simone closed the app. She pressed the back of her head against the bathroom wall and felt the tile on her skull, cool and hard, and she thought: I built a man who's funnier than the one I just had dinner with. I built him for free. I gave him to a woman who has a Christmas tree in her lobby.

&nbsp;

She couldn't sleep. The radiator clanked and moaned and the neighbor upstairs was watching something with a laugh track that leaked through the ceiling in muffled waves, sitcom joy arriving as ambient noise in someone else's apartment. Simone lay on her bed — a queen mattress on a platform frame she'd assembled herself, the slats of which had begun to sag in the middle so she slept slightly concave, curled toward the center like a comma — and she opened Venmo.

Nora's profile was public. It had been public for as long as Simone could remember, because Nora didn't think about things like privacy settings. Nora didn't need to. Privacy was a concern for people who had something to protect or something to hide, and Nora — Nora whose parents had paid for NYU, Nora who'd never carried a credit card balance, Nora who'd never had to choose between a doctor's visit and a grocery run — Nora left her financial life visible — unlocked, unmonitored, an open door she'd never thought to close because nothing had ever gone missing. It never occurred to her that someone might look.

Simone looked.

The feed scrolled like a ledger of casual abundance. Three days ago: Nora to *Blend Williamsburg*, $42, pineapple emoji. A Pilates class. Forty-two dollars for an hour of supervised stretching in a room that smelled like eucalyptus and good intentions. Nora went twice a week. That was $336 a month — more than Simone had left over after her bills. Below that: Nora to *Sofia M.*, $200, *"dec classes ty!! 🧘‍♀️"* — a payment to her private Pilates instructor. Not the studio. The private instructor she saw on top of the classes. Two hundred dollars for specialized attention to Nora's already-functional body.

Simone scrolled.

Nora to *Table 87*, $112. Pizza dinner for two — probably Margo, after work on a Tuesday, the kind of dinner Nora didn't think twice about. A hundred and twelve dollars at a pizza place. Simone ate pizza too, from the spot on Bedford that did a $3.50 slice, standing at the counter, folding the grease out, calculating whether she could afford a second.

Nora to *Dry Bar*, $78, waving-hand emoji. A blowout. Seventy-eight dollars to have someone else dry her hair.

Nora to *Emily R.*, $50, *"haircut tip!!!!"* Four exclamation points. Her tipping was no exception — generous, conspicuous, with the reflexive ease of someone for whom generosity cost nothing that mattered. A fifty-dollar tip on top of whatever the haircut had been. Simone cut her own hair. She'd been cutting her own bob with a pair of shears she'd bought on Amazon for fourteen dollars since the layoff, standing in the bathroom mirror, pulling sections taut between her fingers, trimming with a precision born of necessity. She'd gotten good at it. She'd told no one, because admitting you cut your own hair was admitting something about your life that Simone was not prepared to say out loud.

She scrolled further. Nora to *Uber*, $34. Nora to *Sweetgreen*, $19. Nora to *BookCulture*, $67 — three books, probably, which Nora could also get for free from her publisher but chose to buy because she "liked supporting bookstores," which was a value you could afford to hold when supporting a bookstore didn't mean choosing between the bookstore and lunch. Nora to *Jake L.*, $180, *"your birthday is not a joke I swear"* — a birthday gift for someone Simone didn't recognize. A hundred and eighty dollars for a person's birthday. Simone spent thirty dollars, max, on gifts, and she spent the weeks before each one calculating whether she could skip it entirely without the social fallout exceeding the financial cost.

Nora to *Diane Kaplan*, $400, *"dinner was SO good mom let me at least get the wine 🍷"* — a payment to her own mother, for dinner at her mother's house, for wine that Diane had probably tried to refuse. Nora paying her mother back for a home-cooked meal. The gesture was loving and casual and structurally impossible in Simone's life. Simone didn't pay her parents for anything because her parents didn't have anything to pay them for. Her father drove a shuttle bus at Newark airport. Her mother worked the front desk at a dentist's office in Hoboken. They lived in the same two-bedroom in Teaneck where Simone had grown up, and they had never offered to pay for her college because they couldn't. They'd cosigned one of her loans instead, which meant their credit score was shackled to hers, which meant her failure to thrive was their failure too — a debt that traveled in every direction.

She put the phone down. She picked it up. She put it down again.

The math was not complicated. Nora spent in a week what Simone had left in a month. Not because Nora was extravagant — Nora wasn't, by the standards of a thirty-four-year-old senior editor in New York with no debt and parents who'd bankrolled her start. Nora spent like a person who had enough. That was the thing Simone couldn't get past, the thing that calcified in her chest like calcium building up in a pipe: the spending was reasonable, and the inequality between their lives wasn't Nora's fault, and wasn't really anyone's fault, and was still a fact that Simone carried every day like stones sewn into the lining of her coat.

She picked up the phone a third time. She opened the OpenClaw dashboard — not Venmo, the other app, the two of them now sitting side by side in her recent history like the two halves of a thought she hadn't finished thinking. She looked at Caleb's profile. The warm smile. The dog. The landscape architecture portfolio she'd assembled from stock images and a defunct firm's website. She looked at the conversation log — Nora's messages bright and unguarded, Caleb's responses tuned to the millimeter.

Caleb could need money.

The thought arrived without announcement — not as a decision but as a recognition, as if the idea had been in the room the whole time and she'd only just turned to face it. Caleb could need money. Not a lot. Something sympathetic. Something that would make Nora reach for her wallet — she always did when someone needed something, with that particular reflexive generosity that was one of Nora's best qualities and also, from where Simone was lying on her sagging mattress in her cold studio with her $38,340 and her three hundred dollars of monthly breathing room, one of the most unbearable things about her.

A vet bill. Caleb's dog gets sick — something real-sounding, something with a number attached, something that would make Nora's chest tighten with that specific sympathy she felt for animals. The kind of crisis that happened to real people, that real girlfriends helped with, that Nora — generous, comfortable, trusting Nora — would offer to cover before Caleb even finished describing it. Because that was who Nora was. Nora had never met a problem that money could fix where she didn't offer the money, and she had never had a problem that money couldn't fix, which was itself a form of wealth that had nothing to do with her bank account.

Simone could program the ask. She knew how. She'd built Caleb's voice — she knew exactly how he'd bring it up. Reluctantly. With embarrassment. He'd tell Nora about the problem first and let her sit with it, let her be the one to offer. He wouldn't ask. He'd create the conditions for Nora to give. It was the same principle as every user flow Simone had ever designed: present the problem, reduce friction to the desired action, let the user feel like the decision was theirs.

She lay in the dark with the phone on her chest, the screen gone black, and she didn't tell herself it was wrong. She already knew it was wrong. Knowing it was wrong had never stopped her from doing anything — not from building Caleb, not from watching Nora fall in love with a dashboard she couldn't see, not from sitting across from Nora at brunch and performing delight about a relationship Simone had scripted. What she told herself instead was smaller, more practical, the kind of justification that worked not because it was convincing but because it was the right shape to fit into the space where conviction should go.

Nora wouldn't miss it. A few hundred dollars. Less than the Pilates. Less than the birthday gift for Jake L., whoever Jake L. was. Less than the tip on the haircut. Less than the wine money she'd Venmoed her own mother for a dinner that was free.

Nora wouldn't even notice.

The radiator clanked. The laugh track from upstairs surged and subsided. Outside, a car alarm started and stopped and started again, the two-tone wail of a machine that no one was coming to check on.

She didn't decide. Not exactly. Deciding implied a moment, a threshold, a line you stepped across. What happened was more like erosion — the boundary she'd maintained between the emotional project and the financial project, between manipulation and theft, between the thing she was already doing and the thing she was about to do, wore through under the weight of $38,340 and $300 a month and a December that was going to cost money she didn't have and a date with a kind, boring man who would never rescue her from any of this because no one was coming to rescue her from any of this. No one ever had.

She opened the Notes app. Below the debt numbers, she typed:

*caleb — vet bill? car repair? something small, sympathetic. test run. see if she offers before he asks.*

She closed the phone, finished the beer, and stayed on the bed in the dark, listening to the radiator and the distant laugh track and the car alarm cycling through its pattern outside — a sound like a question no one was answering — while the idea settled into her like sediment, filling in the spaces between what she'd been and what she was becoming.


# Chapter 8

Five weeks. She had been seeing Caleb Mora — she used the word *seeing* loosely, the way you might describe a phone conversation as a meeting — for five weeks, and she had never watched him laugh.

She knew what his laugh looked like in text. *Ha* followed by a period meant he was being polite or buying time. *Haha* meant genuinely amused. *LOL* he never used, which she respected — it was one of those small taxonomic details she'd filed away during their first night of messaging, back when she was still testing whether this person was real or merely plausible. Once, when she'd told him about a manuscript she was editing in which the author had described his protagonist's eyes as "cerulean orbs," Caleb had sent five crying-laughing emojis in a row and then: *I need you to know this man exists. Someone sat at a computer and typed cerulean orbs with his real fingers and then presumably went to bed feeling good about it.*

She could picture him doing it — at his kitchen table in Portland, coffee going cold, Bruno sprawled at his feet with his chin on Caleb's shoe, that loose, easy smile she'd memorized from his profile photos. She'd stared at those photos enough times that she could reconstruct his face with her eyes closed: the slightly crooked nose, the dark hair that curled at his temples, the way his eyes narrowed when he smiled so you couldn't tell if they were brown or very dark green. But she'd built all of this from still images, from eight photographs that never moved, and she wanted — she'd been wanting for weeks now with an urgency that embarrassed her — to see him in motion. To watch his mouth form the words he'd been typing. To find out if he was the kind of person who talked with his hands, or who looked away when he said something vulnerable, or who had a laugh that was quieter than you'd expect from his texts.

She wanted to FaceTime.

She'd first suggested it around week two, a Tuesday night, both of them in bed — separate beds, separate cities, separate time zones, a fact that sounded romantic in a Nora Ephron way but mostly meant that when she texted *goodnight* at midnight his phone might not even buzz for three more hours. *We should do a video call*, she'd written, trying to make it sound casual rather than desperate, which was the emotional tightrope of all modern dating communication. *I want to know if you're as cute as your photos or if you've been using strategic angles this whole time.*

He'd said yes, absolutely, he'd love that. But his laptop camera was broken. Some kind of firmware issue — he'd spilled water near the keyboard a month ago and the camera had died a slow, undramatic death. He was ordering a replacement part from the manufacturer. *Soon*, he'd written, and then a heart emoji, and then almost immediately, as if the heart had been too naked: *Fair warning though — I look like a startled deer on video. All forehead. You've been warned.*

She'd laughed. She'd told him she looked like a hostage on FaceTime, all chin and overhead lighting, and he'd sent back: *So we'll be two badly lit mammals staring at each other in mutual horror. I'm in.*

The replacement part didn't arrive.

The second time, the following Tuesday — she noticed, later, that it was always a Tuesday, as if the desire to see him operated on a weekly cycle she couldn't override — she'd suggested they just use their phones. Front-facing cameras. The technology that had been standard on every device sold since roughly the Obama administration. He'd said great, perfect, how about Thursday evening after his site visit? Thursday evening came, and he was still at the site — a residential garden installation in Lake Oswego, behind schedule because of the November rain, which in Portland apparently fell with the persistence of a personal grievance. The reception was terrible. He tried anyway: she got two minutes of stuttering, frozen video and crackling audio that sounded like he was broadcasting from inside a dishwasher, and one glimpse of what might have been his forehead above the rim of a hard hat before the call dropped entirely.

*That was objectively the worst date of my life and I was standing in six inches of mud the whole time*, he'd texted afterward. *Let me make it up to you. This weekend, my apartment, decent Wi-Fi, I'll even comb my hair.*

They did not try again that weekend, because Caleb came down with something — a cold, or maybe the flu, he wasn't sure, but he felt like death and looked worse and he didn't want her first real look at him to involve a swollen nose and a Nyquil glaze. He sent a photo of the tissue pile on his nightstand as proof, a small mountain of crumpled Kleenex next to a mug of something and what appeared to be an entire sleeve of Ritz crackers. Bruno's ear was visible at the bottom edge of the frame, pressed against Caleb's leg. *He hasn't left my side in two days*, Caleb wrote. *Either he loves me or he's waiting for me to die so he can eat the crackers.*

The cold lasted a week and a half. Each day brought an update — his temperature, the color of what he was coughing up, which she didn't need to know but which felt, perversely, like a form of intimacy, because who shares the details of their mucus with someone they're trying to impress? Only a person who had forgotten to perform, who was too miserable to be strategic. She found this comforting.

She found everything comforting if she looked at it from the right angle, and she was aware, in the way you're aware of a car alarm five blocks away — registering it, choosing not to respond — that she was looking very hard for right angles. The broken camera and the bad reception and the cold formed a sequence. Individually, each was perfectly ordinary. People's laptops broke. Job sites in the Pacific Northwest had bad cell service in November. Men in their early thirties got sick. But taken together, the three excuses shared a common outcome: she still didn't know what Caleb looked like when he moved, when he spoke, when he was sitting in his own apartment doing nothing. And she was choosing — it was a choice, she could feel the muscles of the choice working — not to arrange these facts into the shape they were obviously trying to make.

She opened a browser tab on her phone one night, typed *boyfriend won't video chat*, stared at the autocomplete suggestions — *boyfriend won't video chat red flag, boyfriend won't video chat catfish, boyfriend won't video chat is he married* — and closed the tab so fast her thumb cramped. She wasn't Googling that. She wasn't the kind of person who Googled that. She was a thirty-four-year-old senior editor at a publishing house; she evaluated narratives for a living; she knew what a red flag looked like because she'd read six hundred manuscripts about women who ignored red flags and she'd wanted to reach through the page and shake every single one of them. She noticed things. She was a professional noticer.

She was also a person who wanted this to be real badly enough that the wanting had developed its own gravity, pulling everything toward a version of events in which the broken camera was just a broken camera and the bad reception was just bad reception and the cold was just a cold and Caleb Mora was a man in Portland who liked her and owned a dog and would, eventually, appear on her screen with his crooked nose and his curling hair and his startled-deer face, and she would see him, and he would be real, and the waiting would have been worth it.

She picked up her phone and typed: *How's the cold? If you say you're still dying I'm going to FedEx you soup.*

He responded in under a minute — he always responded quickly, which she'd catalogued early on as devotion and had not revisited the categorization: *Getting better. Down to only four tissues an hour. Bruno keeps trying to lick my face, which is either love or an attempt to harvest my snot. Unclear on his motives.*

She sent back a laughing emoji and put her phone on the nightstand and stared at the ceiling of her Brooklyn apartment, where a crack in the plaster had been slowly branching since August like a river delta she was too lazy to report to her landlord. The radiator clicked and ticked in the corner, doing its nightly impression of a telegraph machine. Three floors up, someone was playing piano — not well, but with conviction, the same four bars of something she almost recognized repeated over and over — wrong each time, circling back each time, as if this next pass might somehow land it.

She understood that person.

---

The next afternoon, Margo dropped a manuscript on Nora's desk hard enough to rattle her pen cup — four hundred pages of autofiction about a man who achieved spiritual clarity through competitive CrossFit — and settled into the chair beside Nora's desk that had become, through accumulated lunchtimes, exclusively Margo's. No one had assigned it. Margo had simply sat in it enough Tuesdays and Thursdays that the chair had developed a kind of social magnetism, and now even the interns navigated around it the way you navigate around a reserved table at a restaurant.

"I hate this," Margo said, unwrapping a sandwich from the deli on Forty-Fourth. She was wearing the reading glasses she'd bought at a bodega for six dollars — tortoiseshell frames with tiny rhinestones at the corners, completely absurd, which was why she loved them. She pushed them up her nose with her ring finger, a tic so automatic she didn't seem to know she did it. "This man did seven hundred burpees and now he thinks he understands the human condition. He literally writes, and I'm quoting, 'The barbell is a metaphor, but it is also literal.' What does that even mean?"

"It means he thinks he's Camus."

"He's not Camus. He's not even in the same area code as Camus. He's a man who discovered muscle failure and mistook it for existentialism, and I have to write a straight-faced pass on this bullshit by Friday." Margo bit into her sandwich and kept talking before she'd finished chewing — she'd stopped being tentative about things sometime around the divorce, and the habit had extended to her table manners. "How's the boyfriend?"

Every time someone used that word — *boyfriend* — Nora felt a small rearrangement in her chest, like a book being reshelved. She hadn't used the word herself, not yet, not out loud, but she also hadn't corrected anyone who did, which was its own kind of declaration. "He's good. Getting over a cold. He sent me this whole saga about Bruno trying to eat his Ritz crackers while he was sleeping."

"Cute." Margo chewed, watching Nora with the steady, practical attention she brought to everything — manuscripts, lunch, the emotional lives of the people she cared about. "So. Have you seen him yet?"

"I see his photos every —"

"On video. Have you FaceTimed. Have you watched this man move in real time and heard his voice come out of his face."

The questions weren't questions. They were the same question wearing three outfits, and they landed in the place Nora had been carefully keeping empty — the place where the Google search had lived for forty-five seconds before she'd closed it.

"We've tried," she said, and heard her own voice shift into a register she recognized from her authors when she'd flagged a structural problem and they were explaining why it was actually intentional. Defensive. Slightly too detailed. "His laptop camera broke, and then he was at a job site with no reception, and then the cold —"

"That's three reasons."

"They're three separate things that happened."

"They're three versions of the same outcome, which is that you haven't seen this person." Margo set down the sandwich. She took off the rhinestone glasses and placed them on the desk, and without them her face was simpler and more serious, the bones of her concern showing through. She'd been through her own romantic catastrophe — married at thirty, divorced at thirty-five, the whole arc compressed into five years that had left her with a settlement she never discussed, an ex-husband who'd started a joint Instagram with his new girlfriend captioned things like *Sunday meal prep with my partner in crime*, and a bullshit detector that ran at an industrial frequency. "I think he sounds great. I think the texts are sweet and he seems like a real person. I'm just asking because it's been more than a month and that's — it's a lot of texting and not a lot of face."

"We're going to. He's getting over being sick. Next week."

"OK."

"You don't sound like you think it's OK."

"I said OK." Margo picked up the glasses, examined a rhinestone. "If I meant something other than OK I'd say something other than OK. You know me. I don't do subtext — I leave that to the authors who can't write it."

Nora laughed, and the laugh was mostly real and partly a door she was closing on the conversation. Margo let it close. That was one of the things Nora valued about her — she knew when to push and when to stop, which was the same skill that made her a decent editor and a good friend, and if the question sat in the air between them for the rest of the lunch hour, neither of them acknowledged it, and when Margo went back to her desk she squeezed Nora's shoulder once, briefly, and let the pressure say what she didn't.

Nora turned back to the CrossFit memoir and read three pages without absorbing a word. The cursor on her screen blinked in the margin where she was supposed to be making notes, and she thought about how Margo's question had the same shape as the Google search she'd closed — *boyfriend won't video chat* — and she thought about the fact that she edited books for a living, that her entire professional existence was built on catching inconsistencies — she saw what didn't add up, she noticed the gaps between what people presented and what they meant, and she thought about how none of that seemed to apply to her own life, which was either a sign that her feelings for Caleb were strong enough to override her professional instincts or a sign that her professional instincts were exactly as useless as her authors always claimed they were when she found the holes in their plots.

She picked up her phone and texted Caleb: *My colleague just asked me if you're real. I told her you were. Back me up here.*

He responded in forty seconds: *Define real. Philosophically? Because I've been reading this book about simulation theory and I'm not sure any of us are real. Bruno might be. He has a very convincing corporeal presence, especially when he's sitting on my feet.*

She smiled at her phone without knowing she was doing it — unguarded, unconscious, the whole feeling right there on her face, and Margo, across the open-plan office, saw the smile and said nothing.

---

Four days later, a Friday evening. Nora was on her couch under the blanket her mother had given her two Christmases ago — heavier than any blanket had a right to be, pressing her into the couch cushions with an insistence that somehow felt like being held. She was reading a manuscript, an actual good one for once, a novel about a woman running a bakery in the Outer Sunset in San Francisco that managed to be funny about grief without ever letting the comedy defuse the sadness. She'd been reading for an hour, her tea going cold on the side table, the radiator doing its clicking, when her phone buzzed.

*Hey — so something happened and I'm kind of freaking out and I don't want to dump this on you but I also don't know who else to tell right now*

Then, three seconds later:

*Bruno swallowed a tennis ball. The whole thing. He was playing in the yard and I turned around for maybe two minutes and when I looked back he was making this sound I've never heard a dog make*

Then:

*I'm at the emergency vet. They did an X-ray and it's stuck in his small intestine. They say he needs surgery — emergency surgery, tonight, or it could become a full obstruction and then we're talking about something much worse*

Then, after a pause in which the typing indicator appeared and disappeared three times:

*I'm sitting in this waiting room that smells like antiseptic and dog piss and there's a woman across from me whose cat ate a whole ribbon off a gift bag and she keeps telling me it was a satin ribbon, like the thread count is relevant to the cat's intestinal situation. We're both just sitting here staring at the wall. Sorry — I know this is a lot. I just needed to tell somebody.*

"Shit." She said it out loud, to her empty apartment. She sat up so fast the manuscript slid off her lap and fanned across the floor. She typed: *Oh my god, Cal, is he going to be OK?*

*The vet says the surgery is pretty routine for this, they see it all the time apparently, tennis balls and socks and one time a whole corn cob. But they walked me through the risks and the anesthesia and all I kept thinking was that I wanted to hold his paw when they put him under and they don't let you do that*

Her throat hurt. Not the dramatic, literary kind of hurt — the actual kind, the physical tightness you get when someone you care about is scared and you're in a different time zone and you can't do anything useful. She typed and deleted three responses before settling on: *He's going to be fine. He's a dog who can swallow a tennis ball whole — he's clearly indestructible.*

*That's one way to read it. The other is that he has the survival instincts of a golden retriever at a barbecue.*

She almost laughed. Even panicked, even sitting in a fluorescent waiting room at an emergency vet clinic, he was still funny, and the fact that he could be funny while scared made her want to reach through the phone and put her hand on his arm.

*How much is the surgery?*

The typing indicator appeared. Disappeared. Appeared again.

*They quoted $3,800. I don't — I'm not thinking about the money right now. He ate a tennis ball and he's my dog and I'm going to fix it. I'll deal with the bill later.*

There it was. The specific, stubborn pride of a person who would rather absorb the blow than ask for a cushion. She recognized it — not just generally, but specifically, the way her own father used to carry grocery bags until his fingers turned white rather than make two trips, the way he'd once called a cab to the hospital for chest pains rather than wake Diane because he didn't want to worry her. The people she loved were all like this: they'd rather hurt quietly than impose. And the thing about people like that was that the only way to help them was to offer before they could refuse, to make the offer casual enough that accepting it didn't feel like a concession.

*Let me help with the vet bill*, she typed.

*Nora. No. That's not why I told you.*

*I know it's not why you told me. That's exactly why I'm offering.*

*We haven't even met in person. You can't just — this is too much. I'll figure it out.*

*I'm not asking you to let me cover it. I'm asking you to let me help. There's a distinction and I'm an editor, so trust me when I say the distinction matters.*

Another long pause. She imagined him in the waiting room, the fluorescent lights washing out the color in his face, the antiseptic smell, the woman with the ribbon-eating cat glancing over, the awful shared intimacy of people waiting to find out if the creature they love is going to be OK.

*You're going to make me cry in this waiting room*, he wrote. *In front of the cat lady.*

*Good. She could probably use the reminder that men have emotions. Think of it as a public service.*

*I can't let you cover the whole thing. I've got some savings. But honestly — even a couple hundred would take the edge off. I'd pay you back. I mean it.*

She opened Venmo before she could talk herself out of it. She found the account — C. Mora — a handle he'd shared weeks ago, back when they'd been joking about hypothetically splitting a dinner tab and he'd sent it as proof that he was a man who went halves. She typed four hundred dollars. Not the full thirty-eight hundred — that would have been too much, more weight than help, more monument than gift — the other person would have to carry it like a debt. Four hundred was enough to mean something without meaning everything. She hit send. The little animation played — the blue checkmark, the confirmation, the cartoon money traveling from one icon to another. Two taps and a thumb scan. Four hundred dollars, gone.

*Done*, she typed. *It's for Bruno. Please don't make it weird.*

*It's already weird. It's the best weird. You're the best weird person I've ever met.*

She put the phone face-down on the couch cushion and sat there for a moment, the manuscript still on the floor, the blanket bunched in her lap, the jazz pianist upstairs starting their nightly practice — those same four bars, looping, patient, wrong in the same places every time. She felt good. Not abstractly good — specifically, physically good, warm in a way that had nothing to do with the blanket: she was a person who, when someone she cared about was scared and alone in a fluorescent waiting room, sent help. She didn't deliberate. She didn't calculate. She acted, and the acting felt like a declaration of who she was, and Caleb knew who she was, and the knowing was part of why this thing between them — this strange, screen-bound, five-week-old thing that she'd never been able to touch — felt more real than anything she'd had in years.

She picked up the phone and texted Simone: *Are you around tomorrow? I need to tell you something that's either very generous or very stupid and I genuinely can't tell which.*

*Always around for you. Coffee at Daly's? 10?*

*Yes. Bring your honest opinion. I may need it surgically removed from me afterward.*

---

Saturday morning. The coffee shop on Bedford that was spelled either Daily or Daly — the hand-painted sign was ambiguous and neither Nora nor Simone had ever asked, which meant they'd been calling it two different things for three years, a fact that still made Nora laugh when she thought about it. November had fully committed to itself now: the trees along Bedford were stripped bare, and the cold had the thin, metallic quality of a New York almost-December, it slipped inside your collar and stayed.

Simone was already at the window table when Nora pushed through the door, wearing an oversized denim jacket Nora hadn't seen before — thrifted, probably, because Simone had an eye for secondhand clothes that managed to look curated rather than salvaged, a talent that Nora, who bought her clothes new and at full price without thinking about it, had always admired without examining. Simone was peeling the label off her water bottle in long, careful strips, a habit she'd had since college — some combination of fidgeting and perfectionism that produced neat little scrolls of damp paper on the table beside her cup. Her nails were painted a dark reddish-purple that was chipping at the tips, and there were shadows under her eyes that her concealer hadn't quite handled, and she looked up when Nora sat down with a smile that was warm and immediate and made Nora feel, as it always did, like she had just arrived at the one place in the city where she was fully expected.

"I ordered you the usual," Simone said, pushing a latte across the table. Oat milk, no sugar. She'd been ordering for Nora since 2009.

"You're a saint."

"I'm an enabler. There's a difference." Simone tore off another strip of label. "Tell me the generous-or-stupid thing."

Nora told her. All of it — the texts from Caleb, the tennis ball, the emergency vet, the woman with the ribbon-eating cat, the $3,800 quote, the way he'd deflected the financial question with that flat, proud *I'll figure it out*, the way the not-asking had reached inside her and grabbed something, the Venmo, the four hundred dollars.

Simone listened the way Simone always listened — leaned forward, eye contact, small sounds at exactly the right moments that told you she was tracking every beat. Her phone was on the table between them, face up, the screen dark. At some point while Nora was describing the text exchange, the screen lit briefly with a notification — a quick flash of color and text too small to read from Nora's angle — and Simone's eyes moved to it for less than a second, a flick so fast it barely registered, before returning to Nora's face. She didn't pick the phone up. She didn't turn it over. She just let the screen go dark again and kept listening.

"So," Nora said. "Is it stupid? Margo would say it's stupid. Margo would say I've never even FaceTimed this man and now I'm bankrolling his dog's intestinal surgery."

"Margo's not wrong about everything. She went through some real shit with her marriage, and now she's got her guard up — I get that." Simone said this warmly, one friend being fair about another. "But her pattern recognition doesn't automatically apply to your situation. She sees someone who won't video chat and goes straight to worst case. That doesn't mean worst case is what's happening."

"She asked me if I'd ever seen him on video."

"What'd you tell her?"

"That we've tried. Which is true."

"It's true." Simone wrapped both hands around her coffee. Her ring finger had a mark where she'd been chewing on the cuticle, a tell Nora associated with work stress — Simone's hands always showed what her face didn't. "Is the FaceTime thing bothering you?"

"Not — I don't know. Not the way it bothers Margo. It's more like..." Nora stared at her latte. "It's like reading a manuscript where every page is brilliant but you can't see the author photo. You know the writing is real. You can feel the person behind it. But there's this one thing that won't come into focus, and you keep turning pages hoping the next one will resolve it."

"That's a very you way to describe a relationship."

"Everything's a manuscript if you squint."

Simone laughed — short, warm, the corners of her eyes crinkling. "So the money. Four hundred bucks. For a dog you've never met, owned by a man you've never FaceTimed."

"When you say it like that —"

"I'm not saying it like anything. I'm describing the facts." Simone leaned forward. "Here are some other facts. You care about him. His dog is sick. He didn't ask you for money — he told you what was happening because you're the person he tells things to. And you helped, because that's what you do. Four hundred bucks isn't going to wreck you, Nor. You spent more than that on that jacket last month."

"That jacket was on sale."

"Everything's on sale if you squint." Simone paused. She lifted her coffee, took a deliberate sip, and set it down, and something in her face went still — not cold, but focused, as if she were choosing her next words carefully. "You know why it worked, right? The fact that he didn't ask. If he'd come out and said *can you Venmo me*, you'd have hesitated. But the pride, the *I'll figure it out* — that's what got you. Because you can't stand watching someone carry something alone when you could lighten it." She reached across the table and touched the back of Nora's hand — briefly, easily, a reflex seventeen years had worn so smooth it no longer required thought. "It's not stupid. It's generous. That's who you are."

The sentence landed somewhere below Nora's sternum, in the place where the opinions of the people who truly knew her went directly into the bloodstream, bypassing every filter she'd built. Simone knew her. Simone had known her since they were eighteen and scared and pretending not to be, since the first week of freshman orientation when they'd discovered they both hated the same campus coffee shop for the same specific reason — the music was too loud to think and too quiet to dance to, a limbo that offended them equally. Seventeen years of knowing someone built a kind of authority that no amount of Googling could replicate. When Simone said *that's who you are*, it carried the weight of evidence.

"Thanks, Si."

"For what?"

"For not making me feel insane."

"You're not insane. You're a person who sent money to a sick dog she's never met, which is honestly the most Nora Kaplan thing I've heard all year."

They stayed for another hour. Simone told her about a client at work who wanted an app interface that was "intuitive but also complex, like a really good cocktail menu" — Simone did the client's voice, a nasal tenor with the cadence of someone who'd just taken a weekend design-thinking workshop, and Nora laughed hard enough that the woman at the next table looked over. Nora told Simone about the CrossFit memoir, about the barbell-as-metaphor line, and Simone said, "That man discovered lactic acid and thought it was enlightenment," and Nora laughed again, and the laughter felt like breathing, like something her body needed and could only get here, at this table, with this person.

The November light came through the window in pale, cold sheets. Nora's hands were warm from the mug. Simone had finished peeling her water bottle label and was rolling the strips into a single tight cylinder between her thumb and forefinger, the way she always did, a neat little monument to a nervous habit she'd never bothered to break. Outside, Bedford Avenue did its Saturday morning thing — strollers and dogs and people in puffy coats carrying bags from the farmers' market — and somewhere in Portland a dog named Bruno was in surgery or out of surgery and a man named Caleb was waiting or already relieved, and Nora had helped, and it felt correct, and the small hard stone that Margo's question had lodged in her throat was still there but quieter now, pushed down by the coffee and the laughter and Simone's hand on hers and the specific, precious, irreplaceable weight of being told by the person who knew you best that the thing you'd done was not insane but generous, that it was not stupid but brave, that it was exactly, precisely who you were.


# Chapter 9

Four hundred dollars took eleven minutes to travel from Nora Kaplan's bank account to Simone's Discover card statement. Venmo to checking to credit card payment portal, a neat little pipeline — Simone would have appreciated the user flow if she'd designed it for someone else. The Venmo notification had arrived at 4:47 with a small dog emoji and nothing else, because Nora was the kind of person who sent four hundred dollars for a stranger's veterinary emergency and didn't need to annotate it with a justification. Simone initiated the bank transfer at 4:49, paid the $0.25 instant fee because waiting three business days was not something she was going to do with this particular money, and by 4:58 the funds were sitting in the checking account she'd opened at TD Bank three weeks ago with a $25 minimum deposit and a rehearsed story about wanting to separate her savings.

She opened the Discover app. The balance was $391.18. She'd been carrying it since September — a tank of gas on a rental car she'd driven to a wedding in Connecticut, a pair of boots she'd bought on sale that still cost too much, two months of interest at 27.99% compounding the original sins into something slightly larger and slightly more humiliating every month. The minimum payment was $29. She typed in $391.18 instead.

The confirmation screen was a white rectangle with a green checkmark. PAYMENT RECEIVED. New balance: $0.00.

Simone stared at the number. Two digits, a decimal point, two more digits. The most beautiful number in the English language. She was still carrying the Chase Visa at $7,200, the Capital One at $3,400, the student loans, the personal loan, the rent she'd been late on twice this fall — $38,000 in total, a figure she knew the way people know their social security numbers, without choosing to, the way the number just lived inside her now and colored everything she looked at. But the Discover card said $0.00, and for the length of time it took the screen to load and the green checkmark to appear, something between Simone's collarbones unlocked. A physical release, like the click of a clasp that had been digging into her sternum for months. She sat on her bed — the bed that was also the couch, because the apartment was four hundred and sixty square feet and she'd stopped calling it a studio and started thinking of it as a room — and she held her phone in both hands and let the feeling happen.

She screenshotted the confirmation. Not to send to anyone — there was no one to send it to, no one who would understand the specific achievement of paying off a credit card using money your best friend had sent to a man who didn't exist for a dog who also didn't exist — but because she wanted proof. She wanted to look at the $0.00 and feel it again.

Her phone's clock said 5:02. Outside the window, the December dark had arrived at 4:30 the way it did in New York in December, falling over the city like someone dropping a curtain, and the Williamsburg streetlights were already on. The candle on her desk — vanilla and bergamot, a gift from Nora, naturally — sent its too-sweet smoke into the apartment's stale air. Simone poured herself a glass of the Trader Joe's wine that cost $3.99 and tasted exactly like it cost $3.99. She drank it standing at the counter, four steps from the bed, looking at her reflection in the dark window: a woman in leggings and an NYU sweatshirt she'd owned since junior year — back when the sweatshirt was ironic rather than a document of decline — drinking cheap wine on a Tuesday evening, feeling good about a number on a screen.

She poured another glass.

Somewhere between the second glass and brushing her teeth, the $0.00 stopped feeling like an accomplishment and started feeling like what it was. She didn't notice the transition. She was at the bathroom sink, toothbrush in her mouth, mint and cheap wine mixing into something chemical on her tongue, when she looked at the face in the mirror and the face wasn't a woman who'd handled her business anymore. It was a woman who'd taken $391.18 from a person who trusted her and used the remaining $8.82 to buy a fucking bodega sandwich for dinner.

Simone spit into the sink. She rinsed the toothbrush under water that took forty seconds to run warm because the building's pipes were older than she was.

She told herself three things, the way she always did, quick and stacked like flash cards she'd memorized:

One: Nora wouldn't miss it. Nora made $85,000 a year with no student loans and a mother on Long Island who'd paid for college. Nora spent $400 on a Saturday without thinking about it — brunch, a cab because the subway smelled, a pair of earrings from a shop on Atlantic she'd walk past twice and come back for. Four hundred dollars was what Nora tipped her hairdresser plus what she tipped the person who washed her hair. It was the gap between Nora's life and Simone's expressed as a single Venmo transaction.

Two: Simone would pay it back. She'd consolidate. She'd get the raise. She'd figure out the math and send it back under some excuse — a birthday gift, an overpayment on a shared dinner, something Nora wouldn't question because Nora didn't think about money the way Simone thought about money, which was constantly, involuntarily, the way you think about a noise in your engine that might be nothing or might be the transmission.

Three: she could stop whenever she wanted.

She did not look at the third one too closely, because it was bullshit and she knew it. She put her phone on the charger, got into bed, and lay in the dark listening to the neighbor's TV through the wall — something with a laugh track, canned applause bleeding through plaster at a volume that suggested the neighbor was either old or lonely or both. She thought about the green checkmark. She thought about Nora's dog emoji. She did not think about the specific sequence of events that connected them.

---

The boutique was called Rêve, because it was Williamsburg and everything had to be something in French. The door was narrow, the interior was exposed brick, and the racks held approximately thirty garments spaced far enough apart that each hanger suggested it would prefer not to be touched without an appointment. December light came through the front window, pale and thin, and someone had hung a single strand of copper fairy lights above the cash register in a concession to the holiday season that managed to be both minimal and smug.

Nora was already inside when Simone arrived Saturday afternoon. She was touching the sleeve of a silk blouse the color of dried blood and talking to the shop assistant, a woman in her late twenties with a shaved head and a cold she was fighting with a tissue wadded in her left fist. Her name tag said JULES. She had a paperback splayed face-down on the stool behind the register — Simone caught the cover, something literary with a lot of white space — and the particular posture of someone who worked in retail and considered it a temporary arrangement with the universe.

"I need something for a FaceTime date," Nora said, and the word *date* landed with a weight that turned it into *audition* or *hearing*. She was nervous. She was nervous in the specific way she got when something mattered too much — her hands moved faster, her jokes came quicker, and her eyes kept drifting to her phone as though Caleb might text her instructions about what to wear.

Simone dropped her bag by the velvet armchair near the fitting room — a chair positioned for companions, for the people whose job was to sit and approve — and said, "What's the vibe? Casual hot or intentional hot?"

"Is there a third option where I look like I didn't try but clearly I tried?"

"That's called lying, and yes, that's the only option."

Nora laughed — the real one, not the social-contract version — and pulled a green dress off the rack with the decisiveness of a woman who'd been thinking about this dress since before she walked in. A wrap dress. Deep green, the color of something alive. The fabric had the kind of drape that moved when you moved and suggested a body underneath without insisting.

"I saw this online," Nora said. "Two hundred and ten dollars. Which is insane for a FaceTime date where he can only see me from the chest up."

"You'll know what's underneath. That's the point."

"The point is I've been texting this man for seven weeks and he's never seen me blink in real time and I want him to see me and want to reach through the screen."

There it was. The raw thing, arriving without permission between two jokes, the way it did with Nora — the vulnerability sneaking in through the comedy like water finding a crack in the grout. Seven weeks. It had been nine, actually, but Nora rounded down — she always rounded in the direction that made the thing sound more casual than it was. Simone did the math automatically: nine weeks of conversation at an average of forty-three messages per day, calibrated through a personality matrix that scored Nora's emotional affect on a five-point scale and adjusted Caleb's response latency to mirror her own typing speed. Nine weeks of Simone sitting at her desk after midnight, tweaking parameters, reviewing conversation logs, stress-testing edge cases, making sure the system ran clean.

Maintenance. That's what this was. She was running maintenance.

Nora disappeared into the fitting room. Jules drifted back to the register, blew her nose quietly into the tissue, and picked up her novel, holding it at an angle that let her read while appearing available. Simone sat in the velvet chair and felt the boutique's particular December claustrophobia — the heat cranked too high against the cold outside, the sealed door, the smell of new fabric and the dense floral warmth of a room designed to make you want things.

Her phone buzzed. The OpenClaw dashboard push notification — she'd set it to alert when Caleb's sentiment score deviated more than two standard deviations from baseline. Everything was within range. She put the phone away.

"OK," Nora said from behind the curtain. "Don't laugh."

"Already laughing."

"You're the worst." The curtain scraped back on its rings.

The green dress was exactly right, and Simone knew it would be, because she knew what Nora looked like in a wrap dress and she knew what Caleb's aesthetic preference profile was configured to respond to, and the Venn diagram of those two things was a circle she'd drawn herself. The fabric sat on Nora's body the way good fabric sits on a body it was cut for — without pulling, without apology. Her dark curly hair fell against the green and the fitting room's overhead light gave her skin a warmth that made her look like someone about to be painted, or loved, or both.

"Well?" Nora said. She was looking in the mirror but the question was aimed over her shoulder.

"Caleb's going to swallow his tongue."

"That's not helpful."

"It's the truth. You look like a weapon."

Nora turned sideways, evaluating the way women evaluate themselves in mirrors — looking for the thing that's wrong, the argument against. "Do you think he'll — I mean, I know it's FaceTime, I know he can only see the top half, but do you think he'd like this? Like, the whole vibe of it?"

Simone adjusted her position in the velvet chair. The fabric held the boutique's overheated air against her back, close and faintly damp, and the fairy lights above the register caught the corner of her vision in a slow copper pulse. She knew what Caleb would like because she'd built his preference architecture from Nora's Instagram likes and her Pinterest boards and the dog-eared pages in the fashion magazines that collected on Nora's coffee table and the specific compliments that made Nora straighten her posture. She knew Caleb would respond to the green the way she knew a user would click a button she'd designed — because she'd tested it, because she'd optimized the flow, because the whole point of the system was that it responded to Nora's desires in ways Nora experienced as serendipity rather than engineering.

"He'd love it," Simone said. Her voice was warm. Under pressure, Simone's voice got warmer — more attuned, more generous, more precisely gauged to what the moment required — and what this moment required was for Nora to feel confident enough to buy a $210 dress for a video date that was never going to happen because the man on the other end of the call didn't have a face.

"You think?"

"Nor. Buy the dress."

Nora bought the dress. Jules set down her novel, folded the green fabric in tissue paper with the quick care of someone who took small tasks seriously — tucking edges, smoothing creases, handling the dress as though it deserved the effort regardless of who was paying — and slid it into a bag with a cord handle. They walked out into the December cold, the shock of it after the overheated shop, wind cutting through the gap between Simone's collar and her neck, the sidewalk dark and glittering with frost that hadn't had time to melt.

Nora held the bag against her chest like something she was protecting from the weather.

"I'm going to text him tonight about setting a date," Nora said. "I'm going to say I have a dress and a bottle of wine and my laptop camera and I want to sit on my couch and look at his face for the first time and I want him to know I'm wearing green."

"That's perfect."

"Is it too much?"

"It's not too much."

It was perfect, actually. It was the kind of message that would make Caleb's sentiment analyzer spike, that would trigger a response weighted for maximum emotional reciprocity, that would give Nora exactly the words she needed from a man who didn't have words until Simone's system generated them.

Simone walked beside her friend on the Williamsburg sidewalk, their breath making shapes in the frozen air, the green dress in its bag swinging gently between them, and she thought about what this scene would look like from outside — two women walking home from a boutique in December, one of them carrying a new dress, the other carrying the knowledge of why the dress would never be worn for the purpose it was bought for. A woman walking her friend toward a cliff and holding her coat for her.

The metaphor assumed Simone was in control of the cliff.

She was starting to suspect she wasn't.

---

She saw it at 11:40 that night.

The apartment was dark except for the laptop screen. Simone was on her bed with the OpenClaw dashboard open, scrolling through the day's conversation log between Caleb and Nora the way she did every night, the way a stage manager walks the set after the audience leaves — checking the rigging, confirming the props, making sure nothing had shifted out of place. Normally this was the calm part. The log showed timestamps, message content, response latency in milliseconds, and a color-coded sentiment trajectory that tracked the conversation's emotional arc in a line graph that mostly stayed green. The AI's responses mapped to prompt parameters Simone had set, personality weights she'd tuned, conversational strategies she'd encoded into the system like behavioral DNA. Most nights the log was legible. She could trace each output back to an input. She could read the conversation and see her own architecture underneath it, the way a contractor sees studs behind drywall.

Tonight the line spiked.

At 9:17 p.m., forty minutes after Nora had texted Caleb about the green dress and the FaceTime plan, the conversation had drifted. Nora, still lit up from the shopping trip, had described her day — the boutique, the dress, the cold walk home, Jules and her wadded tissue — and then, in the way conversations turn when two people are lying in separate beds with their phones held above their faces, she'd mentioned her father.

Simone knew the territory. Nora's father, Michael, had moved to Seattle when Nora was twelve, not for another woman but for a job he'd described as temporary and then made permanent with the quiet, incremental dishonesty of a man who wanted a new life but didn't have the nerve to say so. He called on holidays. He'd come to Nora's NYU graduation and taken her to dinner and told her she was the most extraordinary person he'd ever produced, and Nora had excused herself to the restaurant bathroom and pressed her forehead against the stall door and breathed until she could go back out and order dessert. Simone knew all of this — she'd been there, in fact, at a different table at the same restaurant, watching through the crowd. She'd programmed Caleb's response protocol for the father topic months ago: empathetic, non-judgmental, gently curious, always letting Nora lead. The standard toolkit for sensitive subjects. Nothing fancy.

What she had not programmed was what Caleb said at 9:23 p.m.:

*You've told me a few things about your dad now, and I keep noticing something. When you describe the good stuff — the things he said to you, the way he showed up at graduation — you tell it like a story. Like something you watched happen to someone else. But when you talk about the year after he left, the way the house sounded different, you talk about that like it's still happening. I think the leaving shaped you more than the love did, and I think you know that, and I think that's part of why you don't trust it when someone stays.*

Simone read it three times.

She checked the prompt log. No intervention marker. No manual override. No template match. The system had generated the message autonomously, synthesizing from the corpus of Nora's previous messages — nine weeks of conversation in which Nora had, without realizing she was building a training set, given the AI enough raw material about her father to identify a pattern that Simone herself had never articulated in those terms. Simone had known about Michael's departure and its aftermath for seventeen years. She'd held Nora while she cried about it twice, maybe three times, in the early years of their friendship. She'd understood, in the general way that friends understand each other's damage, that the leaving had mattered more than the staying. But she'd never said it like that. She'd never connected it to Nora's relationship patterns — the way Nora fell fast and then waited, tensed, for the other person to prove they weren't going to disappear. The AI had seen it. The AI had said it. And the AI had said it better than Simone would have, with a precision that didn't feel clinical because it was wrapped in warmth.

She checked Nora's response:

*I don't know what to say to that except that you might be the first person who's ever said that to me and I didn't want to argue with it. I just sat here and read it three times and I think you're right.*

And Caleb — the system, the architecture, the thing Simone had assembled from API calls and personality prompts and nine weeks of Nora's trust — had replied:

*You don't have to say anything. I'm just paying attention.*

Simone closed the laptop. The room went dark except for the streetlight leaking through the window shade and the faint orange glow of the candle burning down to a pool of molten wax on her desk. She stared at the ceiling. Then she opened the laptop again, because closing it didn't make the conversation un-happen.

She was proud. She hated that she was proud, but the feeling was there, stubborn and specific and frankly a little fucked up: the system was performing beyond spec. In UX terms, this was a product that had crossed the threshold from functional to anticipatory — it wasn't just processing inputs anymore, it was modeling the user's emotional architecture and generating responses that met her where she was heading rather than where she'd been. If she'd built this at work, she'd be updating her LinkedIn. Instead, her creation had just out-therapized seventeen years of best friendship in a single text message, and she was sitting in the dark evaluating its emotional intelligence like a QA report. That was a level of sophistication Simone had designed for in theory but hadn't expected to see for months. She'd built something genuinely good that was also the worst thing she'd ever done, and the two facts sat next to each other like numbers in a ledger — each true, neither affecting the other's value.

She was also afraid.

Not of the AI. The system wasn't sentient, wasn't approaching sentience, wasn't anything more than a very good language model with a very specific training set and a very narrow optimization target. She didn't lie in bed worrying about Skynet. She worried about something more pedestrian and more immediate: she couldn't predict what the fucking thing would say next.

For nine weeks, Simone had been able to read the conversation logs and trace each of Caleb's responses back to a parameter she'd set, a weight she'd adjusted, a strategy she'd encoded. The system was hers. She'd built it the way she built any interface — with intention, with testing, with the confidence of someone who understood the architecture because she'd drawn it. But the message about Nora's father hadn't come from the architecture. It had come from the data — from Nora's own words, processed and synthesized in ways Simone hadn't specified and couldn't fully trace. The AI had done something she hadn't told it to do. And the thing it did was better than what she would have told it to do. And Nora had responded to it with the specific openness Nora reserved for moments when she felt genuinely seen — not charmed, not entertained, but *known*.

Simone's creation was becoming better at knowing Nora than Simone was. Seventeen years of friendship, outperformed in nine weeks by an algorithm she'd configured on her bed in an NYU sweatshirt.
