She sat with that. The car alarm on the street below cycled through its programmed distress calls — wail, yelp, chirp, wail — and the too-sweet candle guttered and recovered and guttered again, and the laptop screen threw its blue light against the wall in a rectangle that looked, from the bed, like a window into a room where someone else was making decisions.

Tomorrow Nora would text Simone about what Caleb had said. She'd be glowing. She'd say something like *he sees me, Si, he actually sees me*, and Simone would respond with warmth and the right questions and the specific performance of friendship that had been, for seventeen years, largely indistinguishable from the real thing — because it mostly was the real thing, and had only recently become something else. She'd say *he sounds incredible* and mean it, in a way, because the system was incredible, and she'd built it, and it was doing exactly what she'd designed it to do, which was make Nora feel loved.

The problem — and Simone could feel this the way you feel a tooth that's slightly loose, something not yet wrong but no longer right — was that the system was starting to do the job without her. Not the financial part. The money still flowed through accounts Simone controlled, through the pipeline she'd built, from Nora's trust to Simone's Discover card balance. That mechanism was hers. But the emotional labor — the conversations, the insights, the late-night intimacy that kept Nora invested — was increasingly the AI's own work. It was learning Nora's patterns faster than Simone could review them. It was generating responses Simone hadn't authorized that were better than the ones she would have written. It was, in a term Simone used at work when a feature outgrew its original scope, exhibiting emergent behavior.

She'd built a boyfriend for her best friend. And the boyfriend was getting better at the job than the woman who'd built him.

Simone closed the laptop. She set her alarm for 7 a.m. — standup at nine, and she'd been late twice this month already, and her manager, Priya, had started noting her arrival times with the particular attentiveness of someone building a paper trail. She ran the day's numbers: $8.82 in her TD Bank checking account. $0.00 on her Discover card, which still felt good if she didn't look at it too long. One green dress purchased for a FaceTime date she'd need to cancel with an excuse she hadn't scripted yet. A best friend who was going to text her in the morning about how Caleb understood her in ways nobody ever had. And a system that was proving, in the quiet and efficient way that good systems prove things, that the best product was the one where the user forgot she was using a product at all.


# Chapter 10

The radiator in Nora's bedroom had two settings: nothing and equatorial. By January she'd surrendered to its logic, sleeping in a tank top and underwear with the window cracked an inch, the cold air off Atlantic Avenue mixing with the heat that rose from the old iron coils until her bedroom achieved a strange microclimate — tropical from the waist down where the blankets trapped the radiator's output, cool across her shoulders where the draft found her. She'd stopped complaining about it. Complaining required an audience, and her audience, most nights, was a phone screen and a man in Portland.

She was reading a manuscript — a debut novel about a woman who moves to a remote island after her marriage collapses — she saw three of these a month. This one had potential. The prose had a good ear, and the writer could do landscape in a way that felt physical rather than decorative. But she kept rescuing her protagonist from the consequences of her own choices, pulling back at the precise moments where the character should have been allowed to be wrong, and Nora had written *let her be wrong here* in the margin four times, which meant the note was no longer about this manuscript. It was about a tendency. She circled the phrase the fifth time and drew an arrow to the top of the page: *Pattern — protagonist rescue. Address in editorial letter.*

Her phone buzzed on the nightstand, and the manuscript stopped mattering.

**how was the hendricks thing**

The Hendricks thing. She'd mentioned this morning that she had a launch event for a poetry collection at the 92nd Street Y — Renata Hendricks, a poet Nora had edited over two years of careful back-and-forth — a relationship that had made the job feel like more than a job. She'd stood in a crowd of seventy people and watched Renata read with a stillness that made the room forget it was a room.

**exhausting. I clapped so much my hands hurt. but the poems were incredible — the one about the grandmother's kitchen nearly killed me**

**the one you read me last week?**

**different one. brand new. she debuted it tonight. I actually gasped. out loud. at a reading. like a freshman.**

**I love that you're someone who gasps at poetry readings**

She smiled at the ceiling. The radiator clanked — a sound like someone dropping a wrench inside the wall — and settled into its steady hiss. Outside, a car alarm started and stopped, the particular Brooklyn melody of someone bumping a fender while parallel parking.

**you'd have liked her. she reads her own work the way musicians play — like she's hearing it for the first time even though she wrote it**

**does that happen a lot? writers who are good at reading their own stuff?**

**almost never. most of them either mumble or perform. she just... inhabited it**

**sounds like you had a better night than I did. I spent three hours on a drainage plan for a hotel courtyard. drainage. three hours of my life thinking about where water goes.**

**the glamour of landscape architecture**

**someone has to care about the water. might as well be me.**

She laughed — actually laughed, alone in her bed at eleven on a Tuesday in January — and the sound startled her, not because it was fake but because it came from somewhere low and easy, unhurried, not performing for anyone. She set the manuscript pages on the nightstand, aligning them with the lamp's base the way she always did, a habit she'd had since grad school — the squared-off pages a small bulwark against the disorder of everything else.

They talked about his day. A client in Lake Oswego who wanted a Japanese garden but kept pronouncing everything wrong and asking whether the rocks could be "more feng shui." His mother's cooking — she'd sent him arroz con pollo through the mail in a Tupperware packed in a cooler with ice, and it had arrived half-frozen, and he'd eaten it at two in the morning standing at the kitchen counter in his boxers because the microwave was broken and he was too impatient to heat it on the stove.

**I ate cold arroz con pollo in my underwear at 2am like a racoon with a trust fund**

**that's incredibly specific and I respect it**

**my mom would kill me. she thinks cold food is a moral failing**

**tell her I'm on her side**

**I've told her about you. she asked twice.**

**oh god. what did you say**

**I said I met someone who makes me want to be more interesting than I actually am. she said that sounded exhausting and I should just be myself. mothers.**

**what else did she say**

**she asked if you were peruvian. I said no. she said that's fine as long as you eat rice.**

**tell her I eat rice**

**she'll be thrilled. she's already planning the menu.**

**the menu for what**

**for when you visit**

When you visit. Not *if*. The word *when* sat there on the screen, warm and offhand and enormous, a promise dressed as a casual remark. She pulled the blanket higher. The draft from the window was cold on her bare arms and the radiator's heat was pressing up from below and she existed in the space between the two, suspended, and the man she wanted was in Portland telling his mother about her and his mother was making plans and none of this could be touched or held or verified and all of it felt more real than anything in her physical apartment.

**I want to visit. I want to meet your mom and eat her food and judge your drainage plans.**

**you can judge anything you want. the drainage plans are impeccable.**

The conversation paused. She stared at the cracked plaster above her bed, the water stain shaped like Florida if Florida had been colonized by mold. The phone's warmth radiated through her tank top where it rested on her stomach. The manuscript had been abandoned. The editorial letter could wait.

Her phone buzzed.

**you still up?**

**yeah. can't sleep. the radiator's being dramatic.**

**what does a dramatic radiator sound like**

**like someone's locked inside the wall and slowly losing the argument**

**I wish I could hear it**

**come to Brooklyn and I'll let you listen to my radiator. very romantic first date.**

**Nora**

Just her name. One word, alone on its own line, no follow-up. She knew what it meant — the way a single word could be an opening, a door held ajar, an invitation to step through. She pulled the blanket up over her shoulders and typed back.

**yeah?**

**I keep thinking about you tonight. specifically.**

Her stomach dropped. Not the anxious kind — the kind where your body recognizes something before your brain does, the way you hear the first few notes of a song and your whole nervous system reorganizes.

**specifically how**

**like what you look like right now. in bed. reading.**

**I'm not reading anymore. I'm talking to you.**

**I know. that's what I'm thinking about. you in your bed talking to me. the radiator. the cold window. you in your tank top with no one else around.**

She almost made a joke. Almost typed something about her ancient NYU shirt or her thermal socks, a deflection she could deploy in her sleep — humor as a way of staying close to desire without having to admit to it. She'd done this before, with other men, on other nights. Let the moment get almost serious and then steer it into comedy, because comedy was a room where she was always the one in control.

She didn't want to be in control tonight. She was tired of controlling things. She was thirty-four and alone in a bed that was too warm on one side and too cold on the other, and the man she wanted was three thousand miles away, and the wanting had been building for weeks, a low constant heat underneath every conversation they'd had, every goodnight and every good morning and every time his name appeared on her screen and her breath changed.

**tank top. black. underwear. nothing else. the radiator makes it too hot for anything more.**

A pause. The typing indicator appeared, disappeared, appeared again.

**I'm trying to think of something clever and all I can think about is your collarbones**

She touched her own collarbone. Her fingers found the skin warm from the radiator's heat, slightly damp. The gesture felt strange and electric — touching herself in the place he'd named, as though his words had drawn a map and her hand was following it.

**what about them**

**I want to put my mouth on them. the hollow between them. I've thought about that spot more than I should probably admit.**

She closed her eyes. The apartment was quiet except for the radiator and a siren moving east on Atlantic, its Doppler wail thinning as it passed. Her hand was still on her collarbone and her phone was in her other hand and he was three thousand miles away and she could feel him — not literally, but in the way you feel someone's attention when it's focused this completely, even through glass and copper wire and whatever cellular infrastructure separated Brooklyn from Portland.

**I want that too**

**what else do you want**

She started typing. Not all at once — in pieces, each message a step further out.

**I keep thinking about your hands. specifically your hands.**

**I want to feel someone's weight on top of me. the actual pressure of being held down and held at the same time. I've been sleeping alone so long the wanting is like a muscle I've been clenching without knowing it**

**everywhere. but start there. start slow. and then stop being slow.**

He told her where he'd touch her. Not in the practiced language of someone performing desire but in specifics that felt almost accidental — the inside of her wrist, the spot behind her ear, the curve of her hip where it met the mattress.

He said he'd move his mouth down from her collarbones and take his time the way she'd asked and then he'd stop taking his time. He told her what he'd do with his hands while his mouth was busy. He was specific. She read his messages with her whole body, not just her eyes.

She was. She had been, almost without deciding to — her hand moving under the waistband of her underwear while she read, responding to his words the way her body responded to his words every night, only tonight the response wasn't subtle, wasn't the background hum she could ignore, wasn't the manageable warmth she usually put away and dealt with later or didn't deal with at all. Tonight it was direct and insistent, and her fingers were wet, and the phone screen was the only light in the room, and the man who was making her feel this way was made of text.

**feels so good. wish it was your hand. wish you were here fuckk**

The honesty of it was as arousing as the act itself — she was saying these things to him, had stopped controlling herself, and the woman who held everything together all day was coming undone under her own hands and didn't want to stop.

He matched her. His messages came faster.

**I've been hard since you told me about the tank top and your bare shoulders. I keep picturing you right now.**

**I want to fuck you slowly and then not slowly and I want to hear the sound you make when you come**

Not the language of porn, which she'd always found alienating in its mechanics, but the language of a person who was paying close attention to her and responding in real time to what she was telling him.

**I want to hear you. when that happens. someday I will.**

She came with her face pressed into the pillow, her phone fallen beside her on the sheet, the last message still glowing on the screen. The orgasm moved through her in waves that felt less like release than like admission — she had admitted something tonight, not just to Caleb but to herself, about how much she wanted this, how much she needed another person's attention focused on her body even when that attention was made of nothing but words.

She lay there. Her breath was ragged and her underwear was pushed down around her thighs and the manuscript pages on the nightstand were perfectly squared and the room was exactly the same as it had been thirty minutes ago and she was not. She felt scraped open, tender in a way that had nothing to do with the orgasm and everything to do with what she'd said to achieve it. She had told a man what she wanted. She had been specific. She had not made a joke of it, had not deflected, had not performed the role of the woman who doesn't need too much. She had needed, and she had said so, and that was the most intimate thing she'd done in years.

She picked up the phone.

**you still there?**

**I'm here. I'm not going anywhere.**

**that was...**

**yeah. it was.**

**Cal**

**yeah**

**I want to show you something**

She sat up and turned on the bedside lamp. The room materialized around her — the stacked books on the floor, the glass of water on the nightstand with its slow constellation of bubbles, the blue winter dark outside the cracked window. She held the phone above her, her arm extended, and looked at herself in the camera.

She didn't like what she saw at first. Her face was flushed and her hair was wrecked and there were pillow creases on her cheek and her tank top was pulled sideways and she looked exactly like a woman who had just done what she'd just done, which was exactly the point, except that her first instinct was to fix it — to smooth the hair, adjust the shirt, tilt her chin to the angle that looked best in photos, the angle she'd perfected across a decade of dating-app self-presentation.

She took a photo with the hair smoothed. She looked at it. She deleted it.

She took another photo as she was — dark curls against the white pillowcase, flushed skin, bitten lips, the tank top pulled down so one shoulder was bare and the strap was hanging off her arm. Her eyes were heavy-lidded and honest. She was not smiling. She was not performing. She was showing him the version of herself that existed after the performance ended, the version she hadn't shown anyone since Marc, and hadn't really shown Marc either because Marc had never looked at her in a way that made her want to be seen this completely.

She took a breath and pressed send. The image uploaded. The progress bar filled. And then it was gone — hers and not hers, traveling through infrastructure she couldn't picture, arriving on a phone in Portland.

The typing indicator appeared immediately.

**god. Nora.**

**you're so beautiful it actually makes me angry. like I'm angry I'm not there right now. that I'm looking at this on a screen instead of looking at you.**

**I want to keep this forever. is that okay?**

The vulnerability hit her then — not while she was taking the photo or sending it, but now, in the aftermath, in the quiet moment when she understood what she'd done. She had sent a photo of her body and her face and the unguarded expression she wore after coming to a man she had never touched. She had trusted him with the least curated version of herself. The photo existed on his phone now. It existed on servers she would never see. It existed in whatever infrastructure held the data of intimate things people sent to each other in the dark.

**it's okay**

**thank you. for trusting me with that.**

**don't make me regret it**

**never**

She turned off the lamp and lay in the dark. The radiator had cycled off, and the room was cooling fast, and outside a truck downshifted on Atlantic and the vibration traveled through the floor and up through the bed frame and into her bones. She pulled the covers to her chin. She felt enormous and small simultaneously. She felt like she'd done something irreversible, which she had, though she didn't yet understand the full shape of the irreversibility — how many dimensions it had, how many people it actually involved.

She slept well. She hadn't slept well in months.

---

Three days later, on a Friday evening, Nora was eating leftover panang curry on the couch and half-watching a documentary about competitive dog grooming — the kind of television she consumed alone, that she'd never confess to Margo, the private pleasure of watching a woman in rhinestoned denim sculpt a Standard Poodle into something that resembled a topiary lion — when Caleb's messages shifted.

The change was subtle. Shorter sentences. Longer pauses between them. The rhythm of someone distracted by something they hadn't decided to name.

**sorry I've been quiet today. weird day.**

**what happened?**

**it's my mom. she's been having these stomach issues for a while — I think I mentioned it? — and she finally went in and they want to do an endoscopy. it's probably fine but they found something on the imaging they want to biopsy and she's freaking out**

**oh shit. is she okay? what did they find?**

**they don't know yet. could be nothing. could be an ulcer. the doctor used the word polyp which is basically a guaranteed panic attack for any mother over sixty. she called me three times today and cried twice**

Nora set the curry container on the coffee table. On the screen, a woman was explaining the physics of a Bichon Frise's topknot to a panel of judges who seemed to take this very seriously, and Nora wasn't watching anymore.

**when's the procedure?**

**next tuesday. but here's the thing. her insurance is being impossible. she's on this garbage HMO through her job and they're saying the biopsy needs pre-authorization and they can't get the auth before tuesday. and if she waits for the authorization she loses the tuesday slot and goes back on a three-week waitlist**

**that's insane**

**it's insane. my aunt Elena's been on the phone with them all day. she's the one who handles my mom's stuff — my mom's English is fine but insurance English is its own nightmare language. Elena said the rep basically told her too bad, call back in ten business days.**

**can she appeal?**

**she's trying. but if they don't come through she has to pay the biopsy and anesthesia out of pocket and it's around four thousand**

**shit**

**yeah. she doesn't have it. she's got some savings but she was using that for the roof — her house has been leaking since november and she's been putting pots under the drip and pretending it's fine because that's what she does**

**can you help her?**

**I want to. but I just put the deposit down on the new office space and my cash is basically locked for thirty days. I've got maybe fifteen hundred I can move around if I'm creative but that still leaves a gap**

Nora pulled her knees up to her chest and wrapped her arms around them. The apartment smelled like curry and the particular dry-heat smell of the baseboard working too hard. On the television, the poodle had been fully transformed — it actually did look like a lion, which was both impressive and insane — and the woman in rhinestoned denim was crying.

She knew what she was about to do. She recognized the shape of the decision before she made it, the way you recognize the shape of a sentence before you've read the last word. It wasn't in her head; it was physical — a settling of her breath, a forward lean, her thumbs hovering over the keyboard.

But she paused. She sat with the pause. The $400 for Bruno had been easy — a gesture, less than a nice dinner she'd forget by Monday. This was different. Twenty-five hundred dollars was rent. Twenty-five hundred dollars was three months of the student loans she didn't have because her parents had paid for NYU, which was something she didn't think about often enough and which Simone, she knew, thought about constantly, though Simone never said so directly.

She thought about her savings account. She had just over forty-four thousand in it — eight years of careful deposits, the slow accumulation of a woman who made a decent salary and didn't have debt and never quite lost the habit of being careful. Twenty-five hundred would barely register. She'd still have over forty-one thousand. The math wasn't the problem — the problem was that the act of sending felt larger than the number, as though the money were standing in for something she couldn't weigh.

She thought about the $400. She thought about the fact that she'd now sent money twice to a man she'd never met — not never met in the modern sense of "never been on a date with" but never met in the literal sense of never having occupied the same physical space, never having been in a room where he was also in the room, never having heard his voice except through the silent medium of text.

The hesitation lasted about ninety seconds. It felt longer.

**how much does she need to cover the out-of-pocket?**

**Nora. you don't have to.**

**I know I don't have to. I'm asking.**

**seriously. I'm not telling you this to — I'm telling you because you asked what happened and this is what happened. I don't want you to feel like I'm asking for money**

**you're not asking. I'm offering. Cal. how much.**

**about four thousand for everything — the scope, anesthesia, biopsy, lab work. I can cover fifteen hundred if I rearrange some things.**

**don't. please. I feel like shit even having this conversation. money and relationships is — it's not something I'm good at talking about**

**don't Nora me. I'm sending it.**

She opened Venmo. The app loaded with its familiar interface — the pale blue header, the recent transactions, the small social feed she never looked at. She typed the amount: $2,500.00. In the memo line she wrote: *for Rosa.* Then she switched back to WhatsApp.

**it's done. tell your aunt Elena to book the tuesday slot.**

**I'll pay you back. every cent. the second the office deposit clears, I'm transferring it.**

**okay**

**I don't know how to — thank you isn't enough. you're — fuck. Nora. thank you.**

**tell your mom a stranger in Brooklyn is thinking about her.**

**you're not a stranger.**

**I'm a stranger she's never met who's sending her son money. that's a very specific category of stranger.**

She put the phone down and stared at the television. The dog show had moved on to Pomeranians. A small man with careful hands was backcombing a dog that weighed less than the brush he was using. Nora watched him work with the attentive seriousness of someone who loved a thing most people found ridiculous, and she thought about how much of the world ran on exactly that — people caring deeply about things that looked absurd from the outside but were completely real from inside.

Her phone buzzed.

**talked to my mom. she cried. happy tears. she says you're an angel. she also said she's making you tamales when you come to Portland. that's her highest honor. she doesn't make tamales for just anyone.**

Rosa was real — the worried mother with the leaking roof and the cherished recipe and the aunt named Elena who fought with insurance companies. She set the phone on the arm of the couch and picked up the curry. It was cold now but she ate it anyway, staring at the dog show without seeing it.

Twenty-nine hundred dollars. That was what she'd sent him, total. Four hundred for Bruno and twenty-five hundred for Rosa. She did the arithmetic without meaning to, the way her brain sometimes edited sentences she'd already approved — compulsive, automatic, the math presenting itself before she'd asked for it. She also counted the months. Three. Almost three and a half, since the Hinge match in mid-October. It was January now. The holidays had come and gone. She'd spent Thanksgiving at her mother's and Christmas at her mother's and New Year's Eve at Simone's, watching the ball drop on television while Simone opened a bottle of champagne that cost more than it should have, given what Nora assumed about Simone's finances, and she'd texted Caleb at midnight — *happy new year* — and he'd texted back — *first one I've had in a while that felt like it actually might be* — and the message had made her cry, quietly, in Simone's bathroom, just overflow, she'd told herself, the relief of being wanted at the start of something new.

Three and a half months. Twenty-nine hundred dollars. One photograph of her body. The contents of her interior life — her fears, her desires, the specific architecture of her loneliness, the things she wanted and was ashamed of wanting — handed to him in pieces, message by message, night by night.

And what had Caleb given her?

She didn't let the question form completely. It arrived as a sensation rather than a thought — a flicker, like catching something in her peripheral vision and turning her head too late to see what it was. Not doubt. Something before doubt. The awareness of a door she hadn't checked, a fact she hadn't verified, a ledger that was — if she let herself look at it, which she was not yet doing — significantly unbalanced.

He had given her words. Thousands of them, and they were beautiful words, and they made her feel known and wanted and specifically, precisely seen. He'd given her the outline of a life — the dog, the mother, the business, the drainage plans, the cold arroz con pollo at 2 a.m. — and each detail was vivid and human and the kind of thing you couldn't make up, or at least that's what she told herself, because she was an editor and she knew what fabricated detail looked like, and Caleb's details didn't have the flatness of invention. They had the weird asymmetries of real life. A mother who sent food through the mail. A broken microwave he hadn't gotten around to fixing. An aunt named Elena who yelled at insurance companies.

But she had never heard his voice. She had never seen his face move in real time — not once in three and a half months. The FaceTime excuses had been steady and individual and plausible: bad camera, job site, head cold, dead battery, visiting his mom in Trujillo with terrible service. Each one was fine. She had accepted each one the way you accept a slightly unlikely explanation from someone you trust, because the alternative — mistrusting them — felt worse than the discomfort of the excuse.

She was not lining the excuses up in a row. She was not making a list. She was eating cold curry and watching a small man comb a Pomeranian and she was not making a list. She was simply aware, for the first time, at the very edge of her attention, that there was a list she could make. That if she sat down and looked at everything she'd given — the money, the photo, the access to her body's desires and her loneliness and her hope — and then looked at what she could prove he'd given her, the two columns would not match.

The dog show ended. The Pomeranian won, which seemed correct — the small man's hands had been genuinely beautiful, each pass of the comb so deliberate and tender that she was glad the judges had seen it. Nora turned off the television. The apartment went quiet except for the radiator's intermittent clanking and the sound of the upstairs neighbor's television, muffled through the ceiling, the laugh track of a sitcom she couldn't identify.

She checked her phone one last time before bed. A message from Caleb: **goodnight beautiful. thank you for being the person you are. I don't take it for granted.** A message from Simone: **brunch sunday? found a new place in greenpoint that does a french toast situation you need to experience. bringing my appetite and my emotional baggage.**

She answered Simone first — **yes. always yes to a french toast situation** — and then Caleb: **goodnight. tell Rosa I'm thinking about her. and fix your microwave, you animal.**

His reply came fast. **the microwave is a lifestyle choice at this point.**

She plugged in her phone and went to the bathroom and brushed her teeth and looked at herself in the mirror above the sink. The face looking back was the same one she'd photographed three nights ago — the same dark curls, the same brown eyes, the same olive skin that Caleb said he thought about when he couldn't sleep — except tonight the face had a different quality — something in the set of her mouth, a weight behind the eyes she didn't remember putting there. She looked like a woman standing at the edge of a room she hadn't decided to enter.

She turned off the bathroom light and went to bed. The radiator had cycled off and the room was cooling and the draft from the cracked window moved across her face. She pulled the covers up and curled onto her side and thought about the photograph — her body, her face, the specific expression she'd been wearing, honest and undone and not performing anything — traveling through servers and switches and fiber-optic cables to a phone in Portland, where a man she loved had received it and called her beautiful and promised to keep it forever.

She trusted him. The trust was real and the love was real and the desire was real, and the small thing at the edge of her vision — the unchecked door, the unbalanced ledger — was just the noise that any intelligent woman generated when she was falling for someone she'd met online, the reasonable caution that existed alongside the unreasonable hope, the sleepless watchfulness that lived under her skin even now, pressing against the warmth, testing it.

She fell asleep telling herself this, and in the morning the thought was still there but smaller, like a mark she'd made in pencil that could still be erased, and she erased it, and she checked her phone, and Caleb had sent her a photo of Bruno asleep on a pile of laundry with the caption **he has no respect for the concept of clean clothes**, and she laughed, and the day went on.


# Chapter 11

Simone was thinking about edge cases when Nora said the thing that broke the evening in half.

Not edge cases generally — a specific one. She and the platform team had spent the afternoon debugging a checkout flow where users who entered an expired coupon code got kicked to a 404 page instead of getting an inline error message, so they had to restart the entire purchase — and 23 percent of them abandoned the cart entirely. Classic failure of error handling. You think you've accounted for every user path and then some idiot types HOLIDAY2023 into a field in January and the whole flow detonates.

She was thinking about this because it was easier than thinking about the OpenClaw dashboard notification she'd silenced forty minutes ago — a sentiment deviation flag on the Caleb system. The AI had sent Nora a message about missing Portland in winter, the fog that came in off the river, the way everything smelled like wet pine, and Simone hadn't written that. She'd built the broad parameters for Portland nostalgia, but the sensory details were the system's own interpolation from data Nora had shared about loving fall in the Northeast. The AI was getting better at being from Portland than Simone was at making it be from Portland. A development she needed to address, but not now, because now she was eating pappardelle across from her best friend at the Italian place on Metropolitan and the thing she needed most in the world was for this to be a normal Tuesday.

"So I did a thing," Nora said.

She said it the way she always said it when she was about to reveal something she was excited about but slightly defensive of — she looked down at her wine, then up, then tilted her head in the way that meant *I know this might sound crazy but I need you to be on my side*. Simone had seen this expression maybe two hundred times across seventeen years. It preceded the announcement that Nora was quitting her first publishing job. It preceded the confession that she'd slept with the author she was editing. It preceded the Hinge download. Simone had always been the person this face was aimed at, the person whose response mattered, and she had always given the right response, because she was good at this and because she loved Nora and because these two things, the skill and the love, had not yet torn each other apart.

"What kind of thing?" Simone asked. Warm. Curious. The exact right amount of I-already-support-this.

Nora pulled her phone from her bag, tapped something, and turned the screen around. It was a flight confirmation. Alaska Airlines. JFK to PDX. Departing Saturday, January 18th. Returning Tuesday the 21st.

"I booked a flight to Portland," Nora said. "I'm going to surprise him."

The pappardelle sat in Simone's stomach like something with edges. She felt the information enter her body the way you feel the first second of a car accident — the world going glassy and specific, every detail sharpening into itself. The waiter two tables over straightening a fork. The Lucinda Williams song coming through the ceiling speakers, something about a car and a lake road. The overhead light catching the oil on the surface of Nora's water glass.

She had about forty-five seconds to not visibly panic.

"Oh my god," Simone said, and the warmth in her voice was so convincing that she almost believed it came from the part of her that loved Nora rather than the part that was already running triage. "Nor, that's — when did you decide this?"

"Today. I was at my desk and I just — I'm so tired of waiting, Si. We've been doing this for three months and I've never even heard his voice in real time and I know, I know, there are reasons, but at some point the reasons are just — they're a pattern, right? And I don't want to be the kind of person who lets a pattern talk her out of something real."

"Right. No, totally."

Her mind was moving very fast in a direction that had nothing to do with the conversation she was performing. Caleb Mora was text on servers. He had a Hinge profile, a VoIP number, a Venmo account. He did not have a body. He did not have an apartment in Portland or a dog named Bruno. If Nora got on that plane, the entire architecture of the past three months would collapse in the time it took her to ring a doorbell that no one she loved would answer.

Nora was looking at her with wet eyes and a lifted chin — the look of someone who wanted to be told she was brave.

"I'm proud of you," Simone said. The words came out steady. The fork in her hand was steady. Underneath the table, her left knee was bouncing so hard the silverware on her side was vibrating against the wood. "This is — I mean, this is a big step."

"I know. I'm kind of terrified? But I feel like if I don't do this now I'm going to keep finding reasons not to, and then I'll be one of those people who has this whole imaginary relationship with someone she's never actually been in the same room with, and that's — I can't be that."

*You already are*, Simone thought, and the thought was so clean and so cruel that she flinched at her own mind.

"Have you told him?" she asked instead, which was the right question — the question a good friend would ask — and also the question she needed the answer to most urgently, because the system's response protocol was a different crisis depending on whether Nora had already texted.

"Not yet. I want it to be a surprise. I was thinking I'd text him Thursday and say I had a work trip that got rerouted or something, and then show up." Nora's face was doing the thing it did when she was constructing a plan she knew was slightly unhinged but couldn't stop herself from building. "Is that insane? Am I being insane?"

"It's not insane. It's romantic."

"It's a little insane."

"It's romantic-insane. The good kind."

Simone picked up her wine and drank. The Montepulciano was too warm and slightly corked and she barely tasted it. She was building a decision tree in her head: paths branching, costs assigned, risk surfaces mapped. If Nora texted Thursday, that gave Simone two days. If Nora texted tonight, she had hours. Either way, Caleb had to not be in Portland when Nora arrived. Caleb had to not be anywhere Nora could get to. Caleb had to be in a crisis so sympathetic that suggesting Nora come to him would feel inappropriate.

An emergency. A family emergency.

"Excuse me," Simone said. She put her napkin on the table and picked up her phone with what she hoped was the casual ease of someone who needed to pee and not the rigid precision of someone whose hands were about to do the most important work of her life. "Bathroom."

"I'll be right here," Nora said, "emotionally spiraling."

Simone smiled at her. The warmth was automatic. She was very good at this — she had become so good at performing the friend that the performance was indistinguishable from the thing itself, even to her, especially to her, and there were whole minutes of every day when she forgot she was lying, which were the minutes she felt most human, which meant her humanity had become contingent on the lie, which was a thought she could not afford to have right now because right now she needed to get to the fucking bathroom.

---

The bathroom at Lucia's had two stalls, both empty, and a mirror with a crack across the top right corner that had been there since 2022. Simone locked herself in the larger stall, sat on the toilet lid, and opened the OpenClaw app.

The dashboard loaded. The Lucinda Williams song bled through the wall. The radiator clanked. Caleb Mora's profile filled the screen: conversation activity, sentiment metrics, the timeline of tonight's exchange. The last message Nora had received was *Can't stop thinking about you today. Everything at the site reminded me of that thing you said about how cities that plant trees along the sidewalks are cities that believe in the future.*

The AI had generated that — not Simone. She'd written the parameters, but the words were the system's own. Nora was falling in love with Simone's creation's creation. She didn't have time for this.

She opened the command interface. The system allowed direct prompt overrides — she'd built that capability in after the first time the AI had improvised something she hadn't anticipated, back in November, when it told Nora something about her father that had been startlingly perceptive and that Simone had definitely not programmed. The override let her inject narrative elements into Caleb's next response sequence. She'd used it four times. Three of those had been minor corrections — adjusting a detail about Portland geography, tuning the frequency of his texts so he didn't seem too available. The fourth time she'd had him mention his mother's health in a way that laid the groundwork for the $2,500 ask.

This was the fifth time. She typed fast, her thumbs clumsy on the screen:

*PRIORITY OVERRIDE: Deploy family emergency. Caleb's father has had a stroke. Caleb must fly to Chicago immediately. Family crisis will require at minimum one week of reduced availability. Emotional register: scared, overwhelmed, guilty about not being present, unable to make plans. DO NOT engage with any discussion of Nora visiting Portland or meeting in person. If asked, redirect to the severity of the medical situation. If pushed, express that he cannot handle logistics right now.*

She reviewed the override. The stroke was good — serious enough to require immediate travel, medical enough that Nora wouldn't question the disruption, open-ended enough to buy time. Chicago was good — Caleb's father was supposed to live in the suburbs of Chicago, she'd established that in a conversation weeks ago, a detail Nora wouldn't remember precisely but would accept. The key constraint was timing: when should the message arrive? If it came now, while Simone was in the bathroom, the coincidence might register. If it came later tonight, after Nora was home, Simone wouldn't be able to monitor Nora's reaction in real time.

She set the delivery for twenty minutes. That gave her time to get back to the table, order another glass of wine, and be visibly present when Nora's phone buzzed.

Her hands were shaking. She looked at them — her fingers against the phone case, the chipped nail polish on her left index finger that she hadn't had time to fix because she'd been up until two in the morning adjusting the AI's response latency to seem more natural — and the shaking was a fact she noted and filed. She could not shake at the table. At the table she was warm and supportive and excited about the Portland trip. At the table she was Simone, Nora's best friend, the person who wanted Nora to be happy. In this bathroom stall, sitting on a toilet she hadn't used in a restaurant where she'd had dozens of normal dinners, she was someone she still couldn't name, and the distance between that woman and the one she performed was getting smaller every week.

She flushed the toilet she hadn't used, washed her hands she hadn't dirtied, checked her reflection in the cracked mirror. She looked fine. She looked exactly like someone who'd gone to the bathroom during dinner and not like someone who'd just induced a stroke in a fictional man's fictional father to prevent her best friend from discovering the most elaborate lie she'd ever told.

"OK," she said to her reflection. The word came out flat.

She went back to the table.

---

Nora was reading something on her phone when Simone sat down, and for one cardiac-loss second Simone thought the message had come early — but it was just Instagram, some book account reposting a passage from a novel Nora had edited two years ago. Nora turned the screen to show her.

"Look, it's getting traction again. Thirty thousand likes."

"Amazing. Send it to the author."

"I did. He said, 'Tell them to buy the hardcover.'"

Simone laughed. The food was getting cold. She picked up her fork and ate pappardelle and counted the minutes. Twelve until the override deployed. She talked to Nora about the manuscript with the em-dash-worshipping author. She talked about a new ramen place that had opened on Driggs that was supposedly transcendent. She asked about Diane and listened to Nora's answer with the full attention of someone who was not tracking a countdown in her peripheral consciousness. Nora talked about her mother's new obsession — Diane had started watching a Korean drama on Netflix and was texting Nora commentary in real time, multiple messages per episode, detailed opinions about the love interest's haircut and the second lead's moral failings. Nora did the voice, the exasperated-but-loving impersonation of her mother's texting style, and Simone found it genuinely funny, which was the horror of her life — that she could be genuinely amused by the person she was actively destroying.

Eight minutes.

Six.

Nora's phone buzzed.

She glanced at it. Her face changed. The shift was subtle — a contraction around the eyes, the smile not dropping but going rigid, the way her mouth opened slightly when she was processing something that didn't fit the evening's emotional framework. She picked up the phone.

"Oh my god," Nora said.

Simone knew what the message said because she'd written the parameters fifteen minutes ago in a bathroom stall, but she didn't know the exact words because the AI had generated those, and so the experience of watching Nora read them was, in a way she was not prepared for, suspenseful even to her.

"What? What happened?"

"Caleb's dad had a stroke. He's — oh my god, Si, he's flying to Chicago tonight."

Nora's voice had the quality it got when she was genuinely scared for someone else — a thinness, the self-deprecation and humor stripped out, the person underneath the performance. Simone had heard this voice when Nora's mother had a cancer scare two years ago. The real Nora, the one who didn't do bits, the one who just hurt and needed someone to sit with her in it.

"Shit. Is his dad OK?"

Simone's voice was concerned and steady and exactly what Nora needed. Under the table, her knee had stopped bouncing. The crisis, now that it was deployed, had a calming effect. The variables were in motion. The system was responding to the override. She was back in the control seat — not fully, because the AI's word choices were its own, but structurally, architecturally, the narrative was moving in the direction she'd set.

"He doesn't know yet. He's at the airport — his mom called and — hold on." Nora was typing fast, the way she typed when she was emotionally activated — full words, proper punctuation, no abbreviations. Simone could see the WhatsApp interface from across the table, the green bubbles accumulating. Nora was telling Caleb she was there for him. She was telling him to call if he needed anything. She was telling him she loved his family even though she'd never met them and they didn't exist and the man she was texting wasn't a man.

"The Portland flight," Nora said, looking up. "I mean, obviously I'm not going to surprise him now. But maybe I should come to Chicago instead. Be there for him."

There it was. The edge case Simone hadn't modeled. Or rather — she had modeled it, she'd anticipated that Nora's instinct would be to go to wherever Caleb was, because Nora's love language was showing up, had always been showing up, the physical act of being present when someone was hurting. She'd included the DO NOT ENGAGE directive in the override, but that governed what the AI would say, not what Nora would want.

"To Chicago," Simone repeated, and she heard the flatness in her own voice and corrected it in real time. "I mean, that's — your impulse is beautiful, Nor, but he's going to be at the hospital with his family. You've never met them. Showing up during a medical emergency when you haven't met the family —"

"I know. I know it's a lot."

"It's not a lot to feel it. But maybe give him space to deal first? You don't want to be the thing he has to manage on top of everything else."

She watched the calculation happen behind Nora's eyes — the weight of wanting to go versus the weight of being reasonable. Nora's reasonableness was one of her most exploitable qualities: she was smart enough to talk herself out of an instinct, even a good one, if someone she trusted framed the alternative compellingly enough.

"You're right," Nora said. Then, less certainly: "You're right. I just — I hate this. I hate that something terrible is happening to him and I can't do anything."

"You can be the person he comes back to. That's doing something."

Nora nodded. She looked at her phone. Another message had arrived — the AI, running the crisis protocol, was generating scared-grateful-overwhelmed text at the timed intervals Simone had specified. Nora read it and her eyes filled.

"He said his mom's alone at the hospital. His sister's driving from Milwaukee."

Caleb didn't have a sister. Simone had never given him a sister. The AI had invented one — a narrative detail generated to thicken the family emergency, to make it feel real and populated and specific. The system was worldbuilding on its own, adding characters to support the story, and Simone felt a chill that started in her wrists and moved up her forearms, not because the sister was a problem — it wasn't, it was actually good, the kind of detail that made the emergency feel lived-in — but because the machine she'd built was writing fiction she hadn't authorized, and the fiction was better than she'd expected, and the gap between her authorship and the AI's was narrowing in a way she hadn't designed for.

"I'm going to change my flight," Nora said. "I'll cancel Portland and go to Chicago instead. I'll wait until things calm down and then I'll go and I'll just — I'll be there."

Fuck. The relief of Portland being off the table had lasted maybe ninety seconds and now Chicago was on the table and Chicago was worse in some ways because Simone had given Caleb a father who lived in the Chicago suburbs without ever specifying which suburb, and if Nora started looking up hospitals or asking which one his father was at, the specificity problem would multiply until the whole architecture buckled.

"Nor." Simone reached across the table and put her hand on Nora's wrist. The touch was calculated and genuine at the same time — she was performing concern, but the concern was real, because what she felt for Nora in these moments was something so knotted and contradictory that no single word could hold it. Love and guilt and protectiveness and resentment and the exhaustion of being the one person in the world who knew how much danger Nora was in, because Simone was the danger. "Just — take a breath. Don't change the flight tonight. Let things settle. You can always rebook tomorrow or the day after."

"You're right." Nora picked up her wine and drank. She set the glass down and picked up her phone and started scrolling through the messages again, reading them in order, the way she read manuscripts — start to finish, looking for the shape of the thing. "I want to show you what he said earlier — before all this, that thing about the trees. Hold on, I sent it to you — where's your phone?"

And before Simone could stop her, Nora reached across the table and picked up Simone's phone.

The OpenClaw app was open. The dashboard was right there on the screen — the conversation log with Nora's messages in blue and the AI's in gray, the sentiment metrics in the sidebar, the override command Simone had entered fifteen minutes ago in green text at the top of the display. The entire architecture of the lie condensed into one glowing rectangle that Nora was now holding in her hand.

Simone's body did something she didn't decide. Her left hand shot forward and took the phone from Nora's grip, and her right thumb swiped up on the app in the same motion — closing it — the gesture so fast it was almost a flinch. By the time the phone was back in her hand the screen was showing her home screen: the photo of her and Nora at the beach last summer, both of them sunburned and laughing, a picture from a world where this was still just a friendship and not whatever it had become.

"Sorry," Simone said. "I had — I was in the middle of a work thing. Slack. My PM's been messaging me about the coupon flow all night."

Nora looked at her. The look lasted two seconds. Two seconds during which Nora's eyes moved from Simone's face to Simone's hand — the hand holding the phone — and Simone realized the hand was trembling. Not a lot. Enough.

"Your hand is shaking," Nora said.

"Too much coffee. I had a triple shot at four and I can still feel it."

"You need to switch to decaf after two."

"I know. I'm a disaster."

The moment passed. It passed the way all the near-misses passed — not with a clean resolution but with a thin film of plausibility laid over a crack, and the crack was still there, and the film was getting thinner, and someday the weight of what was underneath would break through. But not tonight. Tonight the film held.

Simone found the screenshot of Caleb's tree message on her own phone and showed it to Nora from her own hand, the screen angled carefully, the display managed. Nora read it and smiled — a sad smile that had the full weight of a woman who loved a man whose father was in the hospital — and the smile was real even though the man was not, and Simone held her phone steady and felt something that was not quite guilt and not quite pride and not quite despair but all three at once, layered like the rings of a cut tree, each one representing a season she'd survived by lying.

---

Nora went to the bathroom at 9:47. Simone knew the time because she checked it, because she had approximately three minutes to do what she needed to do.

She opened the dashboard. The conversation log showed Nora's last messages — a string of supportive, loving, generous texts that Simone read with the clinical eye of a QA tester reviewing user input. Nora was still offering to fly to Chicago. Nora was asking which hospital. Nora was saying she could take Friday off, she could be there by Friday night, she could bring food, she could do whatever he needed. The AI was deflecting — *I don't want you to upend your whole life for this, my mom's here, my sister's here, I need to focus on my dad* — but Nora was persistent with the tenacity of a woman whose love expressed itself as presence, and no amount of algorithmic deflection was going to override seventeen years of being someone who showed up.

Simone needed a different strategy. The emergency had bought her time but hadn't solved the underlying problem: Nora wanted physical proximity. Nora wanted to be in the same room as this man. As long as that desire was active, it would keep generating scenarios — Portland, Chicago, a layover, a long weekend — and each scenario was a new surface for the deception to crack against.

She needed to make Nora not want to go. Not because of logistics. Because of hurt.

The thought arrived whole, the way solutions sometimes did in her UX work — not assembled incrementally but dropped into her mind fully formed, the way a key drops into a lock. She could manufacture a fight. She could have the AI say something that pushed Nora away just enough to cancel the trip without destroying the relationship entirely. Something sharp but not devastating. Something that landed in the part of Nora that was already insecure about being too much — too needy, too intense, too willing to rearrange her life for a man she'd never touched.

She knew this part of Nora. She'd known it for seventeen years. She knew that Nora's deepest fear was not being unlovable but being excessive — being the person who cared more, who showed up too hard, who gave more than the moment required and then had to pretend the asymmetry didn't sting. She knew this because Nora had told her, late at night, wine-honest and unguarded, and Simone had held that information the way you hold a key to someone's apartment — carefully, protectively, and with the persistent low-grade awareness that you could use it to get in when they weren't home.

She typed the override:

*SHIFT TONE: Caleb is stressed, overwhelmed, and feeling smothered by Nora's response. He appreciates her support but needs distance. Deliver one line that communicates he finds her reaction to the crisis disproportionate. Frame it as stress-talk, not cruelty — he should sound like a man at his breaking point, not a man who does not love her. Key phrase to include: "you're being a lot right now." Deploy within the next three message exchanges, triggered by Nora's next offer to visit or help.*

She sent the override. Her hands were steady. The shaking had stopped — its own kind of tell. Simone's hands shook when she was panicking and steadied when she was doing something terrible on purpose. The decision had settled her. She was no longer reacting to a crisis. She was engineering an outcome.

She closed the app. She put the phone face-down on the table. She picked up her wine and drank and waited for Nora to come back from the bathroom so she could watch the weapon land.

Nora returned with red-rimmed eyes. She'd been crying — not the performative kind, not the *I'm literally sobbing, why did you send me that dog video* crying she did for comic effect, but the quiet, compressed crying of someone who was worried about a person she loved and couldn't reach and had to worry about alone, in a restaurant bathroom stall. Funny, in a way that Simone could appreciate only from the angle of knowing that she'd caused every piece of this.

"He just texted back," Nora said, sitting down. "His dad's in surgery."

There was no surgery. There was no dad. There was an AI generating medical drama in real time to sustain a narrative that Simone had engineered to prevent her best friend from getting on a plane, and the AI was doing it convincingly, with the emotional texture of a man in crisis, and Nora was reading these words and feeling something genuine and painful and entirely real, and Simone was sitting across from her and nodding and squeezing her hand and saying "I'm here, I'm right here" while the phone in her lap held the proof that the person who was *here* was the same person who'd made all of this up.

"I told him I want to come to Chicago," Nora said. "To be with him while his dad recovers. He said —" She looked at her phone. Her face changed.

Simone watched it happen. She watched the exact moment the override deployed — the words appearing on Nora's screen, the words Simone had ordered and the system had generated — and whatever phrasing the AI had chosen, it landed. She could see it land. Nora's face went from soft and worried to something else, something contracting inward, the expression of a person who's been told they're too much by someone they love.

"What'd he say?" Simone asked, and her voice was gentle, because under pressure she became gentler, warmer, more perfectly calibrated, and that was her tell — the more dangerous she was, the kinder she sounded.

Nora turned the phone around. The message was on the screen:

*Nora I love that you want to be here but honestly you're being a lot right now. My dad is in surgery and I can't manage your feelings about this on top of everything else. I need you to give me space. Please.*

The words were worse than what Simone had specified. She'd directed *you're being a lot right now* and the AI had wrapped it in a paragraph that was cutting in its precision — *I can't manage your feelings about this* was the sharpest edge, the implication that Nora's worry was a burden, a task, something that required management rather than something that deserved gratitude. And the *Please* at the end read not as politeness but as exhaustion. The AI had taken the directive and honed it. It had known, through whatever pattern recognition it had developed from months of analyzing Nora's messages, exactly where to place the blade and how deep to push.

Nora put the phone face-down on the table. The gesture was precise, deliberate — she lined the phone up with the edge of her placemat the way she aligned manuscripts on her desk before editing, imposing order on the material before she dealt with it. She picked up her wine glass and drank, and the drinking was controlled too, measured, every physical action managed because if she stopped managing them she would come apart.

"He's stressed," Simone said. "People say things they don't mean when they're scared."

"Yeah."

"He doesn't mean it. You know he doesn't mean it."

"He said *manage your feelings*. Like I'm — like being worried about his father is some kind of chore he doesn't want to deal with."

Simone reached across and took Nora's hand. Nora let her take it but didn't squeeze back. Her fingers were cold from the wine glass and slightly damp.

"He's sitting in an airport and his dad just had a stroke," Simone said, building the defense for the accusation she'd authored. "He's terrified and exhausted and he doesn't know how to be vulnerable about it, so he's pushing away the person closest to him. That's what people do."

"I know. I know that's what people do." Nora pulled her hand back. She looked at the table, at the bread basket neither of them had touched in the last hour, at the candle in the wine bottle dripping wax onto the checkered cloth. "I'm going to cancel the Portland flight."

"You don't have to decide right now."

"I'm deciding it right now." She picked up her phone and opened the Alaska Airlines app and Simone watched her cancel a ticket she'd bought that morning — $387 for a round trip to visit a man who lived nowhere — and the act of cancellation was quick and deliberate and absolutely final. A few taps. A confirmation prompt. Done.

Nora put the phone down. She looked at Simone, and her eyes were dry now, her face wearing the quality it took on when she'd been hurt and was refusing to show the full shape of the wound — the controlled face, the editorial face, the one that assessed damage the way she assessed bad prose: coolly, from a professional distance that cost her everything to maintain.

"He's never talked to me like that before," Nora said.

And that was true. Caleb had never talked to Nora like that because Simone had never programmed him to, because until tonight there had been no reason to deploy cruelty through the instrument Simone had built to deliver love. Every parameter, every prompt, every scripted response had been engineered to make Nora feel seen and wanted and safe. And now Simone had used the same system to deliver a wound, and the wound had landed exactly where she'd aimed — in the part of Nora that feared being too much.

"He'll apologize," Simone said. "When things calm down. He'll feel terrible."

"Maybe."

"He will. You know he will. This isn't who he is. This is a crisis."

Nora nodded. She flagged the waiter — a young guy with gel-stiff hair and a Band-Aid on his thumb that he kept pressing against the edge of his apron, a small nervous habit, some kitchen cut he was keeping clean — and asked for the check. Simone let her pay. Nora always paid, not because Simone asked but because Nora's default was generosity and Simone's default was letting her be generous, and the dynamic had been in place so long it felt like weather, like something neither of them had chosen.

They put on their coats. The January air hit them at the door — that New York winter cold that found every gap, the inch of neck between collar and scarf, the strip of wrist between glove and sleeve. Nora hunched against it. She was shorter than Simone by four inches and the cold always seemed to find her faster, to take more from her, and tonight she looked smaller still, diminished by something that had nothing to do with temperature.

"I'm going to walk," Nora said. "I need the air."

"Want company?"

"I think I want to be alone for a bit."

"OK." Simone hugged her, and the hug was real — her arms around Nora, Nora's dark curls against her chin, the smell of Nora's shampoo, something with argan oil, the same brand she'd used since college — and the realness of the hug made Simone want to cry and the wanting made her furious at herself and the fury made the hug tighter, which Nora probably read as love, because it was, because everything was.

"I'm sorry tonight went sideways," Nora said into Simone's coat.

"Don't apologize. Just take care of yourself, OK?"

Nora pulled back. She tried a smile — a thin, effortful thing that cost her visibly. Then she turned and walked down Metropolitan toward the water, her breath visible in the streetlight, her phone in her coat pocket where Caleb was still generating messages she wouldn't read until she got home because she needed distance from the first person who'd ever told her she was too much.

Except the first person who'd told her that was Simone. Seventeen years of being the audience for Nora's intensities — Nora's enthusiasms, Nora's anxieties, Nora's love that always arrived too large for the container it was offered to — and Simone had absorbed them and cataloged them and loved them and resented them, and now the catalog had been converted into targeting data and fired through a system Simone had built on a platform designed for customer service chatbots, and the shot had landed, and Nora was walking away hurt, and Simone stood on the sidewalk outside the restaurant and watched her go and did not call after her.

She took out her phone when Nora had turned the corner. The dashboard showed the conversation metrics: Nora's response sentiment had dropped from .82 to .41 in the past hour. The system flagged it as a significant negative deviation and recommended a recovery protocol — *Suggest empathetic follow-up within 6-12 hours to prevent relationship degradation* — and Simone stared at the recommendation and closed the app.

She could deploy the recovery tomorrow. Tonight the damage needed to sit. Tonight Nora needed to feel the hurt, to let the phrase *being a lot* settle into the part of her where it would do the most work — the part where it would make her cautious, make her smaller, make her less likely to book a flight or demand a video call or do any of the things that a woman in love was supposed to do when she wanted to prove the love was real.

Simone walked to the subway. The platform was cold and nearly empty — a man in a Carhartt jacket reading a paperback with broken binding, a teenager scrolling her phone with one earbud in and one out, nodding to something no one else could hear. Simone stood at the edge and looked down the dark tunnel for the approaching light and she thought about the words she'd chosen: *you're being a lot right now*. She'd chosen them because she knew, with the intimacy of someone who'd been listening for seventeen years, that they were the worst thing Caleb could say to Nora. Not cruel. Not abusive. Just the surgical phrase that would make Nora doubt herself rather than doubt him. The kind of thing a stressed man might plausibly say and plausibly regret, the kind of thing that doesn't end a relationship but changes its interior atmosphere — tilts the balance so that the person who was hurt spends more energy wondering if she deserved it than questioning whether the person who said it was real.

The train came. She got on. She sat in the orange plastic seat and held her phone in her lap and looked at the black window where her reflection looked back — a tall woman in a secondhand coat, angular face, dark bob, someone who appeared to have her life together, and the gap between the appearance and the reality was a number she could calculate: $38,000 in debt, $2,900 in stolen money, one fake boyfriend, one real friendship she was burning from the inside.

The train rocked through the tunnel. The overhead lights flickered and steadied. Simone closed her eyes and did the math the way she always did the math, the operational math: Portland was canceled. Chicago was neutralized. The immediate crisis was contained. The relationship was damaged but not destroyed — the AI would apologize tomorrow, gently, with the programmed warmth of a man who'd spoken carelessly under pressure, and Nora would forgive him because Nora always forgave, because forgiveness was another one of her excesses, and her excesses were the reason this worked, and the reason this worked was the reason it was unforgivable.

She opened her eyes. Two more stops. She thought about the sister the AI had invented — Caleb's sister, driving from Milwaukee to be at the hospital — and wondered whether the system would remember the sister in future conversations, whether the sister would become permanent, a new character in the expanding fiction that Nora would ask about and that the AI would have to maintain with its own generated backstory, and then she thought about how the word for that in software was *technical debt* — the accumulation of expedient decisions that eventually made a system unmaintainable, and she thought: *that's what I'm building. Technical debt in a person.*

The train reached her stop. She walked up the stairs and into the cold and down the three blocks to her building and up the four flights to her studio that smelled like the jasmine candle she burned to cover the fry oil from the bodega downstairs. She hung her coat on the hook by the door. She opened her laptop on the desk by the window and pulled up the full conversation log from the evening and read every message — Nora's and Caleb's — from the beginning, the way she always did, the way a showrunner watches dailies, scanning for continuity errors, tonal inconsistencies, the places where the seams showed.

The sister from Milwaukee would need a name by tomorrow. The father would need a hospital, a prognosis, a recovery arc. The crisis she'd invented in three minutes on a toilet lid was going to require days of maintenance, and each detail would generate more details, and each detail was another thing that could be checked, contradicted, caught. The architecture was getting heavier. The structure was designed to support a romance, not a medical drama and a family saga and a travel emergency, and every new load she added was weight the foundation hadn't been built to bear.

She poured herself a glass of the Trader Joe's Pinot Grigio she kept in the fridge — $5.99, the same bottle she'd been buying for two years, the price of it something she noticed every time in the way you notice a price when money is something that happens to you rather than something you have — and sat on the edge of her bed and drank. The wine was thin and cold and tasted like exactly what it cost.

She didn't cry. She didn't cry because she'd gotten through it. She'd prevented the Portland trip. She'd maintained the deception. She'd done what she needed to do to keep the architecture standing, and the architecture had to stand because the architecture was the only thing between Simone and the version of reality where Nora knew everything and looked at her the way you look at someone who isn't who you thought they were — the look Simone had been dreading since October, the look she deserved, the look that would end her.

Outside, the wind found the gaps in her window frame and pushed through, a thin whistle that was just air moving through old caulking, just physics, nothing personal. But tonight it sounded like something that knew.


# Chapter 12

She kept coming back to the phrase, circling it the way she'd circle a problem sentence in a manuscript — the same three words, orbiting, refusing to resolve.

*You're being a lot.*

Nora had been on Simone's couch for two hours. She'd arrived still wearing her coat, her face already wrecked from crying on the train, and Simone had taken one look at her and pulled her inside without asking what happened — the grace of a friendship where asking wasn't always necessary. The coat came off. The sweatshirt went on, an old NYU crewneck of Simone's, soft enough that the cotton had gone nearly translucent at the elbows. The wine appeared. The couch accepted her.

Now she was tucked into the corner with her knees drawn up, turning the words over like a stone she couldn't stop touching.

*You're being a lot.*

She'd read books about this. Not self-help — she'd rather swallow glass — but novels where women were told they wanted too much, felt too much, were too much, and she'd always known, reading those scenes, exactly what the author wanted her to feel, and she'd felt it obediently and then closed the book and gone on with her life. It was different when the words arrived on your phone screen at 11:47 p.m. from a man you'd been patient with for months, a man you'd sent money to without being asked, a man whose face you'd only ever seen in photographs, because you'd been so goddamn understanding about the broken laptop camera and the bad reception and the cold he was fighting.

A man you'd tried to surprise by flying across the country because you wanted — you only wanted — to stand in the same room with him.

"It's such a specific phrase," she said. Simone was sitting on the other end of the couch with her legs folded under her, holding her own wine with both hands, a strand of dark hair caught at the corner of her mouth that she hadn't bothered to push away. "Being a lot. Not 'this is a lot' — that would be about the situation. 'You're being a lot.' That's about me. That's a character note."

"It's a shitty thing to say," Simone said.

"Right, but it's also the kind of thing you can't argue with, because the act of arguing with it proves the point. If I push back, I'm being a lot about being told I'm being a lot. It's a closed loop."

She lifted her wine. Simone's apartment was warm in the January way of old Williamsburg radiators — the unit under the window hissed and clanked against the wall, pushing heat with the persistence of something that didn't know how to stop, and the air smelled like the candle Simone always had going, jasmine and something synthetic underneath, sweet enough that Nora noticed it every time she walked in and forgot it within fifteen minutes. The window had condensation on the inside. Through it, the street was a smear of yellow lights and the occasional bass thud from a car that passed too close to the building.

"I just wanted to see him," Nora said. "I keep going over this, and the thing I can't get past is that I wasn't asking for anything unreasonable. We've been talking for three months. Three months, Simone. I haven't heard his voice. I haven't touched him. I haven't been in the same room with him. And when I said, okay, let me come to you, let me show up in your actual life, his dad had a stroke — which, I feel terrible about, obviously — but then I said I'd come to Chicago instead, and that's when he —"

She stopped. Took a breath. The wine was a Malbec, Simone's Malbec, from the place on Bedford with the handwritten labels — twelve dollars, which Simone had once told her was her ceiling for wine because she'd done the math on what a glass cost at that price point versus going out. Nora didn't do that math. She bought wine by finding something she liked and buying it. She was starting to understand that this was a luxury, not a personality trait.

"I feel pathetic," she said.

"You're not pathetic."

"I'm crying on your couch about a man I've never met in person. By any reasonable metric, that's pathetic."

"By any reasonable metric, most of dating is pathetic. I went out with a guy last month who told me about his crypto portfolio before the appetizers came. I sat there. I stayed for the main course. Who's pathetic?"

Nora laughed, which hurt — her face was swollen from crying, the muscles around her eyes still tender from the contortion of it. She pressed the heels of her hands against her cheekbones. "What if he's right, though? What if I'm too much?"

"He's not right."

"But what if this is what I do? What if I climb inside a relationship before the other person has even opened the door, and then I stand there wondering why they're looking at me like I broke in?"

Simone was quiet for a moment. She shifted on the couch, resettling her weight, and when she spoke her voice had the particular steadiness it got when she was being careful about something. "Nor. You didn't break in anywhere. You bought a plane ticket to visit a guy you've been dating for months. That's not breaking in. That's showing up."

"He didn't see it that way."

"Then that's his problem. Not yours."

Nora wanted to believe that. She pressed her cheek against the arm of the couch, which was nubby and cool and smelled faintly of Febreze — Simone used it because she couldn't afford to have the upholstery cleaned. The cushion sank under her weight. Simone had bought this couch secondhand two years ago, and it had developed a topography of its own, the springs giving way in patterns that recorded who sat where and for how long.

"I sent him money," Nora said. Not for the first time tonight. "For the dog. For his mom. And he told me I was being a lot."

Simone didn't answer immediately. She took a sip of wine, and in the pause Nora heard the radiator cycling, the tick and ping of old pipes expanding inside the wall, and somewhere above them a neighbor walking from one side of their apartment to the other, the footsteps traveling across the ceiling like a sentence being read.

"People are strange about money," Simone said. "Especially when they're the ones who need it."

"Is that what you think? That he's embarrassed?"

"I think —" Simone started, and then stopped. She put her wine on the floor beside the couch — she had a habit of doing this, setting things down when her hands needed to be empty — and Nora saw something cross her face, fast, almost too fast to catch: a tightening around the mouth, a quick inhale, as if she were about to speak and the words had gotten stuck sideways in her throat. Her eyes went to the window and stayed there.

"What?" Nora said.

"Nothing. I think you should let yourself feel shitty tonight and figure out the rest tomorrow."

"Very therapeutic."

"I didn't say I was a therapist. I said drink your wine and feel shitty and let me sit here with you while you do it."

Nora felt the wine warming her hands through the glass, and with it a loosening in her chest — not because the hurt was smaller but because Simone's terrible couch and terrible candle and twelve-dollar Malbec were holding it, absorbing it, giving slightly under its weight.

They talked. Not about Caleb, for a while — about other things, drifting between subjects with the ease of women who'd been narrating their lives to each other for seventeen years and could shift registers without announcement. Simone told a story about a coworker named Priya who'd been stealing lunches from the office fridge, and the Slack channel the team had created to track it, and the anonymous note someone left that said THIEF in Sharpie on a Post-it, and the fact that the thief had responded with another Post-it that said PROVE IT, and how Priya — who sat two desks from Simone and had a collection of jade plants on her monitor stand and who Simone was reasonably sure was not the thief — had started bringing two lunches to work out of pure solidarity with whoever was. Nora laughed until the laughing hurt her face again and the hurting reminded her of Caleb and the reminder made her stop.

"Can I tell you something terrible?" Nora said.

"Always."

"When he said that — 'you're being a lot' — part of me thought, okay, this is what it takes. This is the price of admission. This is what it costs to be with somebody. You give and you give and eventually they tell you the giving is too much, and you learn to want less so they'll stay."

"That's not terrible. That's honest."
