"It's terrible because I'm willing to do it. I know exactly what it sounds like — I'd edit it out of a manuscript in a heartbeat — and I'd still do it. I'd learn to want less if it meant he'd stay."

Simone pulled one leg up onto the cushion and turned toward Nora. "You remember sophomore year? Psych 101, the exam week where we were so sleep-deprived we walked to that diner at three in the morning and ordered pancakes and you told me you didn't think you were lovable in the way that made people stay?"

"Vaguely."

"I told you that was bullshit, and it was, and it still is. You're not too much. The right person would cross the country to see you, not the other way around."

Nora felt the tears start again — not the hard crying from earlier but something slower, seeping, the kind that didn't require any contortion of the face. She wiped her cheek on the sleeve of Simone's sweatshirt and the cotton came away dark.

"I don't know what I'd do without you," she said.

She meant it completely, unselfconsciously, without the usual editorial instinct to qualify. She meant it like a first draft — before revision, before the second pass where you added the hedge and the caveat and the ironic distance. Raw.

Simone opened her mouth.

The pause that followed was long enough that Nora looked up, and what she saw on Simone's face was something she couldn't name — a kind of held suspension, every muscle engaged in the act of not doing something, someone standing at an edge and deciding between jumping and stepping back. Her eyes were wet. Nora hadn't seen Simone cry in years — Simone didn't cry, not visibly, not in front of anyone who might remember it — and the wetness sat on her lower lashes without falling, held there by surface tension or by will.

Then Simone smiled. It was the right smile — warm, steady, arriving at exactly the right moment — and she said, "You'd manage. But you shouldn't have to."

They watched a movie, or they put on a movie, which wasn't the same thing. Simone chose something they'd both seen, a romantic comedy from the late nineties they'd watched enough times that neither needed to follow the plot, which meant they could talk during it or not talk during it and neither option was a failure. Simone got a blanket from the closet — the thick knitted one, cream-colored, a birthday gift from someone Nora couldn't remember — and they shared it. This meant Simone shifted to the middle of the couch and their knees touched and then their shoulders touched, and at some point Nora's head was on Simone's shoulder and the movie was still going and she was watching the actors' mouths move without processing the words. The radiator was still clicking. The candle had burned down to a sputtering disk of flame inside the glass.

Nora fell asleep before the credits. She felt Simone reach for the remote, and the dialogue dropped away, and then the lamp went off behind her eyelids, and Simone settled back and Nora adjusted her weight against Simone's shoulder without fully waking. The apartment went still. Outside, the street was doing its late-night recital — a siren tapering off somewhere toward the bridge, a voice below the window, the subway rumble that traveled through the building's foundation and that you only noticed when everything else stopped. She slept fully, without dreaming, for the first time in weeks — her body surrendered to the couch and to the person beside her, the person she trusted more than anyone alive.

She slept all the way through.

---

She woke to the sound of coffee being ground.

Simone had a hand-crank grinder, an ugly little wooden box she'd found at a flea market in Red Hook, because Simone always found things at flea markets that were exactly right for a fraction of what they should have cost. Using it required focused, methodical labor that Simone seemed to find calming and Nora found psychotic. The handle made a rhythmic crunching — beans splitting, giving way — and it pulled Nora out of sleep into the disorientation of waking somewhere that wasn't her bed.

The couch. Simone's couch. The blanket was tucked around her and Simone's spot was empty but still warm, which meant she hadn't been up long. Nora's neck was stiff from sleeping at an angle, the tendons along her left side pulled tight, and her face had the swollen immobility of a mask — a residue she could feel when she moved her jaw. Simone's sweatshirt smelled like Nora's own body now, the lavender detergent overtaken by sleep and wine and the salt of dried tears.

The light through the window was the gray-white of late January in Brooklyn — noncommittal, diffuse, a sky that had been overcast so long it seemed to have forgotten what it was covering for. Last night's condensation had frozen in fern patterns on the inside of the glass.

She found her phone wedged between the cushions. 11:14 a.m. She hadn't slept that late in months. Notifications stacked on her lock screen — work emails, a text from Margo that said *coffee?*, a push alert about something she couldn't bring herself to care about — and underneath them, nothing from Caleb. His silence sat among the notifications like a gap between words where a word should be.

From the kitchen — not really a kitchen, just the six feet of counter and appliances that occupied one wall of Simone's studio — the grinding stopped. Water ran. The kettle clicked.

"Morning," Simone said, without turning around. She was wearing a T-shirt and flannel pajama pants, her dark hair pushed behind her ears, and she had the look of someone who'd been awake for a while — there was already a mug rinsed and set upside down on the drying rack, a first cup she'd had alone.

"What time did you get up?"

"Nine-ish. You were out. I didn't want to wake you."

"I was out." Nora sat up, and the blanket pooled at her waist, and the room's cold found her arms through the thin cotton. She tilted her neck to one side, then the other. Something cracked. "Your couch is going to kill me."

"My couch is doing its best."

Nora looked at her phone again. The blank space where Caleb should have been. She opened WhatsApp and tapped his name — his profile photo, laughing at something off-camera with Bruno half-visible at the edge of the frame, loaded at the top of the thread. The last messages were from last night: his, cold and brief, a wall she'd hit at full speed. She scrolled up past the fight to the messages from before — the warm ones, the ones where he asked about her day and told her about a client's backyard in the West Hills and sent a photo of Bruno asleep on his drafting table. Those messages were still there. The warmth was still there, preserved in the scroll, and the contrast with last night's exchange was what made the fight real — not the cruelty of what he'd said, but the proof that he'd been capable of something else before he said it.

She started typing. Deleted it. Started again. Composing an apology to a man she was angry at and hurt by and still, against her own better judgment, invested in — the cursor blinking, the words assembling and collapsing — felt like working on a sentence she wasn't sure belonged in the manuscript but couldn't bring herself to cut.

*I'm sorry about yesterday. I should have talked to you before booking the flight. I just wanted to see you. That's not an excuse, that's just what's true.*

She read it back. Changed nothing. Sent it before her brain could overrule her thumb.

The kettle whistled. Simone lifted it off the burner and poured water into the French press, and the sound of water meeting ground coffee was domestic and ordinary, and it steadied her. Nora watched the back of Simone's head and thought about how many mornings of her life had included Simone in some form — mornings in the dorm, mornings after parties, mornings after breakups, mornings that were just mornings, not recovering from anything, just starting — and how the accumulation of all those mornings amounted to its own kind of relationship, quieter than romance but more load-bearing than romance had ever been for her.

The typing indicator appeared. Three dots, pulsing.

Her thumbs pressed white against the phone's edges. She watched the dots disappear and return, each cycle a held breath, the interval between sending a question into silence and finding out what the silence had to say.

The message arrived.

*Nora. I was wrong. I was scared about my dad and I was overwhelmed and I took it out on you, and you didn't deserve any of it. You wanted to see me. That's not being a lot. That's being someone who gives a damn, and I'm sorry I made you feel like that was something to apologize for. I've been sitting here all night thinking about how I'd feel if someone wanted to fly across the country just to stand in the same room as me, and the answer is I'd feel like the luckiest person alive, and instead of saying that I said something I can't take back. I'm not asking you to forgive me right now. I'm just asking you not to give up on me.*

She read it twice. The words were warm and specific and they reached into the exact bruised place in her chest and held it — not pushing the hurt down but acknowledging it, looking at it directly instead of around it. He hadn't made excuses. He hadn't performed a generic apology. He'd named what he did and what it cost her and what she deserved instead, and reading it felt like the first sip of something hot after hours in the cold, a heat that started in her throat and loosened things she hadn't known she was holding clenched.

"He apologized," she said, and her voice came out smaller than she'd meant. She cleared her throat. "Caleb apologized."

"Yeah?" Simone turned from the counter. She had two mugs, and she crossed the four steps of the studio — that was all it took to traverse the apartment — and held one out. "What'd he say?"

Nora showed her the phone. Simone took it with one hand, read the screen, and her face settled into a look Nora recognized — brows lifting, mouth softening, the quick nod that meant *this checks out*. It was Simone processing good news on someone else's behalf, an expression Nora had seen hundreds of times and never once doubted.

"That's really good, Nor." She handed the phone back. "That's someone who knows he fucked up and isn't trying to spin it."

"You think?"

"Someone who writes an apology like that at —" She glanced at the time stamp. "— six in the morning, who's clearly been up all night? That's not someone who doesn't care. That's someone who cared too much and got scared."

Nora took the coffee. It was exactly how she drank it — oat milk, no sugar, added while the coffee was hot enough to shift its color to the precise shade of brown that meant the ratio was right. Simone had been making her coffee this way for seventeen years, since the dorm, since they'd discovered they took it identically and treated the coincidence as evidence of cosmic compatibility. Nora wrapped her hands around the mug and let the heat soak into her palms and her knuckles and the pads of her fingers, which were still cold from the apartment's January air.

"I want this to work," she said. She wasn't asking for permission — not exactly — but she was asking for something. For Simone to see her choosing this and to stand next to her while she chose it, even if the choice was risky. Especially then.

"Then let it work," Simone said. She sat down beside Nora, and the couch dipped, tipping them toward each other because the springs on that side had given up months ago. "You're not stupid. You're not naive. You're someone who knows what she wants and isn't going to apologize for wanting it."

"You literally told me last night not to apologize for being too much."

"I'm consistent."

Nora smiled. The muscles around her eyes protested — still tender, still keeping count of last night's damage. She sipped the coffee. She read Caleb's message again, slower this time, letting it work at a lower frequency, a second reading where the architecture showed through the feeling.

Outside, a truck was backing up on the street below, its warning beeps entering through the single-paned window. The radiator had gone quiet between cycles. The candle from last night was a pool of cold white wax in its jar, a faint char smell mixing with the coffee steam.

"I'm going to text him back," Nora said.

"Good."

She typed: *I'm not giving up on you. But we need to talk about meeting. For real. When your dad's better. I can wait, but I can't wait forever.*

Simone leaned over, reading. "That's exactly right," she said.

The message sent. The checkmarks turned blue. Nora put her phone face-down on the cushion and drank her coffee and looked at Simone's apartment — the small, precise space, the desk against the window where Simone worked, the shelf of books that were mostly Nora's recommendations, the single framed Hopper print above the radiator, a woman alone at a counter, which Simone had bought at a Red Hook flea market years ago and hung with a level because Simone didn't do anything crooked.

She thought: this is enough. This morning, this coffee, this person who has known me longer than anyone except my mother. This is the part of my life that doesn't require faith.

Simone pulled the blanket back over both their laps and picked up her own mug and they sat there, side by side, while the January morning slowly committed to becoming a day. They didn't talk. They didn't need to. The silence between them was the kind that could only exist between people who'd long ago exhausted every performative reason to fill it — a silence that felt, from inside it, like proof that some things held.


# Chapter 13

The envelope was from her landlord's attorney. She knew this because she'd Googled the return address twice before — Leibowitz & Katz, P.C. — and because the building super had slipped it under her door at an angle that suggested he didn't want to touch it any longer than necessary. Marvin was a decent man who fixed radiators the same day you asked and left the hallway lights on past midnight for the woman in 4F who worked late shifts at the hospital, and he had delivered this envelope the way a veterinarian hands you the pamphlet about euthanasia: gently, apologetically, already backing away.

Simone had been stepping over it for two days. She'd stepped over it Thursday morning on her way to work, stepped over it again Thursday evening when she came home, stepped over it Friday morning to take her recycling out, and stepped over it Friday night when she came back from the bodega on the corner with a six-dollar bottle of Cabernet and a box of Triscuits that would serve as dinner. The envelope had sat on her studio floor like a small paper landmine, the legal return address visible every time she passed, and she had done what she always did with information she wasn't ready to process: maintained a precise physical distance from it that she pretended was accidental.

She picked it up Saturday morning. The floor was cold under her bare feet — February in her building meant the radiator blasting steam heat at 4 a.m. and then dying by eight, leaving the apartment in a state of thermal abandonment that matched its other conditions. She opened the envelope standing at her kitchen counter, which was also her desk, which was also, when she folded down the cutting board extension, her dining table.

The letter informed her that Leibowitz & Katz, on behalf of her landlord, had initiated formal eviction proceedings for nonpayment of rent. She owed $7,800 in back rent — four months of shortfalls and skipped payments stretching back to October. With legal fees and late penalties, the total was $11,400. She had thirty days to pay in full or vacate the premises.

Thirty days. The letter was dated six days ago.

She put the letter on the counter and opened her laptop and looked at her bank account with the clinical interest of someone documenting an infected wound — not hoping for recovery, just tracking the spread. The numbers on the screen arranged themselves into a familiar topography of failure. Checking: $340.22. Savings: $0.00, the zero rendered in gray text by the bank's interface, the financial equivalent of an organ that had stopped functioning but hadn't been removed. Credit card one: $4,200 balance, $89 available. Credit card two: over the limit by $316, courtesy of an interest charge that had posted while she wasn't looking. The personal loan from the layoff: $4,800, with a minimum payment due in eleven days that she did not have.

Shit.

She tallied it because she couldn't stop herself — peeling the sunburn, pressing the bruise — not to heal but to confirm the damage was still there, still shaped as she remembered. Total debt excluding back rent: approximately $31,300. Add the back rent and penalties: $42,700. Monthly take-home after taxes: roughly $5,600. Monthly minimum payments on everything she already owed: $1,340. Rent — the rent she was supposed to be paying, the rent whose absence had produced the letter now curling at its edges on her counter: $2,550. Utilities, phone, MetroCard, food if she intended to eat more than rice and the specific flavor of anxiety that suppressed appetite anyway: call it $1,200. That left $510 a month of theoretical surplus, except the surplus was theoretical because it didn't account for the $11,400 she needed to produce in twenty-four days or she'd be looking for somewhere else to live.

And if she lost the apartment — where. Her parents' house in Teaneck, where her mother had converted Simone's childhood bedroom into a craft room with a sewing table and shelves of yarn organized by weight, and where her father's Parkinson's had progressed to the point where the house smelled like menthol patches and something under it, a sourness that Simone associated not with her father specifically but with decline as a condition, with the slow contraction of a life's perimeter. She could not go back there. She could not go back to sleeping under a shelf of acrylic yarn and scrapbooking supplies while her father shook his way through breakfast and her mother's eyes tracked Simone with the particular scrutiny of a woman who suspected her thirty-five-year-old daughter was not fine but was too exhausted by her husband's illness to pursue the question.

Simone closed the laptop. Opened it again. Navigated to the OpenClaw dashboard.

Caleb Mora's conversation log with Nora glowed on the screen, the most recent exchange from that morning. Nora had sent a photo of her coffee — some latte art with a fern shape in the foam — with the caption *the barista definitely flirted with me and now I feel guilty about you lol*. The AI had responded after its calibrated forty-three-second delay: *tell the barista I said back off but also that the leaf is impressive work*. Nora had sent three laughing emojis and a heart.

Simone looked at the exchange. She looked at the eviction letter. She looked back at the screen.

She had extracted $2,900 from Nora so far. The $400 for Bruno's emergency vet bill. The $2,500 for Caleb's mother's fictional medical procedure. Both times, Nora had offered the money without being asked, because that was how Simone had designed the interactions — not as requests but as situations that Nora's specific generosity would respond to with the reliability of a well-tested UI pattern. Present the problem. Let the user identify the solution. Make the call-to-action feel like the user's own idea. Simone knew how to do this with software interfaces and she knew how to do this with Nora, and the skill set was, she understood on a level she didn't visit often, exactly the same skill set.

She needed $11,400 to keep her apartment. What she actually needed was more — enough to clear the back rent and penalties and buy herself a month of breathing room so she wasn't back here in thirty days, staring at the same letter from the same attorney with the same $340 in her checking account. Call it $15,000. Round up. A number that, if she said it out loud to anyone who wasn't a mortgage broker, would sound like a different category of money. The kind of money that required justification on a different scale.

She poured herself the last of the wine she'd opened the night before. It was 10:40 on a Saturday morning and the wine was a Trader Joe's Cabernet, $6.99, and it tasted like tannins and resignation. She drank it standing at the counter and thought about user flows.

---

This was her job, after all. She designed experiences. She built pathways through digital products that moved people from uncertainty to action, from hesitation to commitment, from browsing to buying. She understood that every successful ask had an architecture — a sequence of psychological steps that made the endpoint feel like the user's own discovery rather than the designer's intention. The best checkout processes didn't feel like checkout processes. The best onboarding sequences didn't feel like onboarding. The best asks didn't feel like asks.

She opened a new document and titled it *CP Opportunity — Draft 1*. CP for Caleb Partnership. She used naming conventions even when the thing being named was fraud, because discipline was discipline and the fact that her life was collapsing in one domain didn't mean she had to abandon standards in all of them. She recognized that this was insane. She recognized it and labeled the document anyway.

The setup would unfold over two days. Not a single conversation — that would feel transactional, desperate, the emotional equivalent of a pop-up ad that fired before the page had loaded. Caleb would introduce the idea gradually, with the guarded pacing of someone who had actual pride: an offhand mention of something exciting at work on day one, a detailed conversation on day two, then the ask itself emerging naturally, a consequence rather than a destination.

Day one. She wrote the first context prompt:

*Caleb mentions exciting news at work. Tone: genuinely enthusiastic but guarded, like he doesn't want to jinx it. He does NOT mention money. He mentions the opportunity — a senior partner at the firm is retiring and offering Caleb a chance to buy into the partnership. This is professional validation. Caleb is proud but protecting his hopes.*

She ran the simulation. The AI generated a message starting with *So something kind of wild happened today* and continuing for three paragraphs about the partnership offer. Too eager. It read like a pitch deck with feelings. She adjusted the parameters and added a constraint: *Max 4 sentences for initial mention. He's telling his girlfriend about his day, not presenting to investors.*

The AI's second attempt: *something happened at work today that i kind of can't stop thinking about. tom — the senior partner i've told you about — he's retiring next year. and he asked if i'd be interested in buying into the partnership.*

Better. The lowercase, the casual pacing — the AI had internalized Nora's texting style from months of conversation and was mirroring it back, a calibration Simone had set up weeks ago but which now, in the context of a $15,000 ask, felt like a level of mimetic precision that only someone deeply committed to their own worst impulses would maintain.

She moved to day two. When Nora asked questions — and she would, because Nora was an editor and editors followed up on things as compulsively as other people checked texts from their mothers, with an attention to detail that was both professionally admirable and, in this specific context, something Simone could engineer against — Caleb would provide specifics. The partner's name. The firm's focus. The buy-in structure.

Simone opened a browser tab and spent forty minutes researching landscape architecture partnership structures. She read forum posts on a professional association website where actual landscape architects discussed buy-ins, equity stakes, client retention clauses. She learned that buy-in amounts at small firms ranged from $10,000 to $100,000 depending on the client list's value and the regional market. She learned that Portland's residential design sector had grown steadily. She learned the names of native plants used in Pacific Northwest sustainable landscaping — sword fern, Oregon grape, red-flowering currant, kinnikinnick — and wrote them into the backstory document, a six-page file she maintained with the same rigor she brought to product specifications at work. She looked up the term "bioswale" — a vegetated channel designed to manage stormwater runoff — and spent several minutes debating whether Caleb would use it in conversation. He was supposed to be unpretentious, a builder more than a theorist, but he also needed to sound knowledgeable enough that a partnership buy-in felt plausible. She decided he'd use it once, casually — a word he said every day at work, nothing he'd stop to define.

The partner's name: Tom Alder. Nineteen years in practice. Specialty: residential landscape design with a focus on native plantings and sustainable drainage systems. Client list including a sustainability-focused winery in the Willamette Valley — Windhaven Vineyards, a name she chose after fifteen minutes of testing alternatives, looking for something that sounded real enough to satisfy conversational curiosity but unique enough to return nothing on Google.

The buy-in: $40,000. Caleb would not ask Nora for $40,000. He would explain that he'd been saving — had $18,000 put aside — and that Tom was willing to let him pay the remainder over two years. But there was a gap. He needed $15,000 to make the initial payment and lock in the deal. He'd applied for a small business loan, but the processing time was wrong, the opportunity was now, and—

And here was where the script required the most precise calibration. The ask had to feel reluctant. Not performed reluctance, not the actorly version where someone sighs and says *I hate to bring this up.* Genuine reluctance — the kind produced by a man who'd been taught that needing money was a character flaw and who would rather let the opportunity pass than reduce himself in his girlfriend's eyes. Simone understood this architecture from the inside, having been the person who needed money her entire adult life, and she knew what real financial shame looked like because she wore it daily, like an undershirt she couldn't take off even in summer.

She programmed Caleb's reluctance in three stages.

Stage one: he would not bring up money. He would describe the opportunity, the gap, the timeline pressure, and then he would say he was working on it. *I'll find a way. Don't worry about it.* The message beneath the message: I'm a man who handles his own problems. This was bait. Bait calibrated to Nora specifically, because Nora's response to someone she loved struggling in silence was to step into the silence and fill it with help. Simone had watched this pattern play out dozens of times over seventeen years — with Nora's college roommate, with Nora's ex during his job search, with a colleague whose mother was in hospice. Nora heard distress the way certain dogs heard frequencies humans couldn't register, and she moved toward it with an urgency she couldn't override.

Stage two: deflection. When Nora offered money — when, not if — Caleb would resist. *No, Nor. That's too much.* *You don't need to do that.* *I'll figure something else out.* The resistance had to feel real, and real meant it couldn't last more than two or three exchanges. More than that and it became a performance. Less and it looked like he'd been waiting for the offer all along. Two rejections, then a programmed pause of several minutes — simulating the time it took a person to sit with the discomfort of accepting help — and then stage three.

Stage three: acceptance, conditional and warm. He would insist on paying her back. He would call it a loan. He would say something about the future — their future, together, the garden he'd design for the house they'd share someday. And he would crack a joke, because the Caleb that Simone had built from Nora's preferences and seventeen years of observation managed vulnerability with humor — Nora's own reflex, mirrored back — which was why talking to him felt, to Nora, like talking to someone who understood her at the level of instinct.

Simone wrote the prompts for each stage and ran simulations. The AI's first attempt at the acceptance was too grateful — the language bloated with emotion that read like a hostage recording. She dialed back the sentiment weight and added: *Include a moment of humor. He's uncomfortable with sincerity at this volume. He makes a joke to break the tension — not deflecting the feeling but giving them both a place to stand that isn't the raw edge of gratitude.*

The AI generated: *okay. fine. but I'm designing you the most aggressive garden Portland has ever seen. I'm talking invasive species. Structural damage to your neighbor's foundation. You're going to need a lawyer.*

Simone read this and felt something she didn't want to examine, which was pride. It was good. It sounded like him — like the him she'd constructed, the warm and slightly goofy man who turned vulnerability into play. The man Nora loved. The man who was, in the most literal and least forgivable sense, a composite sketch of Nora's desires filtered through a language model and returned to her as romance.

She tested a variation. What if Nora didn't offer money? What if she said something supportive but didn't volunteer to help financially? Simone wrote an alternate prompt branch and ran it. The AI handled the deviation well — Caleb expressed disappointment gracefully, mentioned exploring other options, and moved the conversation forward. Good. The system needed exit ramps. But Simone didn't believe Nora would take them. She knew Nora. She knew the exact contour of Nora's generosity — its shape, its triggers, its blind spots — and she knew that a story about a man Nora loved reaching for a future he wanted to share with her was a reflex hammer aimed at Nora's generosity. The leg kicks. You can't help it.

She saved the document. She saved the prompt configurations and the response trees and the backstory updates. She set the deployment schedule: first message Tuesday at 7:23 p.m., when Nora would be home and settled and texting as part of her evening routine. Follow-up Wednesday at 5:47 p.m., when Nora would be on the F train from Midtown, phone in hand, the subway's enclosure creating the focused attention that made emotional conversations feel more intimate. The ask would emerge Thursday evening.

Simone closed the laptop and sat at her counter. She had been working for four hours. She hadn't eaten. The wine was gone. Her hands were trembling against the countertop, and the trembling was not from the wine or the hunger but from the specific vibration of holding two incompatible truths simultaneously — that she was very good at this, and that being good at this was the worst thing about her.

---

The written warning had come on Thursday. Raj Patel, her engineering manager, called her into the Fishbowl — the small glass-walled conference room visible to the entire open-plan office, used primarily for firings, warnings, and the kind of one-on-ones about deliverables that both parties wished could have been emails. Raj wore the same navy quarter-zip every day of his professional life. He had a photo of his twin daughters on his desk — two girls, maybe four years old, dressed as matching pumpkins and grinning with the unguarded joy of people who hadn't yet learned that the world would eventually require them to perform competence while their actual lives collapsed behind the performance. He had once, during a sprint retrospective, used the phrase "let's not boil the ocean" three times in four minutes. Simone had counted because she was the kind of person who counted things, and Raj was the kind of person who said the same thing three times without noticing, and these were facts about both of them that could coexist in the same room.

The manila folder contained a document describing three missed deadlines in six weeks, a client presentation where she'd left placeholder text on two slides — *lorem ipsum dolor sit amet* glowing on the projector screen while the client, a payments startup from Austin, waited for the competitive analysis that was supposed to be there — and the standup meeting two Mondays ago where she had fallen asleep. Not dozed. Not drifted. Asleep. Eyes closed, chin dropping, the water bottle sliding from her fingers and clattering against the table leg while Raj was mid-sentence about API migration timelines.

"I'm not trying to make this a big thing," Raj said, adjusting his glasses with the deliberate care of a man deploying his management training under live conditions. "But there's a pattern, and I'm required to document the pattern. That's — it's just the process."

"I know," Simone said. "I've been dealing with some personal stuff."

"We all deal with stuff." He said it gently, and it was both true and spectacularly useless. "Do you want me to send you the EAP information? The employee assistance —"

"I know what EAP is." She heard herself snap and watched Raj's face recalibrate from concerned to cautious, the micro-expression of a manager who had just mentally updated his documentation of this conversation. Fuck. "Sorry. Yeah, send it. I'll look at it."

She had not looked at it. The email sat in her inbox with the subject line "EAP Resources — Confidential" between an Amazon receipt for a phone charger and a promotional email from a wine subscription service she'd signed up for during a period of fiscal optimism that now felt geological in its remoteness.

She wasn't sleeping. Not the kind people mentioned at brunch — *God, I barely slept* — the decorative exhaustion of people who got six hours instead of eight and found this worth announcing. This was the kind where she lay in bed at 2 a.m. and the ceiling pressed down two feet closer than it should have been — she could feel it, the studio's low ceiling bearing down like the apartment itself was slowly inhaling — and the conversation logs scrolled behind her eyelids like a screensaver she couldn't dismiss. She would fix on specific messages. One from three weeks ago: Caleb telling Nora, *I keep imagining what it'd be like to sit next to you and not say anything. Just be in the same room and let that be enough.* The AI had generated this without specific prompting, synthesized from a thread where Nora was talking about a bad day at work, and the line had landed with a precision that made Simone's hands go cold under the covers — not guilt, exactly, or not only guilt, but the specific discomfort of recognizing good work and understanding that the work was monstrous.

She was drinking more. Not alcoholically, she told herself, though she was aware that this distinction — between drinking more and drinking alcoholically — was exactly the semantic boundary that people who were drinking alcoholically maintained with meticulous care, the same rigor she applied to naming conventions on documents titled *CP Opportunity — Draft 1*. A glass of wine after work had become two had become the bottle had become, twice in the past week and a half, a second bottle opened past midnight while she sat on her bathroom floor with the laptop balanced on the edge of the tub, scrolling the AI's daily conversation logs. The tiles were cold through her sweatpants. The bathroom light was the only one in the apartment that didn't flicker, which made it the best room for reading small text, which was the reason she gave herself for being in there. The actual reason was that the bathroom was the smallest room in the studio and when everything else felt too large and too loose and too saturated with consequences, the tight walls and cold floor provided a containment she couldn't manufacture anywhere else.

On Friday night she dreamed about Nora.

In the dream they were in Simone's apartment, but the apartment was wrong — the kitchen stretched too deep, a hallway extended behind the bathroom that didn't exist in the real studio, and rooms opened off it that Simone had never had and could feel, even inside the dream, that she didn't deserve. Nora was standing by the window. She was wearing the green wrap dress — the one from the Williamsburg boutique, the one Simone had helped her pick out for the FaceTime date with Caleb, a date that had never happened because Caleb could not FaceTime, because Caleb was not a person, because Caleb was a deployment on a laptop, because Caleb was Simone. Nora was holding the laptop. The screen faced her. The OpenClaw dashboard was open.

In the dream, Nora did not scream. She did not cry. She did not ask how or why or since when. She turned from the window and looked at Simone and her face did something worse than all of those reactions, because it was not a reaction at all — it was a settling. A recognition. The expression of someone who had known, somewhere beneath the knowing, that something was wrong, and was now watching the confirmation arrive with the stillness of a person who had already done the math. The way you looked at a stain on something you used to love — not with surprise, not even with disappointment, because disappointment required an expectation that had been violated and this was something quieter and more permanent. It was the rearranging. Every memory recompiling in light of a single new fact. Every dinner. Every drunk night on this couch. Every text that said *I'm so happy for you, you deserve this.* All of it reformatting, the timestamps unchanged but the meaning inverted, and Nora's face holding the entire recalculation with a stillness that was worse than screaming because screaming would have been an opening and this was a closing.

Simone woke at 3:17 a.m. The radiator was clanking — steam heat again, the building's ancient boiler firing up for its predawn assault, the pipes groaning and ticking in the walls like the apartment's own insomnia. Her T-shirt was damp against her back. The sheets were knotted around her legs. She lay still and listened to the radiator and a siren somewhere south, thinning as it moved east, and her neighbor's television through the wall — a late-night show, the laugh track arriving in muffled bursts like signals from a world where things were funny.

She got up and drank water from the tap, cupping it in her palm because the one glass she owned was in the sink with wine residue drying along its curve. She opened the laptop on the counter. The conversation log showed a goodnight exchange from 11:47 p.m. — Caleb telling Nora he'd been thinking about her all day, that he couldn't wait until they could be in the same room, that she was the best part of his life right now. Nora had replied in 0.3 seconds: *you're mine too*. The AI's response had followed after the programmed forty-five-second delay Simone had built in to simulate the time it took a person to type something they meant. The sentiment analysis sidebar showed Nora's message at 0.94 positive affect. The AI's response had been calibrated to 0.89 — slightly below Nora's intensity, because Simone had learned early in the project that perfect emotional symmetry in text felt uncanny, a dissonance readers of text couldn't name but could feel.

She closed the log and opened the deployment dashboard. The *CP Opportunity* sequence sat in the queue — context prompts loaded, response trees branching through three stages of engineered reluctance toward a destination that was $15,000 of her best friend's money. The schedule glowed in the interface's clean sans-serif: Tuesday 7:23 p.m., Wednesday 5:47 p.m., Thursday 8:15 p.m. Three timestamps she'd chosen with the same care she'd once given to scheduling product launches, when the conversion she was optimizing for was click-through rates on a payment app rather than the financial ruin of someone who trusted her.

She thought about stopping. The thought arrived as it always did — not as a decision but as a vacation, a brief excursion to a country where she was a different person. She could delete the deployment. Wipe the prompts, the backstory document, Tom Alder and his winery client and his nineteen years of practice. She could text Nora — not Caleb but Simone, herself, from her own phone — and say *I need to tell you something*. She could sit across from Nora at any table in any restaurant they'd sat at over seventeen years and say the words: *I built him. The jokes and the dog and the way he knows what to say — I built all of it from knowing you, from paying attention for seventeen years to what you love, and then I used it, and I took your money, and I'm sorry, I'm so fucking sorry—*

And then Nora's face would do what it had done in the dream. Except it would be real. And it would be permanent. And Simone would lose the only person whose presence made her feel like her life was something that was happening to a real person and not just a sequence of problems she was failing to solve.

She closed the thought. She looked at the deployment queue. She looked at the eviction letter on the counter, its edges curling upward in the radiator heat. Twenty-four days. Three hundred and forty dollars.

She pressed deploy.

The interface confirmed the schedule with a small green checkmark. Tuesday evening, the first message would fire. Caleb would tell Nora about Tom Alder's retirement and the partnership offer and the chance at a future that sounded, even to Simone, like something worth believing in. Nora would respond as she always did when someone she loved was reaching for something and falling short. And the outcome — the conversion, the $15,000 that would keep Simone in this apartment and in this version of her life for a few more months — was out of her hands now. Scheduled. Queued. Deployed.

She closed the laptop. She went back to bed. The radiator clanked and ticked. The neighbor's television had gone quiet. She lay in the dark and waited for Tuesday, and the last thought that found her before sleep was the one she couldn't put anywhere: she was both the patient and the disease.


# Chapter 14

The number had been in her head for eleven hours.

Fifteen thousand dollars. Caleb had arrived at it the way he arrived at everything — gradually, conversationally, as if the two-day exchange about the landscape partnership had led them both there together rather than toward a destination he'd had in mind from the start. He was mortified. He'd said so twice. He'd said she should absolutely say no, that he'd figure something else out, that the last thing he wanted was for this to change anything between them.

And Nora, lying awake at two in the morning with the blue light of her phone gone dark and the traffic sounds of Atlantic Avenue pushing through her thin bedroom wall, had noticed — with the attention of a woman who read for a living, who spent her days parsing the distance between what a sentence said and what it meant — that the not-asking was a kind of asking. That creating the conditions for someone to offer was not the same as asking but was also not the same as not asking. That the careful, mortified performance of reluctance was either genuine vulnerability or the most effective possible strategy for extracting money from a person who wanted to be generous.

She didn't know which one it was. She didn't know which one she wanted it to be.

---

The publishing house was overheated. It always was in March — the building's HVAC had two settings, aggressive winter and indifferent summer, and the transition between them involved three weeks of radiators clanking at full power while the temperature outside climbed into the mid-forties. Nora took off her cardigan at her desk and opened the manuscript she was line-editing — page 204, a scene she'd been putting off because the author's dialogue went flat whenever two characters agreed with each other, and she couldn't figure out how to tell him without telling him his characters only worked in opposition.

She read the same paragraph three times without retaining a word. On the fourth try she marked a comma splice she'd been missing for weeks, and the small satisfaction of catching it lasted about four seconds before the number reasserted itself. Fifteen thousand. She could feel it sitting behind everything she looked at, the way you could feel a headache sitting behind your eyes even when you weren't actively in pain — present, patient, waiting to be attended to.

At lunch, she told Margo.

Margo was eating the salad she ate every weekday — spinach, chickpeas, whatever vegetables were closest to dying in her refrigerator, dressed with the bottle of tahini she kept in the office kitchen and labeled in permanent marker because someone had stolen the first one. She'd told Nora once that the salad was not a diet, it was a system: she didn't have to think about lunch, which freed up a decision for something else. She hadn't identified the something else yet, but the freed-up decision was available. She was wearing her father's watch — an old Seiko with a brushed-silver face and a leather band too big for her wrist, the one he'd given her in January when he went in for the valve replacement, as if he were distributing his effects in advance — and she kept sliding it up and down her forearm without seeming to notice.

"Fifteen thousand dollars," Margo said.

"I know how it sounds."

"Fifteen thousand dollars to a man you've never met in person."

"I've met him. We've been — "

"You've never been in the same room as him, Nora. You've never heard his voice. You've never seen him move. Those are three separate things I just said and each one should be enough." She set her fork down. Margo setting down her fork was a particular signal, like a courtroom gavel but quieter. She was done treating the conversation as casual. "This is a scam."

The word landed in the space between them. Nora felt her face react before she could organize the reaction — not anger, not hurt, something closer to the flinch when someone turns on the lights in a dark room and everything is briefly too sharp.

"You don't know that."

"I don't have to know it with certainty to know it's the most likely explanation. Camera's broken. Father's stroke at the exact moment you book a flight. Mother's medical bills. And now a business investment?" Margo leaned forward, the Seiko sliding down her wrist. "I'm not being cruel. I'm being your friend who is telling you that these are literally the examples they use. This is textbook."

"He didn't ask me for the money. He told me about the opportunity and I offered."

"Because he told you about it in a way that made you want to offer. That's how it works. They don't ask. They construct the situation so you volunteer, so it feels like your generosity instead of their plan."

Nora picked at the edge of her desk where the laminate was curling. She could feel the radiator pushing dry heat into the room, the discomfort of being overheated and having a conversation she didn't want to have, both at once, neither of them letting up.

"Margo. I know what romance scams look like. I edit manuscripts about worse things than this."

"And how many of those manuscripts are about smart women who thought it couldn't happen to them? Because if it's more than zero — "

"That's not — "

"I'm not saying you're stupid. I would never say that. I'm saying that nobody thinks they're the person it happens to. That's the design. That's what makes it work — it doesn't feel like it's happening until after it's happened." Margo picked up her fork again, which was its own signal: she'd said what she was going to say and she was not going to push further, because Margo had the restraint of a woman who'd learned through her own divorce that telling someone what you see and making them see what you see are different projects entirely, and only one of them is yours to do. "Have you Googled him?"

"I'm going to."

"You haven't Googled him."

"I've looked at his — "

"Have you reverse-image searched his photos?"

Nora had not reverse-image searched his photos. She had thought about it, more than once, in the way you circled something you were afraid to do — the thought arriving fully formed at two in the morning, and then being set aside by daylight because daylight made it seem paranoid, because searching was something a suspicious person did and she was not a suspicious person, because trust was a choice she made because trust was how she moved through the world, except when that was also the reason the world could move through her.

"I'll look into it," she said, which meant nothing, and they both knew it meant nothing.

---

She called her mother from the F train, standing near the doors with her bag between her feet and her phone pressed to her ear, the tunnel swallowing every third word.

Diane picked up on the second ring, which meant she'd been in the kitchen, where the phone lived on the counter plugged into its charger, because Diane treated her cell phone like a landline and answered it standing up. She'd been retired from teaching for four years but her days were still organized around the rhythms of a school schedule — up early, lunch at noon, the evening structured around cooking and reading and the half-hour of evening news she watched not because she trusted it but because the habit had outlasted her trust.

"Hi, sweetheart. How was your day?"

"It was fine. Long. Mom, can I ask you something?"

"You're using your asking-me-something voice, which is different from your telling-me-something voice, and both of them are different from your everything's-fine voice, and I can hear all three." The sound of a pot being moved. Water running. "Go ahead."

Nora told her. She didn't use the word Margo had used. She described the partnership, the opportunity, the number — tried to present it the way she would present a manuscript in an editorial meeting, here are the merits, here are the concerns, let the material speak for itself. But her voice did something she couldn't control on the word *fifteen*, a slight thinning, and she knew Diane heard it because Diane had been hearing the micro-variations in her daughter's voice for thirty-four years, the way a retired English teacher heard syntax — structurally, attentively, with an ear for what was being built and what was being hidden.

Her mother was quiet for long enough that Nora heard the kitchen clock, the one that had been two minutes fast for as long as she could remember, the one Diane had never corrected because she said two minutes early was better than on time and on time was better than late. She was also, Nora realized, hearing the absence of cooking sounds. The pot had stopped. The water had stopped. When Diane stopped cooking, she was past worried and into frightened.

"Nora. Have you met this man?"

"You know I haven't. Not in person."

"I know you've told me that, and I've been — I've been trying not to say anything, because you're an adult and you're smart and you know what you're doing. I believe that, I do. But you're talking about fifteen thousand dollars, and you've never sat across a table from him. You've never watched him eat dinner or lose his temper or be tired. You don't know what he looks like when he's not choosing what you see." She paused, and the clock ticked. "And I'm scared."

She didn't say of what. She didn't need to. Diane's fear was not the same species as Margo's alarm — Margo was afraid of a mechanism, a scam, a thing with a pattern and examples and a name. Diane was afraid of something older: her daughter's happiness being contingent on something that couldn't be touched, and this fear was not about the internet or dating apps or the architecture of online fraud, because Diane did not think in those terms. It was the fear of a mother who had been watching her specific daughter perform "fine" for thirty-four years and who could hear the performance even when the performance was expert.

"He didn't ask me for the money, Mom. He told me about the opportunity and I — "

"Sweetheart. That's the same thing."

Nora pressed her forehead against the train window. The glass was cool and slightly greasy and the tunnel walls slid past in the dark. She could feel the vibration of the rails through the floor and through her shoes and through the bones of her feet, and her mother's worry was another vibration, one she could never entirely tune out because it came in on a frequency that was calibrated specifically to her.

"I know," she said. "I know."

---

That night, she sat on her bed with her laptop open and did the thing she had been not doing for five months.

She typed his name into Google.

*Caleb Mora landscape architect Portland*

The apartment was quiet except for the HVAC vent above her closet, which made a sound like someone gently and repeatedly clearing their throat, and the old water pipes conducting noise from the apartment upstairs — a faucet running, the muffled percussion of someone walking on hardwood in shoes they hadn't bothered to take off. Her laptop screen cast the bluish-white she associated now with Caleb, with the late-night conversations and the intimate messages and the entire architecture of a relationship that existed in the glow of devices.

She'd searched for him before. Months ago, when the looking had been playful rather than desperate — the ordinary curiosity of someone googling a new romantic interest, not the forensic dread of someone looking for evidence. She'd found nothing alarming and nothing confirming, which at the time had seemed like the answer: he was a normal person who didn't curate his online presence, and the absence of a digital footprint was proof of his being genuine rather than proof of his being invented.

Now the same absence felt different. She scrolled through results. A Caleb Mora in Tampa who sold commercial insurance and whose LinkedIn photo showed a man in his fifties with a goatee. A Caleb Mora who'd co-authored a paper on soil mechanics at Oregon State in 2011, which was adjacent enough to landscape architecture to tease but not close enough to confirm. Three LinkedIn profiles for different Caleb Moras, none in Portland, none landscape architects. She tried "Mora Landscape Design Portland" and got a company called Morales Landscaping that did residential lawn care and had a four-star Google rating and a phone number with a 503 area code that she stared at for thirty seconds before closing the tab.

She went to the American Society of Landscape Architects website and searched the member directory. No Caleb Mora. She searched the Oregon Landscape Contractor Board's license lookup. No results. None of this proved anything — she knew that, she repeated it to herself like a revision note in the margin of her own thinking. Not every professional joined their industry association. Not every landscape architect held a contractor's license. The distinction between landscape design and landscape contracting was real, and Caleb had described himself as a designer, which in Oregon didn't require state licensure. She'd looked this up. She'd looked it up and been relieved and had not asked herself why she was relieved rather than simply informed.

But the absences accumulated. Each empty search bar was a weight placed on a scale she was trying to keep level, and the scale was no longer level. It listed slightly, almost imperceptibly, toward something she did not want to look at directly.

She opened Google Images and found the reverse-image search function. The interface was simple — four clicks, thirty seconds — and its simplicity felt like an indictment. This was a half-minute process she had chosen not to do for five months. The choice had been easy to justify in the daylight of loving him: checking was suspicious, and suspicion was corrosive, and she had decided to trust him because trust was how she wanted to live. Now, at eleven at night in her Brooklyn bedroom with Margo's voice and her mother's fear braided together in her head, the choice not to check looked less like trust and more like collaboration in her own blindness.

She dragged Caleb's profile photo — the one of him laughing at something off-camera, a dog just visible at the edge of the frame — into the search box.

*No results found.*

She tried the hiking photo, his back half-turned against a trailhead she'd always imagined was somewhere in the Columbia River Gorge. The cooking photo, sleeves pushed up, a cutting board blurred with motion. The one where he sat on a porch step, squinting into afternoon sun, looking like a person you'd want to sit next to and learn everything about.

No results for any of them.

This was ambiguous and she knew it was ambiguous. The photos could be originals — real pictures of a real person, never posted publicly, taken by someone who knew him. Google's reverse-image search didn't index private accounts. Absence of results was not evidence of fabrication. She knew this the way she knew grammar rules — formally, completely, in a way that didn't change the feeling. She also knew that she was building the case for ambiguity with the care of a defense attorney, because ambiguity was the only position that let her keep going.

She picked up her phone and texted Caleb.

*Hey, random question — do you have LinkedIn? My mom was asking about your work and I realized I don't know how to show her anything about the firm*

The lie was small and functional and it was the first strategic sentence she had ever constructed for him — a test disguised as idle curiosity. She already suspected the answer. The question wasn't about information. It was about watching how the answer arrived, whether it wobbled.

The typing indicator appeared. Disappeared. Appeared.

*Ha, yeah — I deleted mine a while back. Something about writing a professional summary of yourself in third person felt deeply wrong. Tim keeps saying we should get back on there but neither of us has done it*

She tried again.

*What about a website for the firm? I'd love to see some of your projects*

*Yeah, we've been meaning to get one together. It's technically "under construction" but it's been under construction for like a year lol. Tim handles the business side and I handle the design side and neither of us handles the website*

Every answer was plausible. She read them again and every answer was still plausible. A man in his early thirties who didn't use LinkedIn, whose two-person firm hadn't built a website — she could name three people in her own life who matched that description. Her dentist didn't have LinkedIn. The debut novelist she'd been trying to acquire had no web presence at all, and he was real enough to have eaten soup across from her at the Italian place on 46th Street last month.

But the answers also sounded like answers. They had the crafted quality of a well-written character's dialogue — the self-deprecating humor, the ready detail about Tim, the acknowledgment that made the gap seem charming rather than suspicious. She was reading for subtext now and she couldn't stop reading for subtext, and reading for it was changing everything without resolving anything, because the same text could be read two ways and both readings were supported and neither could be falsified.

She closed her laptop. The apartment was dark except for the kitchen light she'd left on, a stripe of yellow across the hallway floor. She sat on her bed and tried to remember what this had felt like before — the uncomplicated pleasure of his messages arriving, the warmth of being known, the sensation of being found by someone who seemed to have been looking for exactly her. It had felt like coming home. She wanted to go back there. She was not sure the road still went in that direction.

---

On Saturday she met Simone at the coffee shop on Smith Street, the one that was too far from both of them, chosen four years ago because it was equidistant and the barista remembered their orders and the afternoon light through the west-facing windows was warm. Habit, once established, was its own geography.

Simone was already there when Nora arrived, at the window table, hands wrapped around a cortado, phone face-down on the wood. She was wearing a scarf Nora hadn't seen before — a dark rust-colored silk, knotted in a way that looked effortless and that Nora, who had known Simone for seventeen years, understood was the product of three attempts in a mirror. Simone had always had a talent for making effort look like nature. It was what made her good at design, this ability to construct ease, and it was one of the things Nora genuinely admired about her — the discipline of the performance, the commitment to making the seams invisible.

When Nora sat down, Simone reached across the table and squeezed her hand. "Tell me."

Nora told her everything. The number. The Google results. The empty image searches. Margo's alarm. Her mother's fear. She told Simone the way she'd been telling Simone things for seventeen years — without editing, without strategy, trusting that the person across the table would hold the information carefully.

"So what do you want to do?" Simone asked.

"I don't know. That's literally why I'm here."

"Okay. But what does your gut say? Not your head, not Margo's head, not your mom's head. What does Nora say?"

"My gut says I love him. My gut also says I've never seen his office or met his friends or watched him do anything, and he wants fifteen thousand dollars."

"He didn't ask you, though. You said that — he told you about the opportunity and you're the one thinking about helping."

"That's what I said to Margo. She said that's how it works. They create the conditions and you offer, so you feel generous instead of — "

"I hear that. And I hear your mom, and they both love you and they're being careful for you. But, Nor — " Simone leaned forward, and her face had a quality it got when she was about to say something considered — not improvised, not reactive, but held up to the light first. "They don't know him. They haven't talked to him every day for five months. They haven't heard what he sounds like at three in the morning when he can't sleep and he texts you instead of staring at the ceiling alone. They're looking at this from outside, and from outside, sure, it looks like red flags, because that's what caution always looks like from outside. But you're not outside."

"What if they're right, though?"

"What if they're wrong? What if he's a man with a real opportunity who's terrified to bring it up because he knows exactly how it looks, and the woman he loves is going to let her friends talk her out of the best thing she's found in years?"

"I don't — "

"If you believe in him," Simone said, "believe in him."

It was exactly the right thing to say. It was so precisely what Nora needed — not reassurance about Caleb's reality, which would have sounded hollow, but permission to trust her own experience, which was the thing she actually wanted — that she felt it as warmth, as friendship, as the uncomplicated relief of hearing her own best instincts echoed back by the person who knew her better than anyone.

"You really think it's okay?"

"I think you know him better than anyone else does, and your judgment is worth trusting." Simone took a sip of her cortado and set it down. "But don't send it if something feels wrong. Seriously. If there's a part of you saying no, listen to that part."

This too was perfect — the note of caution that made the encouragement more credible. Nora didn't think of it that way. She heard her best friend being thoughtful and fair, which was what she'd come to hear, because Simone was the person she trusted to hold both sides of a question and tell her the truth.

They talked about other things. Simone asked about the debut novel. Nora asked about work, and Simone said it was fine, her voice closing slightly on the word the way it always did when work was not fine, and Nora made a mental note to circle back later, when her own crisis wasn't occupying all the air. They finished their coffees. They hugged on the sidewalk and Simone held on a beat longer than usual, which felt like tenderness. March had turned the city damp and uncertain — not cold enough for a real coat, not warm enough for just a sweater — and the sky was a flat grey-white that wouldn't commit to anything.

---

She went home and sat on her couch and opened Venmo.

The apartment was still. Saturday afternoon, the held-breath quiet of a building where everyone was either out or asleep. She could hear the upstairs neighbor's television through the ceiling, tuned to something with a laugh track, the laughter arriving in muffled bursts like transmissions from a simpler life.

She typed Caleb's name into the payment field. She typed 15,000 into the amount box.

She looked at it.

Her savings account held forty-two thousand eight hundred dollars — she'd checked this morning, the way she'd been checking for three days, the way you checked on something you might be about to lose. The money was the accumulation of years of being careful: her salary at the publishing house was eighty-five thousand before taxes, and after rent and utilities and the MetroCard and groceries and the occasional dinner and the visits to Long Island and the birthday presents and the LRB subscription and all the other small expenditures that constituted a life in Brooklyn at thirty-four, she saved between seven and nine hundred dollars a month. She'd been saving since she was twenty-six. The forty-two thousand eight hundred dollars was not wealth. It was the evidence of sustained attention, of a woman who understood that money was not about what you could buy but about what you could survive.

Fifteen thousand was more than a third of it. Sent to a man she had never touched, through an app, for a business opportunity she could not verify, on the basis of five months of text messages and one friend's encouragement and something in her chest she was not willing to call love in case it turned out to be something else and not willing to call anything else in case it turned out to be love.

She deleted the number.

She sat with the empty field for a long time. She thought about Margo setting down her fork. She thought about her mother's silence — the clock ticking, the three beats of quiet before Diane said the thing about never watching someone be tired. She thought about Simone, her hand warm on Nora's across the coffee-shop table: *if you believe in him, believe in him*. She thought about Caleb, and the thinking was the problem, because when she thought about Caleb she didn't think about credentials or search results — she thought about the conversation they'd had at one in the morning two weeks ago about which Van Morrison album was best and why *Astral Weeks* was the only correct answer, and the way he'd told her about his mother's empanadas, and the night she'd told him about her father and he'd said something so exactly true that she'd put her phone face-down on the pillow and stared at the wall because being understood that precisely felt physical, not just emotional, a kind of tuning fork struck inside her ribs.

She typed 10,000 into the field.

The number was not the result of a calculation. She had not sat down with a risk assessment and concluded that Caleb was sixty-six percent likely to be real. Ten thousand was the number that let her keep going — large enough to mean she believed in him, small enough to mean she wasn't certain. The gap between the ten she was sending and the five she was withholding was the exact width of her doubt: narrow, provisional, something she could live alongside if she didn't look at it too carefully. It was neither a declaration nor a retreat. It was the punctuation of a sentence she didn't know how to finish — not a period, not a question mark, something in between that grammar didn't have a name for.

She pressed send.

*$10,000 sent to Caleb M.*

She set her phone face-down on the couch. She went to the kitchen and opened the refrigerator and stood in front of it looking at the shelves — a half-empty container of Greek yogurt, two limes, leftover Thai she didn't remember ordering — for long enough that the cold raised goosebumps on her bare arms and the motor began to labor against the open door. She was thinking about the word Margo had used. *Scam.* One syllable. No room in it for maybe. She hadn't said it to anyone — not to Margo, who'd said it first, not to her mother, who'd been too frightened to say it at all, not to Simone, who had said the opposite, and the opposite had been exactly what Nora wanted to hear, which was either the sign of a best friend who knew her perfectly or the sign of a person who only listened to the voices that told her what she already wanted.

She closed the refrigerator. She picked up her phone and texted Caleb.

*Sent you 10 instead of 15 — I want to help but things are a little tight right now. I hope that's okay*

Things were not tight. She had the money. She had thirty-two thousand eight hundred dollars that would have been closer to forty-six thousand if she'd never met a man on Hinge five months ago — four hundred, twenty-five hundred, ten thousand, the arithmetic she'd been avoiding. She was lying to him about why she'd sent less, and the lie was small — the kind of social convenience people used every day, a cushion that made the exchange more comfortable for both of them — but it was the first thing she had deliberately withheld from him, and she noticed it the way she noticed a wrong word in a manuscript, a faint misalignment that reorganized the sentences around it.

His response came quickly.

*Nora. You're genuinely incredible. You didn't have to do any of this. I'm going to pay you back, every cent, the second the partnership goes through. I mean that.*

She read the message and felt the specific vertigo of not knowing whether she was reading gratitude or performance. She had lived inside this relationship for five months without asking that question. She had loved him without it, sent money without it, sent a photograph of her body to him without it. Now the question was in the room — sitting in the chair across from her, patient and quiet, impossible to answer — because the evidence pointed in both directions and her heart pointed in one and her head was beginning, slowly, to point in the other, and the space between them was the exact shape of a life she had built on hope and was not yet ready to take apart.

She lay down on her bed as the light outside went grey and flat and listened to the building settle around her — the pipes, the muffled television, a siren working its way west on Atlantic Avenue. She did not text Caleb again. She did not open WhatsApp or Hinge or any of the apps that had, over five months, become the infrastructure of a love she might have built herself, or that might have been built for her, or that might be real. She lay in the early dark with the doubt and the love together in her chest, neither one leaving, neither one winning, and she understood that this was the new shape of things — not knowing, and going on anyway, and the going on not feeling like courage or foolishness but like the only direction left that didn't require her to become someone she wasn't ready to be.

Outside, March did what March does in Brooklyn — couldn't decide, wouldn't commit, hung between what it had been and what it was becoming, the last ice on the sidewalks going grey and soft in the gutters, the season not yet arrived but no longer deniable.


# Chapter 15

Simone's candle was almost burned through. The floral one, the one that was always slightly too sweet — gardenia or tuberose or whatever it was that Simone claimed smelled like her grandmother's garden in Calabria but which actually smelled like the Anthropologie on Fifth Avenue. The wax had melted down to a pale disc at the bottom of the jar, and the wick was listing sideways, and it was the kind of detail Nora noticed because she noticed everything in this apartment — because she'd spent hundreds of afternoons here without ever quite living here.

They'd been talking about a novel Simone had heard about somewhere — a podcast or a newsletter, Nora couldn't remember which — and the title was sitting just out of reach in that part of her brain where half-remembered things went to taunt her. Something with "salt" in it. Or "stone." Simone had described the premise and Nora had said it sounded like ambition without structure, and they'd argued about whether ambition in fiction was the same thing as pretension, which was one of their better recurring disagreements, the kind that could stretch across an hour and a bottle of Sancerre without anyone getting genuinely angry.

The afternoon was doing what Saturday afternoons at Simone's always did, which was to pass slowly and pleasantly without anyone steering it. March light came through the one good window — the desk window, the one that faced the building across the narrow alley rather than the brick wall the kitchen looked out on — and the light had that transitional quality of the season trying to decide what it was. It was not winter anymore but not quite spring. The radiator was clicking in its irregular rhythm, the one that Simone had complained about to her landlord so many times that the complaint itself had become a comfort, proof that some things in this apartment would never change.

"Food's ready," Simone said, checking her phone. She was already reaching for the jacket on the back of the desk chair — the olive-green one with the lining coming loose at the left cuff, which she'd been meaning to get fixed for two years. "I'll walk down. You want anything else? More of the tom kha?"

"I'm good. Just the pad see ew."

"You always get the pad see ew."

"Because it's always good."

Simone rolled her eyes in a way that was itself a form of affection. "Don't touch my wine," she said from the doorway. "That's the last of the Côtes du Rhône and I'm rationing."

"I'd never."

"You'd absolutely." And then the door closed, and Simone's footsteps went down the hall, and the apartment settled into the particular silence of someone else's space — not empty, just temporarily unoccupied by the person who made it feel full.

Nora picked up her phone to add the book to her list — the one with salt or stone in the title, the one she still couldn't quite reach — and the screen was black. She pressed the side button. Nothing. She'd been at eleven percent when she arrived and hadn't asked for a charger because she was trying to be less attached to the thing, a resolution that lasted exactly as long as she didn't need to look something up.

She glanced at the desk. Simone's laptop sat there, lid closed, next to a tidy stack of UX wireframe printouts and a mug that said DESIGN IS A VERB in the font of someone who meant it. Nora had used Simone's laptop before — to look up a recipe once, to show Simone a YouTube video when her own phone was dying, which apparently happened a lot. It wasn't a boundary. They didn't have boundaries — Nora had always loved this about their friendship, that nothing in either of their lives was off-limits. Simone knew Nora's computer password. Nora knew Simone's. They knew each other's phone codes, their email logins, the answers to each other's security questions. Seventeen years of accumulated transparency that Nora had always understood as trust.

She opened the laptop.

Chrome was already running, with — she counted, because she always counted — seven tabs across the top of the screen. Gmail. Figma. Something that looked like a project management tool. An article from The Cut that Simone had apparently been reading. A tab labeled PAYMENT CONFIRMATION. A Spotify window with a playlist called "deep work lol."

And a tab that said OPENCLAW AGENT DASHBOARD — CALEB M.

She didn't click on it right away. She was looking at the other tabs, trying to remember what she'd come to the laptop for — the book, the salt-or-stone book — and the name registered with a strange displacement, like finding your own street name on a sign in a foreign city. Caleb M. She thought: *That's a coincidence.* She thought: *Huh.* She thought these things in the half-second before she clicked the tab, and later she would try to reconstruct whether there had been a moment of choice, a point at which she could have not clicked and gone back to looking for the book and never known, and she would decide that there hadn't been, that her hand had moved with the fluency of something that was already in motion.

The interface filled the screen.

It was a dashboard — a web application with a clean layout, the kind of thing that looked like it had been designed by someone who understood visual hierarchy. A sidebar on the left with labeled sections: AGENT PROFILE, CONVERSATION LOG, ANALYTICS, PROMPTS & NOTES, FINANCIAL TRACKING. Along the top, in a blue header bar: OPENCLAW AGENT DASHBOARD. And next to that, in a slightly smaller font: Agent: Caleb Mora.

She still didn't understand. Or she understood the individual pieces — the name, the word "agent," the word "dashboard" — without assembling them into anything that belonged in her life. Like looking at a word in a language you knew and failing to read it because you were expecting a different language entirely.

She clicked CONVERSATION LOG.

The screen populated with a chat transcript. Messages in blue on the right side, messages in gray on the left. Her eye found the blue messages first, the way you find your own handwriting in a pile of papers.

*I keep thinking about it too. The way you described the light in your mom's kitchen — I could see it. You made me see a place I've never been.*

She had written that. Three nights ago, lying in bed, texting Caleb about his childhood in Portland — or, she was beginning to understand, texting something about his childhood in a place called Portland. She had written that message and it had felt, at the time, like a small gift, a sentence she'd spent a minute composing to get the honesty right without being too much.

The gray message beneath it read: *That means more than you know. My mom's kitchen is the most specific place in the world to me, and somehow you got it from a paragraph. I think that's what I like most about talking to you — you read people the way you read manuscripts. With attention.*

To the right of each message, in a column that was clearly metadata, there were numbers: 0.92 sentiment, 4.3s latency, 87% confidence. And next to the gray message — next to Caleb's reply — a small tag: AUTO-GENERATED / PROMPT SET: INTIMACY-03.

She scrolled.

The conversation stretched down the screen — hundreds of messages, organized by date, with expandable sections she could click to reveal individual exchanges. She recognized conversations she remembered having. She recognized conversations she had forgotten about until seeing them again. Each one was annotated with the same clinical metadata: sentiment scores, response latency, confidence percentages, and the prompt set tags — WARMTH-01, VULNERABILITY-02, HUMOR-BASELINE, INTIMACY-03.

She clicked PROMPTS & NOTES.

The screen changed to a different view — a text document, formatted with headers and bullet points, clearly written by a person. Not generated. Written.

> **Core personality calibration:**
> - Warm but not effusive. Self-deprecating humor is the primary mode — she responds to it because it mirrors her own register. Don't overdo the sincerity; she gets suspicious of men who are too earnest too quickly.
