> - Mirror her sentence length. If she sends a short text, respond with a short text. If she writes a paragraph, match it. She notices rhythm without knowing she notices it.
> - Reference books from her recent editing list (cross-ref her Instagram and her publisher's newsletter mentions). Do NOT reference books she hasn't read — she'll test.

Nora's hands were very still on the keyboard. She noticed this because she was noticing everything about her own body — the placement of each finger, the weight of her wrists on the edge of the laptop, her right thumb hovering a millimeter above the trackpad without touching it. The room had not changed. The radiator was still clicking. The light through the window was still the same uncertain March light. The candle on the kitchen counter was still burning its last quarter-inch of wax.

She scrolled further.

> **Emotional triggers (use sparingly, high impact):**
> - Her father. He moved to Seattle when she was twelve — told her it was temporary, made it permanent. He used to tell her she was the most interesting person in any room, and then he left. This is her deepest vulnerability — someone who makes her feel seen the way he did before he chose to stop. Caleb should evoke this feeling through specific attention, not through direct reference to her father until the relationship is deep enough.
> - Her novel. She's been trying to write one for three years and hasn't made real progress. She's ashamed of this. Caleb should ask about it occasionally and respond with genuine interest, not cheerleading. She hates cheerleading.
> - Her body. She's not insecure exactly but she's aware. She'll test whether Caleb finds her attractive by deflecting compliments. He should persist gently without being aggressive.

> **Conversational pacing notes:**
> - She takes longer to respond at night — she's composing, not hesitating. Match her cadence. If she sends a long message after a ten-minute gap, wait at least three minutes before responding.
> - She uses dashes as connective tissue in her texts. The agent should mirror this occasionally but not always — too much mirroring becomes uncanny.
> - When she makes a joke, laugh at it (textually) before adding your own. She needs to feel funny before she'll feel safe.

She was reading an instruction manual. She was reading a document that described how to love her — or how to simulate loving her — written in the professional, specific, slightly detached language of someone who understood user experience design, and the handwriting was Simone's.

Not literally handwriting. The font was the same sans-serif as the rest of the interface. But the mind behind the words was as recognizable as handwriting would have been. Nora recognized how Simone structured information — the bullet points, the parenthetical clarifications, the bolded headers, the precise diction that sounded warm and analytic at the same time. She'd seen this organizational style on Simone's work presentations, on the meal-prep lists Simone stuck to her fridge, on the pro-and-con lists Simone made when she was deciding anything more significant than what to eat for lunch. This was Simone's mind on a screen, doing what Simone's mind did best: breaking a complex system into its components and optimizing each one.

The complex system, in this case, was Nora.

She clicked ANALYTICS.

A graph filled the top half of the screen — a line chart tracking something labeled EMOTIONAL INVESTMENT SCORE over time, with the x-axis showing dates from mid-October to the present. The line climbed steadily, with dips she could now identify: the period in November when Caleb's excuses about video chat had accumulated and she'd started to feel uneasy, the drop in late January when he'd told her she was "being a lot" and she'd canceled her flight to Portland and cried on this couch, in this apartment, in the arms of the woman who had scripted the line that made her cry. The line had recovered after each dip, climbing higher than before. The overall trajectory was up and to the right, like a stock price or a heart rate monitor approaching a crisis.

Below the graph, a summary box:

> **Engagement metrics:**
> Messages exchanged: 2,847
> Average daily messages: 19.3
> Average sentiment (agent → target): 0.89
> Average sentiment (target → agent): 0.91
> Intimate content exchanged: Yes (text + image, Jan 14)
> Financial transfers: 3
> Total amount: $12,900

She looked at the last line for a long time. $12,900. It was rendered in the same neutral font as every other data point, the same size as the average daily messages and the sentiment scores. Twelve thousand nine hundred dollars and a photograph of her body, and on this screen they occupied the same informational tier as the number of texts she'd sent on a Tuesday in November.

She clicked FINANCIAL TRACKING.

A simple table:

> | Date | Amount | Narrative vector | Target response time |
> |------|--------|------------------|---------------------|
> | Nov 23 | $400 | Bruno emergency surgery | 6 min |
> | Jan 08 | $2,500 | Mother's medical procedure | 2 hr 14 min |
> | Mar 02 | $10,000 | Business partnership investment | 3 days |

*Narrative vector.* The column was labeled *narrative vector.* The stories that had been told to her about a dog's intestinal blockage and a mother's insurance complications and a landscape architecture partnership — these were narrative vectors. Plot devices. Tools for moving money from her account to an account she had believed belonged to a man in Portland and which belonged — she knew this now, had known it since she clicked the first tab, before she'd had any evidence, known it the same wordless way she could read a novel's ending in its first chapter — to a Venmo account Simone had set up.

The money had gone to Simone. It had always been going to Simone.

She knew because there was only one person in her life who knew that she responded well to self-deprecating humor, who knew about her father and the thing about being interesting, who knew about the novel and the shame, who knew exactly how she felt about her body and her specific shade of loneliness. There was only one person who had all of this information, seventeen years of it, accumulated through a thousand conversations and a hundred confessions and the slow, voluntary dismantling of every privacy Nora had ever maintained. One person, and that person was walking back from the Thai place around the corner, carrying pad see ew and tom kha gai, and she would be here in ten minutes.

Nora clicked back to the conversation log and scrolled to January.

She found the night. January fourteenth. The night the conversation had shifted from tender to explicit, the night she had typed things she had never typed to anyone, the night her body had responded to words on a screen as if the words were hands, as if the distance between her bed and Portland were the length of an arm reaching across warm sheets. She found her messages. She found the responses — the gray messages, tagged INTIMACY-03, 0.96 sentiment, 2.1-second latency.

And she found the photo.

It was in the conversation log as a thumbnail — small enough that the details were compressed, large enough that she recognized the angle, the light from her bedside lamp, the curve of her own hip, the stripe of the navy pillowcase behind her. Her photo. The one she'd taken alone in her bedroom with the door closed and the blinds drawn, with a feeling in her chest that was equal parts want and terror, the feeling of offering something to someone you trusted enough to see you. She'd sent it to what she had believed was a person. She'd sent it to a text field that fed a prompt set called INTIMACY-03, and the prompt set had generated a response in 2.1 seconds with a confidence score of 0.96, and that response had made her feel desired and seen, and the feeling had been real even though its source was a database and a language model and her best friend's careful, careful notes about what made her feel safe.

She closed the laptop.

She closed it exactly as she had found it, lid flat, edge aligned with the stack of wireframe printouts. She looked at the mug that said DESIGN IS A VERB. She looked at the candle on the kitchen counter. She stood up from the desk chair and walked to the bathroom and closed the door behind her and sat on the edge of the bathtub.

The bathroom was small. Simone's bathroom had always been small — a tub with a shower curtain printed with blue fish, a toilet, a pedestal sink with a crack running diagonally through the basin that the landlord had never fixed. Simone's shampoo and conditioner were lined up on the tub's rim: a drugstore brand, the same one she'd been using since college because she couldn't afford the salon brands, and she'd once told Nora this and immediately made a joke about it so the moment wouldn't curdle into pity. Nora remembered the joke. She remembered laughing. She remembered the slight stiffness in Simone's face when she'd said the word "afford" and the speed with which she'd converted that stiffness into comedy.

She looked at her hands. They were resting on her knees, the fingers slightly spread, and they looked like her hands. Normal hands. They did not look like the hands of a woman who had just read a technical document describing, in the language of interface design, how to make her fall in love with a software application managed by the person whose bathtub she was sitting on.

The room had dropped away but the room was still there. The blue fish on the shower curtain were still blue. The crack in the sink was still running diagonally. The grout between the floor tiles had darkened unevenly, darker on the right than the left, which meant Simone mopped starting from the right side of the room, and this was the kind of detail Nora's brain was offering her now — observable, meaningless, safe. She took it. She cataloged the grout and the fish and the crack and the plastic cap on the shampoo bottle that was slightly askew and she held these details like a railing on a staircase that had lost its steps.

She sat there for what she later estimated was four minutes. She counted the radiator's clicking cycles through the bathroom wall — each cycle was roughly thirty seconds. In eight cycles, Simone would be back. She needed to be on the couch when Simone came through the door, because if she was in the bathroom, Simone would make a joke about the wine going right through her, and they would both laugh, and that laugh would be the first thing Nora had to fake, and she wanted the first thing she faked to be smaller than a laugh. She wanted it to be a smile. She could do a smile.

She stood up. She looked at herself in the mirror above the cracked sink. Her face was the same face. Her eyes were dry. She looked exactly like a woman who had spent a pleasant Saturday afternoon at her best friend's apartment drinking Côtes du Rhône and arguing about novels, which was, from any angle but the one she was now standing at, exactly what had happened.

She opened the bathroom door and walked to the couch and sat down and picked up her wine glass and took a sip. The wine tasted like wine. The light through the window had shifted — more golden now, less undecided. It had made up its mind about spring.

---

The door opened and Simone came in carrying a plastic bag with the green logo of the Thai place, the handles knotted at the top like always. She'd taken off her jacket on the stairs and was holding it bunched in her free hand, and her cheeks were flushed from the walk, and for a half-second Nora saw her the way a stranger would have: a tall woman with a precise bob and good bones and an expression already arranging itself into warmth, into welcome.

"The tom kha smelled so good I almost drank it on the sidewalk," Simone said, setting the bag on the counter. "Also, I think the guy at the register is into me. He called me 'beautiful lady.' Either he's flirting or he calls everyone that, which is equally possible and somehow less flattering."

"Maybe he just has good taste," Nora said.

"Or limited English and a strong customer retention strategy." Simone grinned — her real grin, the crooked one that pulled left — and got plates from the cabinet. The white ones with the blue trim that she'd found at a stoop sale two years ago and was unreasonably proud of, because Simone found most of her beautiful things secondhand and had made a skill of it, a way of having things that looked expensive without paying what expensive cost. Nora had always admired this about her. She had said so, more than once. She had said, *You're so good at finding things*, meaning it as praise, not understanding that to Simone the compliment might have carried a different weight — the weight of a woman who could afford retail complimenting a woman who couldn't on her ability to make do.

Simone unpacked the containers: pad see ew, tom kha, an order of spring rolls they hadn't discussed but which Simone had added without asking, because Simone always added something extra, a reflex of generosity that Nora had always read as love and was now reading as — she stopped this thought. She set it down carefully, like holding a glass she could feel cracking under her fingers. Not yet. Later. She would think about it later, when she was alone, when she could think about it without her face being watched by the person she was thinking about.

They sat on the couch with their plates on their laps because Simone's kitchen table was covered in work papers, which was always the case, the table functioning as a second desk rather than a place to eat. The pad see ew was good. It was always good from this place — the noodles had the right chew and the sauce was darker than it should have been, almost caramelized, which was the part Nora liked best. She ate and tasted the food and recognized, from a great clinical distance, that her body was doing its work — chewing, swallowing, processing — while her mind ran along a separate track, reviewing what she had seen on the screen as if it were a manuscript she'd just finished for the first time, noting the architecture, the choices, the places where the seams showed.

"So the book," Simone said, twirling noodles around her fork. "The one I was telling you about. I looked it up — it's called *The Salt Line*. It's more speculative than I thought. Dystopian, kind of."

"Oh," Nora said. "Right. I was trying to remember the title."

"You could've just Googled it."

"My phone died."

"Again?" Simone shook her head. "You need to replace that battery. Or just get the new one. You can afford it."

The sentence passed through Nora like something with a temperature — not hot, not cold, but present. *You can afford it.* Three words Simone had probably said a dozen times before, words that were unremarkable in the vocabulary of a friendship where one person made more and spent more freely. Three words that had never, until this afternoon, carried the specific weight they carried now. Nora heard them and filed them and said, "I know, I keep putting it off," and put another forkful of noodle in her mouth because chewing was a perfectly good reason not to be speaking.

They talked. Nora listened to herself talk from a remove, half her attention on the sounds she was producing, the other half navigating terrain she couldn't see. Simone told her about a conflict at work, something involving a product launch being delayed because the design team and the engineering team had different definitions of "done," and she was funny about it, doing voices for the different stakeholders, rolling her eyes at the PM who kept using the word "alignment" as if saying it enough would make it true. Nora asked questions at the right intervals. She offered opinions that were close enough to her real opinions that the performance didn't require invention, only subtraction — she was being herself minus one dimension, a photograph of a room, convincing until you tried to walk into it.

Simone asked about Caleb. She always asked about Caleb.

"How's he doing? The partnership thing — any updates?"

Nora had prepared for this without knowing she was preparing. The answer was assembled and waiting, fully formed, the same arrival as a word she'd been circling for an hour that finally settled into the sentence. "He's still sorting out the details. I told him not to rush."

"That's smart." Simone nodded. Her voice had the specific texture of a friend who cared — warm, interested, a little protective. It was a beautiful performance. Nora could see it now — the scaffolding behind the warmth, the structural choices made to produce a specific effect in the listener. She was the reader. She had been the reader for five months, turning pages Simone had written, believing the story because the author knew her well enough to write one she'd believe.

"I told him I'm not in a rush," Nora said, and she smiled, and the smile worked, and she took another bite.

They finished dinner. Simone opened a second bottle of wine — not the Côtes du Rhône, a cheaper one from the fridge, the kind with a screw cap that she bought in four-packs — and Nora drank a glass because not drinking would have been conspicuous. They talked about Simone's date the previous week with a man from a workout class who had used the word "synergy" nonironically in conversation to describe his approach to relationships, and Nora laughed, and the laugh came out right, and she thought about how the human face has forty-three muscles and she was firing them in the correct sequence at the correct intervals, like a pilot running a checklist she'd memorized so long ago the motions didn't require thought.

At eight-thirty she said she should head out. Early morning — she had a manuscript to finish by Monday. This was true. It was also beside the point. She put on her jacket and Simone hugged her at the door, one arm around Nora's shoulders and a squeeze that lasted the right number of seconds, and Nora felt the arm and felt Simone's warmth through her shirt and understood that the body holding her was the body of the person who had written *she responds well to self-deprecation, don't overdo the sincerity, mirror her sentence length.* The body that had tagged her photograph with a sentiment score. The body that had watched her fall in love like a UX designer watching a user test — noting the friction points, adjusting the flow, measuring engagement.

"Text me when you're home," Simone said.

"Always do."

The hallway smelled like someone else's dinner — garlic, something fried, the layered cooking smells of a building where everyone was making a different version of Saturday night. Nora took the stairs because the elevator in Simone's building took forever and because she needed the motion, the physical act of descending, the sound of her own shoes on the concrete steps organizing the space between the apartment she was leaving and the street that would carry her home.

Outside, the air was March air — raw enough to feel useful against her face, damp in a way that was not rain but the promise of it. She walked to the L train. The station was the usual Saturday-evening crowd: couples going somewhere nicer, groups going somewhere louder, a man playing saxophone at the bottom of the stairs with his case open and a few bills folded inside. She put a dollar in the case because she always did, because she was still, from the outside, a person who put dollars in open saxophone cases, and the outside was the only version of herself she was willing to operate right now.

On the train she sat in a window seat and pressed her forehead against the glass and closed her eyes. The L rattled through the tunnel with its familiar rhythm, and she could think now, with the distance of motion, with the tunnel strobing past too fast to resolve into anything she'd have to look at. She thought: *she responds well to self-deprecation.* She thought: *mirror her sentence length.* She thought: *narrative vector.*

She thought about Caleb's message from three days ago, the one about his mother's kitchen. The one that had made something ache behind her ribs with the specific pleasure of being understood by someone who had never stood in the same room as her. The message had been tagged INTIMACY-03 with a 0.92 sentiment score and a 4.3-second latency. The ache had been real. The ache was still real, which was the part she couldn't make sense of — the feeling had not retroactively changed just because its source had. She had felt understood, and the understanding had been manufactured, and the feeling of being understood was still there, like warmth from a lamp after you've learned the lamp is a stage light pointed at you from the wings.

A couple across the aisle was arguing in whispers, the woman's fingers gripping the man's jacket sleeve, the man staring straight ahead. Nora watched them without wanting to and thought about how she'd sent a photograph of her body to a text field. She thought about how she'd lain in her bed and typed sentences she'd never said aloud and pressed send and waited, her pulse loud in her ears, for a response that had arrived in 2.1 seconds with a confidence score of 0.96. She thought about how Simone had read those words — or how the system had logged them — or both, and it didn't matter which, because in either case the most private thing Nora had ever done had been observed from the other side of an interface by the person who had designed the conditions for her to do it.

The train stopped at her station. She walked up the stairs and turned left and walked the three blocks to her building and unlocked the vestibule door and climbed to the third floor and put her key in the lock and opened the door and stepped inside and closed it behind her and turned the deadbolt. The series of actions was so familiar it felt like the one reliable thing — the sequence her body had done a thousand times, the route from outside to in, and the deadbolt's sound was the sound of a border being established.

The apartment was dark. She didn't turn on the lights. She stood in her hallway with her jacket still on and her bag over her shoulder and she could feel it beginning — not from her eyes, from somewhere deeper, the way a fissure starts in the middle of a wall that looks fine from the outside and works its way outward until the surface can't hold. She dropped her bag on the floor. She walked to the couch and sat down and pressed her face into the cushion and the first sound she made was not crying exactly but something closer to the sound of air being released from a space that had been sealed too long, a sound that had no shape and no control and that she would not have recognized as coming from her own body if she'd heard it from another room.

Then she cried. She cried the way she hadn't since the night her father left for Seattle — without composition, without awareness of how she sounded, without the part of her brain that was always editing, always revising, always finding the better word for the thing she was feeling. She cried until the cushion under her cheek was wet and her throat was raw and the crying was no longer about anything specific — not about Caleb, who had never existed, not about Simone, who had existed too much and too well, not about the $12,900 or the sentiment scores or the prompt set called INTIMACY-03, but about the fact that she had been happy. She had been genuinely, structurally happy for the first time in two years, and the happiness had been real in every way that mattered to a body and a heart and a brain that could not tell the difference between being loved and being optimized for engagement, and it had been built by the person she called when she was scared, the person whose shower curtain had blue fish on it and whose shampoo was the drugstore brand and whose voice saying *that's who you are, you're generous* had been the voice of the woman holding the other end of the string.

She cried until she stopped. Then she sat in the dark and listened to the sounds of her building — a television through the wall, water running in the pipes overhead, the particular creak in the upstairs apartment's floor that meant her neighbor was walking to his kitchen. Other people's Saturday nights, continuing without interruption, the ordinary sounds of lives that had not rearranged themselves in the time it took to click a browser tab.

She took off her jacket. She took off her shoes. She plugged in her phone — it had been dead all evening, which meant she'd missed whatever Caleb had sent, which meant she'd missed messages from an algorithm managed by a woman she'd hugged forty-five minutes ago — and this was the first thought that almost made her laugh, a laugh so bent it might have torn something on its way out.

The phone powered on. Three messages from Caleb. She didn't open them. She set the phone face-down on the coffee table and went to the bathroom and turned on the shower and stood under the water until it ran cold.

In bed, in the dark, with her hair still damp against the pillow, she stared at the ceiling and thought about what she'd read. She thought about Simone sitting at that desk — the desk under the window, the one Nora had walked past a hundred times — writing those notes. *She responds well to self-deprecation.* This was true. Nora did respond to self-deprecation. She always had. It was something she liked about herself — her willingness to be disarmed by humor, to trust someone who could laugh at themselves, to open a door for a person who approached it with the right kind of knock. Simone knew this because Simone knew her, because Nora had taught her, over seventeen years, exactly how to open every door in the building. She had handed over the keys one at a time — a story about her father, a confession about her novel, a night of crying about a man who wasn't worth the tears, a night of laughing about nothing because the nothing was the point — and each key had been received with what Nora had understood as love and what she now understood was also inventory. Love and inventory. Both. The love had been real. That was the thing she could not yet make fit inside the shape of what she'd found on the screen — the love had been real, and the inventory had been real, and they had lived inside the same person, inside the same friendship, for years, maybe for the whole time, and Nora had only ever seen one of them.

She didn't sleep for a long time. When she finally did, she dreamed about nothing she could remember afterward, which was its own small mercy.


# Chapter 16

She started with the oldest messages.

It was Sunday, the morning after. Nora sat on her bedroom floor with her back against the bed and her phone in both hands and the radiator clicking the way it did when the building's heat came on, which it shouldn't have — it was late March, the super had supposedly switched to the spring schedule, but old buildings ran on their own calendars and no one had told the boiler that winter was finished. The heat was wrong for the season and the room was too warm and she was still wearing the clothes she'd come home in last night, the jeans and the striped shirt she'd put on to go to Simone's apartment for Thai food, which she had eaten, all of it, every bite of the pad see ew and two of the summer rolls and the mango sticky rice, because she had been performing the role of a woman who did not know, and performing had apparently included appetite.

She opened WhatsApp and scrolled to Caleb Mora's thread and went all the way back to the beginning.

The first message was from October. Five months ago. He'd responded to her Hinge prompt — the one about the last book that made her cry — and she'd moved the conversation to WhatsApp within a day because the Hinge interface felt too formal, too transactional, and she'd wanted the intimacy of a messaging app where she could see when he was typing. She remembered that desire. She remembered the little pulse of courage it had taken to suggest the move, as if proposing a change of venue on a date that was going well.

*What was the last book that actually wrecked you?* That was his opening. His. Its. She didn't have a pronoun for what Caleb was. She read the message and tried to see it as what it was — a prompt response generated by an AI system configured by her best friend, calibrated to her known literary preferences, deployed on a dating app where her profile had been screened in advance by someone who knew her exact taste in men because she'd spent seventeen years describing it. She tried to see the machinery. But the message still looked like a message from a man she'd liked, and the feeling it had produced — the small, warm surprise of being asked a question she actually wanted to answer — was still in her body like a residue that reading couldn't remove.

She kept scrolling.

The early conversations were the worst, because they were the best. The chemistry was impeccable. Every reference he made landed in the center of her sensibility — the Joan Didion essay she'd underlined in college, the Vonnegut novel she'd reread every November, the Alice Munro story she used as a calibration text when she was editing fiction and needed to remember what a perfect sentence looked like. He knew. Of course he knew. Simone had watched her read those books, had heard her talk about them at dinners and on subway platforms and in the particular late-night voice Nora used when she was being honest about what she loved rather than impressive about what she'd read. Simone had fed every one of those references into a system and the system had fed them back, and Nora had felt understood.

She'd felt *understood*. That was the cruelest part, or one of the cruelest parts — the cruelties were stacking up and she didn't have a ranking system yet. She had felt, talking to Caleb, like she was being known in real time by someone who had chosen to pay attention to exactly the things she wanted attended to. And she had been. She had been known in real time. Just not by the person she thought was doing the knowing.

October 19: *I keep thinking about that thing you said about editing being an act of faith — that you're trusting the writer meant something, even when the sentence doesn't land yet. I think that's how I feel about new buildings too. The design is a promise. The landscape has to believe the promise will hold.* She remembered reading that message on her lunch break, standing in the publishing house kitchen with a cup of coffee going cold in her hand, and the specific quality of the feeling — not just attraction but recognition, the sense that someone was speaking a language she'd been waiting to hear. She'd shown the message to Margo that afternoon. Margo had said, "Okay, this one's different."

This one was different because Simone had designed it to be different. The landscape architecture metaphor, the mirroring of Nora's own vocabulary about craft — Simone had written that. Or Simone had configured a system to produce it, which was the same thing, or close enough that the distinction didn't matter when you were the person on the receiving end staring at your phone and feeling your chest open up like someone had found the latch.

She kept scrolling — past November, past December, into the weeks where the conversations changed. The messages got later, got darker, got warm in a different way. She found the first one on January 2nd. *I keep thinking about your hands.* She had typed that alone in bed with her body open in a way she'd forgotten it could be, and what followed was a conversation she'd never shown anyone — not Margo, not Simone, not anyone — because the language she'd used was meant only for the space between her and the person she thought she was talking to. She had been specific. She had told Caleb what she wanted done to her, had used words she would have cut from a manuscript for being too exposed and sent them anyway, because this wasn't a draft, this was wanting, and wanting had its own grammar that only worked when you meant it. The AI had matched her — tenderness braided through heat, the exact rhythm of someone who knew when to slow down and when not to. It knew because Simone knew. Because Nora had spent years telling her best friend how she liked to be touched and talked to and wanted, over wine and in late-night phone calls and in the confessional register that closeness made possible, and Simone had taken all of it and entered it into a system and the system had whispered it back.

And the photo. January 3rd. She'd taken it in the bathroom mirror at one in the morning — the black bra, her face cropped out of frame because the bravery was about her body, not her expression — and sent it with her pulse in her ears and her hands not quite steady. That picture was in a conversation log on Simone's laptop. It existed as data in an interface alongside sentiment analysis and engagement graphs and prompt-engineering notes. Simone may have seen it arrive. May have looked at Nora's body — the body she'd sat next to on couches and subways and restaurant banquettes for seventeen years, the body she'd helped zip into dresses, the body she'd held on her couch while Nora cried about a fight Simone had scripted — and registered it as an engagement indicator, evidence that the user's investment had reached the threshold for the next extraction. The skin on Nora's arms contracted, a slow crawl of gooseflesh that started at her wrists and rolled up to her shoulders and settled at the back of her neck, and she held the phone and breathed through it and could not make the feeling stop — the animal knowledge that someone had seen her naked who was never supposed to see her, and the seeing had been logged, and the log was still open, and the nakedness could not be taken back.

Nora closed WhatsApp and opened her bank app.

The money was easier to look at than the words, because money was specific. Money didn't have a tone of voice or a memory attached to every digit. She went through her transaction history and found them:

November 23. $400. Venmo. Bruno the dog's emergency surgery. She remembered sending it — the warm flush of generosity, the sense that she was being the kind of person she wanted to be, someone who helped without calculating. She remembered telling Simone about it the next day, sitting across from her at the café on Bedford, and Simone saying *It's not insane. It's generous. That's who you are.* Simone, who had scripted the dog's emergency and received the $400 and used it to pay off a credit card minimum and then sat across from Nora and said *that's who you are* with the Venmo notification still warm on her phone.

January 8. $2,500. Venmo. Caleb's mother's medical procedure. Insurance complications. Nora had hesitated on this one — not long, but enough to notice the hesitation, which she'd interpreted at the time as healthy caution and which she now understood as the faintest signal from some sub-basement of her judgment that something was wrong. She'd sent it anyway. She'd overridden the hesitation with love, or with what she'd thought was love, which produced the same neurochemical event regardless of its origin.

March 4. $10,000. Venmo. The landscape architecture partnership. Caleb's future. Their future, he'd said — he'd talked about Portland, about a life there, about space for a garden where she could read. She had sent ten thousand dollars to a future that did not exist in a city where no one named Caleb Mora had ever designed a garden or walked a dog or thought about her at all. She'd sent it three weeks ago. The money had cleared. The money was gone.

$400 + $2,500 + $10,000 = $12,900.

She looked at the number on her screen. It was not a devastating amount of money — she wouldn't miss rent, she wouldn't go hungry, her savings account would recover in six or eight months of careful spending. If her mother had heard the figure she would have cried, but that was Diane, who still thought in schoolteacher economics where $12,900 was a summer's salary. For Nora it was — what? Three months of her 401(k) contributions. A good vacation. The advance on a first novel from a mid-tier publisher, which was a comparison she didn't want to be making but which arrived anyway, because her brain processed financial data through the only industry she understood.

The number wasn't the injury. The number was the receipt.

She put the phone down on the floor beside her and pressed her palms flat against the hardwood. The floor was warm from the radiator. Her fingers spread across the grain of the boards and she noticed, with the hyper-specific attention of someone who was not ready to feel the large thing yet, that the polish was wearing thin along the path between her bed and the door — the trail of her own daily passage visible in the finish, a desire path through her own apartment. She stared at this wear pattern and breathed. The radiator clicked. Outside, a car alarm started and stopped, the truncated two-note chirp of someone accidentally hitting the panic button on their key fob and immediately correcting it.

Then she picked the phone back up and opened her text thread with Simone.

This was the second audit, and it was worse.

She scrolled back to October, to the week she'd matched with Caleb, and read forward. Every text from Simone about Caleb now had a second text underneath it, a ghost text, the message behind the message. The surface was friendship — curiosity, support, the shared excitement of her best friend's new romance. The subsurface was authorship.

October 16, Nora to Simone: *i think i found a human person on this app??* Simone's reply: *TELL ME EVERYTHING. what's his name. what does he look like. what did he say.* She'd read that at the time as the uncomplicated enthusiasm of a best friend who wanted her to be happy. She read it now as the creator checking in on the performance. The questions weren't curiosity. They were diagnostics.

The next weeks were a wall of it. *Nor, that's amazing. He really gets you.* And: *Good! You should be telling people. He's worth talking about.* And: *You sound so happy. I love this for you.* Nora scrolled through them fast, the way she speed-read the opening chapters of a manuscript when she already knew the voice was under control — not reading for surprise, reading for pattern. Each text the right temperature, the right warmth, the right note of happiness-for-you that never once tipped into jealousy, because of course it didn't, because for Simone every text Nora sent back was a response metric and every person Nora told about Caleb was another stake driven into the ground, another witness to the fiction, another reason to stay. Simone had been running a conversion funnel. Nora was the user. The product was a man who didn't exist.

November 28, the day after Nora sent the $400: *It's not insane. It's generous. That's who you are.* Nora stared at this one for a long time. She read it once as a friend's reassurance. She read it again as a scammer's encouragement. She read it a third time and realized it was both — that Simone had been both at the same time, that the warmth was real even though the context was fraudulent, and that this double nature was the thing she couldn't parse, the thing that broke her usual equipment. She could read subtext for a living. She could identify unreliable narration in a manuscript from three sentences in. But she couldn't read a message from someone who simultaneously loved her and was stealing from her, because those two things weren't supposed to fit in the same person, and yet they did, and the person was Simone, and Simone had been both for months, maybe longer, maybe always.

January 14, after the manufactured fight, when Nora had been crying on Simone's couch about Caleb telling her she was "being a lot": *He shouldn't have said that. But people say things they don't mean when they're scared. He's not going anywhere, Nor. Neither am I.* Nora's hands stopped moving on the phone. She could do the surface-and-subsurface thing with this one — she could call it performance, cruelty, the developer debugging a crash. But she remembered the weight of Simone's arm around her shoulders that night. The specific heat of Simone's hand through the fabric of Nora's shirt. And Simone's fingers had been trembling. Nora remembered this now with a clarity that felt physical — the faint shake against her shoulder, a tremor Simone hadn't tried to hide or maybe couldn't. She had scripted the fight, yes. She had manufactured the pain. And then she had held Nora through it, and the holding had been — Nora stared at the message and could not finish the sentence. The holding had been real. Or the holding had been part of it. Or the holding had been both, the way everything with Simone turned out to be both, and Nora could not locate the seam because maybe there was no seam, maybe a person could engineer your suffering and then genuinely ache when they watched you feel it, and if that was true then the word *cruelty* didn't work and the word *love* didn't work and there was no word for the thing Simone had done to her on that couch, which was hold her and mean it while being the reason she needed holding.

But Simone hadn't been calculating. That was the part that didn't work as fiction. Simone had been *doing her job*. The Caleb project wasn't spite — or it wasn't only spite. It was work. Simone had approached it with the same competence she brought to UX design: identifying user needs, building an interface, testing and iterating, optimizing for engagement. The love Nora felt for Caleb was, in Simone's professional vocabulary, a successful user experience. The money was monetization. The cruelty was a feature, not a bug.

Nora put the phone down on her thigh and pressed her fingers against her closed eyes until she saw the static patterns, the formless light. She held them there. The radiator clicked. A siren passed on the street below, the Doppler shift stretching the sound thin before it disappeared. She picked the phone up.

She scrolled forward through December, January, February. She didn't read these twice. She didn't need the surface-subsurface exercise anymore — the architecture was visible now, the way each of Simone's texts had functioned as a load-bearing wall in a structure Nora hadn't known she was standing inside. She cataloged the steered conversations, the redirections when Nora edged toward doubt. Then she found the text from February 24: *If you believe in him, believe in him.* Three days before the $15,000 ask. She looked at the words and did not reinterpret them. There was no second reading. It was a con artist's closing line, timed to the dollar, and she had read it on her lunch break and thought her best friend believed in love.

She read it all, and when she was finished, she set the phone face-down on the floor and sat there with the radiator clicking and the heat that should have been off pressing against her back, and she didn't cry. She had cried last night — standing in her bathroom with the deadbolt turned, her face in a towel she'd grabbed from the rack because she hadn't wanted to hear herself, the sound of her own grief muffled by terrycloth. That was over. The crying had been useful, in the way that vomiting was useful: it emptied something that needed emptying, and afterward there was clarity, and a sore throat, and the strange lightness of having expelled something toxic.

What replaced the grief was not anger. Not yet. It was something more like the feeling she got when she was deep-editing a manuscript that had structural problems — the moment when you stopped trying to fix individual sentences and pulled back far enough to see the whole architecture, all the load-bearing walls and false walls and the places where the author had tried to hide a weakness behind beautiful language. She could see the whole structure now. Every room of it. Every closet where Simone had stashed a lie.

---

She went to work on Monday.

The publishing house was doing what it always did in late March — scrambling toward the spring list, everyone a little frantic, the particular energy of books being finalized and printed and shipped and worried over. Nora sat at her desk and copyedited a galley proof of a debut novel about a woman reinventing herself after a divorce, and the irony of this was not lost on her, but she let it pass through without snagging on it, because irony was a luxury and she was conserving energy for something she hadn't yet defined.

Margo brought her coffee at ten, the way Margo always did, setting the paper cup on Nora's desk with the casual precision of a daily ritual. Margo's hair was in its perpetual ponytail and she was wearing the gray cardigan she wore when she was cold, which was always, because the HVAC in the office ran six degrees below what any human body wanted and no one had successfully complained about it in the three years Nora had worked there.

"You look like shit," Margo said. "Sympathetically."

"Didn't sleep great."

Margo sat on the edge of Nora's desk, which she did when she wanted to talk and didn't want to seem like she was making it a thing. She had her own coffee in her hand, the lid already removed because she'd once read that drinking through a plastic lid released microplastics and had decided, with the absolutism of the recently divorced, that she was done ingesting other people's bad decisions. "Is this a Caleb didn't-sleep-great or a general didn't-sleep-great?"

The name was a knuckle pressed into a bruise. She kept her face on the galley. "General."

"Because I've been wanting to ask, and you know I'm bad at not asking — did he ever do that video call? After the whole thing with his dad?"

"Not yet."

"Nor."

"I know."

"I'm not saying anything you don't already know. I'm just — I had this thought at three in the morning, which is when I do my best catastrophizing, and the thought was: you've sent this man almost thirteen thousand dollars and you've never seen his face move." Margo sipped her coffee. A drop landed on her cardigan and she looked at it with the resigned acceptance of someone who understood that the universe expressed itself through small, daily stains. "That's all. That's the thought. I've said it. You can tell me to fuck off."

"Fuck off," Nora said, and smiled, because the smile was a tool and she could still operate it, even though the mechanism behind it had changed completely since the last time she'd used it in Margo's presence. Margo didn't know that Nora knew. Margo didn't know that the video call was never going to happen because there was no face to move. Margo was still worrying about the possibility that Caleb was a scam. Nora was sitting inside the certainty, and the certainty was a room she couldn't leave, and she couldn't invite Margo in. Not yet. The knowledge wasn't ready to be shared. It was still too raw, still too formless — she didn't have the words yet, or she had too many words and none of them were organized, and Nora didn't say things she hadn't edited.

"Fucking off," Margo said, and squeezed Nora's shoulder as she left, and the pressure of her hand was warm and Nora leaned into it one degree more than she normally would have, because Margo's hand was Margo's hand, attached to Margo's arm, attached to a person who wanted only what she said she wanted, and that simplicity — that legibility — was something Nora hadn't known she could miss until she did.

She worked through the day. She was good at her job and her job was the kind that absorbed attention completely if you let it, and she let it. She read sentences. She fixed commas. She queried an anachronism in chapter twelve of the divorce novel — the protagonist referenced a Spotify playlist in a scene set in 2008, which was too early — and felt a brief, clean satisfaction when the author agreed it was wrong. Small corrections. Fixable problems. A world where errors had solutions and the solutions stayed solved.

At lunch she sat in the office kitchen and ate a salad from the place on Forty-Third Street and scrolled through her phone without reading anything on it, and when a text from Simone appeared — *free for drinks thursday? i found this new place on smith st that does martinis with like actual vermouth* — she typed *yes! sounds great* and hit send and put the phone back on the table and ate a cherry tomato and felt nothing and then felt everything and then ate another cherry tomato.

She took the LIRR to Long Island on Wednesday evening, the 5:47 from Penn Station. Her mother had called twice that week and Nora had let both calls go to voicemail and then called back at times when the conversation would be short, which was a technique she usually reserved for the weeks after a breakup, when Diane's love felt like a searchlight she needed to dodge. Diane hadn't said anything about the avoidance. Diane had said "come to dinner" the way she always said it, which was less an invitation than a gravitational event.

The house smelled like roasting chicken and something with cinnamon — a pie, or the bread pudding Diane made when she was working through a worry, which she called baking and Nora recognized as a form of prayer. The kitchen was warm and overlighted in the way of houses that hadn't been updated since the nineties, the overhead fluorescents casting the particular shadowless brightness that Nora associated with homework and family arguments and being seventeen and desperate to leave, followed by being thirty-four and grateful to return.

"You're thin," Diane said, which was what Diane said when she was worried but hadn't decided what she was worried about. She was wearing an apron over a turtleneck and her reading glasses were pushed up on her head, where they would stay for the next two hours because she forgot they were there every time she pushed them up. She kissed Nora's cheek and held her face for a beat too long, studying it the way she studied essays from her former students — looking for the argument beneath the surface.

"I'm the same size I was two weeks ago."

"Two weeks ago you were thin."

Nora sat at the kitchen table while Diane moved through the room with the efficiency of a woman who had been cooking in the same space for thirty years and could reach the colander without looking. They talked about the spring list at work, about Diane's book club, about the neighbor whose dog had dug up Diane's tulip bulbs for the third year running — "I don't blame the dog, I blame Linda, who lets that animal roam like it's the Serengeti out there, it's a quarter-acre lot in Massapequa" — and Nora laughed in the right places and asked the right questions and was present in the way that she'd been present at Simone's apartment two nights ago eating Thai food, which was to say: her body in the chair, her voice in the room, her self somewhere slightly behind her own eyes, watching the performance from the wings.

Diane served the chicken with roasted potatoes and green beans and a salad she'd made with the vinaigrette she was proud of, the one with the shallots. She put too much food on Nora's plate. She always put too much food on Nora's plate. Nora ate it.

"How's your young man?" Diane asked, and Nora watched her mother's face as she said it — the careful hope, the attempt to ask lightly, the second question folded inside the first, which was *are you happy* and also *are you being careful* and also, underneath everything, the question that Diane had been asking since Nora was old enough to date, which was *is this person going to hurt you, and is there anything I can do about it if they are.*

"He's good," Nora said. "Busy with work. We're talking about him maybe visiting in April."

Lies. Clean, smooth, easy lies. Nora heard herself deliver them with the fluency of someone who had been lied to so effectively that she'd absorbed the technique. She was protecting her mother from a truth that would frighten her, which was the justification Simone had probably used too — I'm not hurting her, I'm managing the information, I'm controlling the flow — and the parallel made Nora's throat close around a piece of potato that she chewed and swallowed with a sip of water while Diane nodded and said "April's lovely, the parks will be in bloom."

After dinner they washed the dishes together, Diane washing, Nora drying, the choreography of it so practiced that their hands never collided over the sink. Diane told a story about a former student who'd emailed to say he'd published a poem — "a real poem, in a real journal, not one of those online things where they publish anyone who submits" — and her pride was so specific and uncomplicated that Nora had to look away, toward the dark window over the sink where her own reflection was translucent and layered over the backyard she'd grown up in, the lawn invisible in the dark.

She could tell her. She could say: *Mom, Caleb isn't real. He's a program. Simone made him. I sent twelve thousand nine hundred dollars to a Venmo account that Simone set up under a fake name, and every conversation I've had for five months has been with an algorithm that Simone configured using everything she knows about me, which is everything.* She could say this and her mother would hold her and cry and make tea and it would feel like rescue, and Nora wanted rescue the way she wanted sleep — with her whole exhausted body, with the dumb animal need for someone else to carry the weight.

But she didn't.

She didn't because the telling would change the shape of what she knew before she'd finished understanding it. She didn't because Diane would want to act — to call someone, to fix something, to deploy the formidable machinery of maternal love against a problem that maternal love couldn't solve. She didn't because the story, told out loud to her mother in this kitchen, would become a story about a victim and a villain, and Nora wasn't ready to be the victim yet, and she wasn't sure Simone was only the villain, and the not-sureness was the part she needed to sit with before she could speak.

And she didn't because — this was the part she wasn't ready to examine — she wanted to confront Simone first. Before anyone else knew. Before the story became public and acquired its own momentum. She wanted to sit across from Simone and say the words and watch Simone's face when the words landed, and she wanted that moment to belong to the two of them alone, the way the friendship had belonged to the two of them alone, the way the betrayal had been intimate in the specific way that only seventeen years of closeness could produce. She wanted the conversation to happen inside the closed system of their relationship before the system was opened to the world.

This desire frightened her slightly. It was too controlled. It was too much like something Simone would do — the strategic timing, the managed reveal, the insistence on being the one who determined when and how and where the truth was deployed. But Nora had been managed for months, had been a user in someone else's flow, and the need to take the controls back was not strategy. It was survival.

On the train home, the LIRR rocking through the dark stretch between Massapequa and Jamaica, Nora sat by the window and looked at nothing — the occasional streetlight, a gas station, the reflection of her own face superimposed on the rushing dark. She thought about what she would say. She didn't rehearse — rehearsal was Simone's method, and Nora had spent seventeen years trusting spontaneity between them, showing up and saying what she felt, believing that the space between them was safe for unedited speech. She would not rehearse. But she would choose the time, and the place, and she would walk through Simone's door knowing what Simone did not yet know she knew, and for the first time in this entire story, the information asymmetry would be hers.

The anger arrived on the train. Not all at once. It came the way spring came to the Northeast — in degrees, mixed with other weathers, deniable for a while. She felt it first as a tightening in her hands around the strap of her bag. Then as a clarity in her thinking that had the cold, clean quality of a freshly formatted page. Then as a sentence that assembled itself in her mind with the precision of a line she would never cut from a manuscript: *She used every single thing I ever told her to build a machine that told me what I wanted to hear, and then she charged me for it.*

That was the sentence. That was the architecture of the injury, rendered in language Nora could stand behind. She held it in her mind as the train pulled into Jamaica and she transferred to the subway and rode the rest of the way to Brooklyn in the close, warm, swaying dark of the underground, surrounded by strangers looking at their phones, each of them talking to someone, or reading something, or watching something, all of them trusting that the faces on their screens belonged to real people with real interiority and real claims on the word *love*, and Nora sat among them and held her sentence and breathed and did not cry.

She got home at ten. She locked the door. She turned the deadbolt. She changed into a shirt that was hers and shorts that were hers and she washed her face with water that came from her own faucet in her own apartment in the life she'd built without anyone engineering it for her, and she set her phone face-down on the nightstand and turned off the light and lay in the dark and listened to the radiator click and the street noise through the thin walls and she thought: *Thursday. Simone wants drinks on Thursday. I'll go.*

And she would tell her. Not what she felt — that was too much, too soon, too shapeless. She would tell her what she knew. She would say the name of the platform and watch the word land and she would not look away.

Nora closed her eyes. Sleep took a long time. But it came, eventually, the way all involuntary things eventually came — on its own schedule, without her permission, carrying her into a darkness that asked nothing of her at all.


# Chapter 17

She brought wine. That was the detail she'd thought about most — not what she'd say or how she'd say it but whether she should bring the Barbera, because she always brought wine to Simone's and showing up empty-handed would be a tell. She'd stood in the wine aisle at the deli on her corner for four minutes choosing the same twelve-dollar bottle she always chose, aware that the price point was itself a piece of information she'd never had to think about before and that Simone — she now understood — had always been thinking about.

The buzzer had been broken since November. Nora texted *here* from the sidewalk and waited for the click of the street door. The sound came — the same small mechanical surrender it always made — and she pulled the door open and started up.

Three flights. She'd walked these stairs so many times she could do them with her eyes closed — the crack in the third step, the hall light that buzzed at a frequency just below conscious perception, the door where the 4 had fallen off and left a clean rectangle of darker paint. These were the landmarks of the most important friendship of her adult life, and she catalogued them now with the particular attention you give to things you're about to lose.

Simone opened the door in a tank top and jeans, a wooden spoon in one hand, her bob slightly grown out in a way that made her look younger and less composed than usual. Behind her the apartment smelled like garlic and olive oil and that candle — the too-sweet floral one she burned to make the studio smell like something other than a studio. Nora had always found the candle's sweetness faintly excessive. Now it smelled like evidence.

"Oh, good, wine," Simone said, taking the bottle. "I'm making aglio e olio. My nonna's version, which is basically an act of garlic aggression. Fair warning."

"Perfect," Nora said. She stepped inside and hung her jacket on the hook by the door — her hook, the one Simone had assigned her after the first time Nora's coat had slid off the desk chair onto the floor. Every surface of this friendship had its small rituals. She had never noticed how many of them Simone had designed.

The apartment was the same. The desk by the window, the laptop closed on its surface, the small bookshelf with Simone's design manuals and the Italian cookbook she never used and three of the novels Nora had given her over the years. The bed was made — Simone always made her bed, even in a studio where the bed was also the couch was also the guest seating, because Simone understood that a made bed in a small space was the difference between living in an apartment and living in a room. Nora had always admired this about her. She still admired it. The admiration and the fury were not, it turned out, mutually exclusive.

Simone poured wine into the two actual stemmed glasses she kept for Nora's visits — not the mugs she drank from alone. She handed one over. Their fingers touched on the transfer, a tiny contact that had always meant nothing and now meant nothing in a different, heavier register.

"How was your week?" Simone asked, turning back to the stove. "You said the Brennan manuscript was eating you alive."

"She keeps burying her best images in relative clauses," Nora said. "Three pages of flat summary and then this gorgeous observation tucked inside a dependent clause like she's embarrassed by it. I keep writing *promote this* in the margins."

"Editors." Simone shook her head. "Little dictators with red pens."

"Track changes, actually. Nobody uses pens anymore. Get the metaphor right."

Simone laughed — the quick, warm laugh that had been the soundtrack of Nora's adult life, the laugh that rewarded a joke at exactly the right amplitude. Nora had heard it a thousand times and never once parsed the mechanics. Now she could see it: the crease around Simone's eyes arriving a fraction before the sound, as if the expression were being staged for the voice to follow. Whether this had always been performance or whether Nora was reading performance into everything now, she couldn't tell. The uncertainty was its own kind of injury.

They talked. Simone described a coworker who'd been sending passive-aggressive Slack messages about meeting-room bookings. Nora told her about a submission she'd read that morning — a first novel, promising but overwritten, the kind of book that needed a ruthless edit and an author willing to endure one. They fell into the easy pattern, the rhythm of two women who'd been narrating their lives to each other for seventeen years. The conversation was warm, quick, funny. It was their best mode. It was the mode Nora was about to destroy.

Simone drained the pasta and tossed it in the garlic oil with a practiced flick that sent a spray of olive oil across the backsplash. She carried two plates to the small table pushed against the wall beneath the window — the table couldn't go anywhere else; the apartment's geometry allowed one configuration and this was it. Outside, the sky was doing what it did in late March, holding onto the last light with a quality that was less golden than bruised, the sun gone but the sky not yet ready to admit it.

"Eat," Simone said. "Aglio e olio has a half-life of about six minutes."

Nora picked up her fork. She twirled pasta and ate. It was good — the garlic sharp, the chili flakes providing just enough heat, the pasta cooked with the specific al dente confidence of a recipe someone's grandmother had perfected over decades. She ate three bites. She drank her wine. She set the glass down on the table, aligning its base with the water ring already stained there, a ring she'd probably made herself on some other visit, some other evening when this table was just a table and not the surface on which she was about to end the most important relationship of her life.

"Tell me about OpenClaw," she said.

The sounds of the apartment reorganized themselves in the silence that followed. The pot ticked with residual heat on the stove. A car passed on Grand Street, its bass thudding through the thin walls. The candle hissed where the wick had gotten too long, the flame leaning sideways in a draft from the window.

Simone's face went through something that was not quite a sequence of expressions but more like a system losing power — confusion, blankness, and then a total evacuation, every social signal dropping away at once, like a storefront going dark.

"What?" she said.

Nora waited. She had learned, in seventeen years of editing, that the most powerful tool in a conversation was silence deployed after a precise question. People filled silence. They filled it with the truth or with lies whose shapes revealed the truth anyway.

"I don't — what's OpenClaw? Is that an app or something?"

The performance was exquisite. The pitch was right, the confusion was right, every parameter of a person hearing an unfamiliar word for the first time was calibrated to the millisecond. And Nora could see every joint and seam, because she'd spent two weeks reading Simone's prompt engineering notes and she understood now how Simone built convincing reactions: stay one beat behind the natural response time, mirror the expected register, keep the eyes soft.

"The dashboard on your laptop," Nora said. She kept her voice in the register she used with authors whose manuscripts had structural problems — calm, specific, not unkind, entirely inescapable. "The conversation logs. The sentiment analysis scores. The prompt notes in the margins. *She responds well to self-deprecation. Don't overdo the sincerity. Mirror her sentence length.*" She paused. "Caleb Mora."

Simone's hand moved to the edge of the table. Her fingers pressed against the wood as though testing whether it was solid — whether anything was.

"How did you — "

"You went to pick up Thai food. Two Saturdays ago. My phone was dead and I wanted to look up a book title and I opened your laptop. Chrome was open. The tab was right there."

Nora watched Simone's face as the denial tried to assemble itself and failed — not all at once but piece by piece, her mouth starting shapes that weren't words, her eyes searching Nora's face for a version of this conversation she could survive.

"That was two weeks ago," Nora said. "I closed the laptop exactly the way I found it. I ate the pad Thai. I laughed at your jokes. I rode the G train home and I locked my door and I sat on the floor of my bathroom and I shook for an hour. And then I stopped shaking and I started reading. I went through every text Caleb ever sent me and I read them as what they were, and I read every text you sent me during the same period and I read those as what they were too. Every *he sounds amazing.* Every *you deserve this.* Every *I'm so happy for you.*" She stopped. Her voice had not risen. She was aware that Simone knew her well enough to understand what this flatness meant — Nora loud was Nora upset, but Nora quiet was Nora finished.

"I can explain," Simone said.

Nora looked at her. Just looked. And waited.

***

The thought arrived in fragments, the syntax of emergency.

*She knows. She's known for two weeks. She ate the food. She went home. She didn't call, didn't text, didn't scream, just — sat with it. For two weeks. And then she brought wine.*

Nora was sitting at the table with her hands resting on either side of her plate and her face arranged into something Simone recognized because she'd seen Nora do it to authors whose manuscripts had fundamental problems: the patient, implacable attention of someone who has already finished their analysis and is waiting for you to catch up.

"It started as an experiment," Simone said. Her voice sounded wrong — too high, too fast, a version of herself glitching under load. She tried to slow down. "I was playing with the platform. Testing the conversational AI. I wanted to see if it could sustain a — "

"With me," Nora said.

Two words. They were not a question. They were a correction — the same correction Nora made in manuscripts when a writer used the passive voice to avoid naming the agent of an action. Not *a conversation was initiated.* You did this. To me.

"I was going to tell you."

"When?"

The word was two syllables and it dismantled everything. Not a rhetorical weapon — an actual question, asked with the specificity Nora brought to editorial meetings, where vagueness was treated as a form of dishonesty. *When, specifically.* Simone opened her mouth and found nothing there, because there had never been a when. There had been *later* and *soon* and *before it goes too far*, and she'd used each one the way you hit a snooze button — not to wake up but to buy another nine minutes of not being awake.

"I don't know," Simone said. The honesty of it surprised her.

"Okay." Nora nodded. The nod was small, precise, and it closed a door. "So there wasn't a plan to stop."

"It got out of hand — "

"When did it get out of hand?" The same editorial calm. "When you built his personality from everything I've ever told you? When you matched him with me on Hinge? When you sat across from me at brunch and watched me read his messages and asked *what did he say* while your phone was showing you the exact same conversation from the backend?"

Each question had a specific address. Not scatter-shot — aimed. Nora had spent two weeks with the evidence and she'd done what she did professionally: organized her notes.

"The money." Simone heard her own voice drop, reaching for the register of genuine remorse, and she couldn't tell anymore whether the remorse was genuine or just the most appropriate response in this particular flow. "I know the money was wrong. That part — I know that crossed a line."

"Which line?" Nora's voice didn't change. "The four hundred for the dog surgery? For a dog that doesn't exist — a dog named Bruno, who you invented because you know I can't say no to a man with a dog? Or the twenty-five hundred for his mother's medical procedure, for a mother who doesn't exist? Or the ten thousand for the landscape architecture partnership?"

"I was going to pay it back."

Simone heard herself say it and felt the specific nausea of recognizing a stock line in real time. It was the thing people said in these situations — the desperate, unexamined cliché, the verbal equivalent of a fire-exit sign you've walked past a thousand times without ever locating the actual door. And Nora, who edited language for a living, who caught dead phrasing the way a jeweler caught a clouded stone, would hear it for exactly what it was: a line from the sympathetic version of this story, the version where Simone was a good person who'd made one forgivable mistake.

Something shifted behind Nora's eyes. Not anger. Recognition — the kind you saw when someone confirmed what they'd already suspected, a diagnosis they'd been hoping was wrong.

"I need you to stop, Si." Nora's voice was very even. "I need you to stop managing this. I can see you doing it — I can see you reaching for the right face and the right tone and I need you to stop, because I sat across from you for months while you ran that program on me and I can't — I won't sit through it again. Not tonight."

The word *program* went through Simone like current. Because it was the right word. She was running one — the warmth protocol, the good-friend interface, the version of herself that Nora had loved for seventeen years, the version that was generous and quick and knew when to laugh and when to hold the silence. And the thing about having your performance named was that you couldn't keep running it, but you also couldn't locate what was underneath, because the performance had been operating so long that the thing beneath it might have atrophied into nothing — or worse, it might be exactly what Simone was most afraid of: the person who'd opened a dashboard and typed notes about her best friend's emotional vulnerabilities and felt, while doing it, the focused satisfaction of well-executed work.

She stopped trying to arrange her face. The collapse wasn't dramatic — no falling, no screaming. She just stopped holding the shape. What came out was smaller, less composed, closer to the person who sat alone in this apartment at two in the morning adjusting sentiment parameters while the interest on her credit cards ticked upward in the background.

She reached behind her and turned off the stove. The burner clicked and went silent. It was a small gesture, but it was an acknowledgment: this was no longer dinner.

"You don't know what it's been like," Simone said. Her voice was different now — thinner, stripped of the social warmth that usually lived in its lower register. This was the voice she used alone, the voice that talked to the credit card balance and the bathroom mirror and the Navient statement.

"Then tell me," Nora said.

And the thing that came out of Simone was not confession. It was accusation. Because that was how it lived inside her — not as a thing she'd done to Nora but as a response to what the world had done to her, what Nora's existence had done to her, a wrongness so old and so structural that she'd lost the ability to see where the unfairness ended and her own choices began.

"Jason Park," she said. "Do you remember Jason Park? Sophomore year?"

Nora's face didn't change.

"He was texting me for months. He'd come to my room and sit on my bed and we'd talk until two in the morning and I thought — I thought it was going somewhere. And then you showed up at that party. One party. He never texted me again. Not because you tried to take him, not because you did anything wrong, but because that's how it works for you. You walk into a room and the room rearranges itself."

"That was fourteen years ago," Nora said.

"Your parents paid for school." The sentences were stacking now, the speed building, because slowing down would mean weighing what she was saying against what she'd done, and she already knew that calculus, had always known it, which was why the speed was necessary. "You graduated with nothing. No loans, no balance, no interest rate winding up in the background of every decision you've ever made. I'll be paying Navient until I'm forty-three. Every single month. And you — you spend four hundred dollars on a dinner. I saw it on your Venmo. You tip fifty on a thirty-dollar haircut. You have a bag you've been carrying for ten years that cost more than my rent."

"So you stole twelve thousand nine hundred dollars from me."

Simone flinched. The number. Not *a lot of money* or *thousands of dollars.* The number, precise, final — delivered the way Nora delivered a word count on an overlength manuscript. A quantity that didn't care about context.

"You got promoted." She couldn't stop. The momentum of seventeen years of silent accounting was too massive, a flywheel she'd been feeding with every small injustice, every swallowed comparison, every time she'd smiled and said *that's amazing, Nor* while the thing inside her recalculated the distance between their lives. "The same month I got laid off. You called me to celebrate, and I was sitting in this apartment with a severance check that wouldn't cover two months' rent, and you wanted me to be happy for you. And I was. I was happy for you. I'm always happy for you. Everyone's always happy for you. Your mother sends me birthday cards with checks in them like I'm a charity case, and I cash them, because I need the money, and every time I do I think about how you've never once had to take someone else's kindness and swallow the taste of it."

She stopped. Not because she'd run out — she had years of material, filed and cross-referenced in the ledger of her resentment, every entry dated, every balance carried forward — but because she'd reached the place where the grievances stopped being things that had happened to her and became the connective tissue to what she'd done, and looking at that tissue under Nora's gaze was like looking at a structure you'd thought was load-bearing and realizing it was rotten through.

"You've never had to work for anything the way I've had to work for everything," she said. She believed it. The tuition, the safety net, the ease of Nora's social passage through the world — she believed all of it. And it was partly true. And it was mostly a lie, because Nora worked hard, Nora was talented, Nora's career was not inherited but built. And it was completely beside the point, because no inventory of unfairness between two people makes it acceptable to build a counterfeit man out of your friend's vulnerabilities and use him to extract her money and her trust and a photograph of her body.

But she said it anyway, because she'd been carrying it so long that putting it down felt less like relief than like setting down a piece of her own skeleton.

***

Nora had listened to all of it.

She'd listened to Jason Park and the tuition and the promotion and the Venmo transactions and the birthday checks and the bag she'd carried for ten years. She'd listened without interrupting, without the responsive expressions that would tell the speaker what she thought — the way she listened when a writer pitched the version of their book they wished they'd written instead of the one actually on the page.

When Simone stopped — not finished but depleted, the fuel that sustains an accusation when the accused won't fight back finally spent — Nora sat for a moment in the silence that followed. The stove was off. The candle had burned down to a stub, the flame guttering in a shallow lake of wax. Outside the window the sky had gone fully dark while they talked, and the apartment's overhead light — Simone never used it, preferring the desk lamp and the candle, because overhead light in a studio revealed the room's true dimensions — wasn't on, so they sat in the soft directional glow from the desk lamp, two women in a room getting darker.

"How much of the money do you still have?" Nora said.

Whatever Simone had been expecting — a defense of her privilege, a counterattack about Jason Park, a fight she could meet with her stored ammunition — this wasn't it. The question was practical. It belonged to a person who had already moved past the wound of the betrayal and was looking at the material fact underneath.

"What?"

"The twelve nine. How much is left?"

"Almost nothing." Simone's voice was barely audible. "I was behind on rent. They'd started eviction proceedings. I used it for back rent and penalties, and the rest — minimums. Card minimums. The interest was — "

"Okay." Nora nodded. The nod was not forgiveness or acceptance. It was the nod she gave when a manuscript confirmed what she'd suspected — a structural problem so deep that no line edit could save it, a book that had to be let go. She'd already known the money was gone. She'd done the arithmetic during the two weeks of silence, sitting in her apartment with her bank statements and the cold, precise understanding that her friend had spent the money on survival and there was nothing to recover.

"I want to tell you what it felt like," Nora said.

Simone's hands found each other in her lap, fingers interlocking and pressing until the knuckles went white. Not the performed poise of attentive listening but the locked grip of someone who understands that the next thing said will be unsurvivable.

"Not the betrayal. I think you understand the betrayal — at least the version where you're the protagonist of a story about someone who made a mistake she regrets. That version's tidy. It has a shape you can live inside." She paused. "I want to tell you about the love."
